“THE BULLETIN.”
(Published at SYDNRY, N.S.W.)
The Unique Weekly—Australia’s Nonpareil
Illustrated Newspaper.
T" lx e Bulletl
Premier A-tietrahan Literary Journal ,
/s on/y paper which has a consistent and dis-
tinctive Australian National Policy.
The Bulletin favours—
A Republican Form of Government.
One .Person, one Vote.
Comolete Secularisation and Freedom of State
-Education.
Reform of the Criminal Code and Prison
System.
A United Australia and Protection against
the World.
Australia for the Australians.—The cheap
Chinaman, the cheap nigger, and the
cheap European pauper to be absolutely
excluded.
A State Bank, the issue of bank-notes to be a
State monopoly.
The direct election of Ministers by Parlia-
ment, instead of Party Government or
rather Government by Contradiction.
A new Parliamentary System one House to
be elected by constituencies as at present;
the other to be chosen by the whole
country voting as one Constituency.
A universal System of Compulsory Life
insurance.
The entire Abolition of the Private Ovvner-
ship of Land.
The Referendum.
Ihe Abolition of Titles of so-called “nobility. *
The public eye rejects as uninteresting more
than half of what appears in the newspapers of
the day. It is only the remainder that is found
in The Bulletin.
The Bulletin is an aggressive Democratic
paper which strives to exclude from its artisti-
cally-condensed columns all matter that is not
of general human interest. The Bulletin was
started 13 years ago with no capital but brains,
and has become the representative Australian
journal, because it possesses a vitality lacked
by the countless news paper- ventures in. which
Australian capitalists have unavailingly spent
large fortunes.
The Bulletin’s conductors claim that every
progressive paper on the Australian continent has
more or less endeavoured to profit by its example
and follow its lines. The result of its powerful
influence and its unprecedented literary and com-
mercial success can at once be seen by comparing
the methods pursued by the Australian daily and
weekly press prior to and immediately after th
first issue of The Bulletin.
From the date of The Bulletin’s inaugural
publication its success was assured. It had at
once a dozen more or less servile imitators, many
of which even took, so far as was possible, its
name. They are now all dead, while The Bul-
letin continues the one phenomenal success in
Australian journalism, the one completely non-
local and distinctly universal Australasian news-
paper.
The Bulletin proprietary have spent some
thousands of pounds in fitting their buildings
with the latest appliances for high-class printing
and the production of illustrations, and they have
now issued The Bulletin in an entirely new
form—printed with new type of improved legi-
bility on the finest paper, and adorned with
sketches from the hands of the ablest and best-
paid newspaper artists Australia has ever seen.
The Bulletin is the only well-established Aus-
tralian journal which, throughout its career, has
been consistently Radical. It points with pride
to the fact that for years it stood alone among
secular newspapers in advocating the Irish
National Cause, and that it was the only Aus-
tralian journal which denounced the Soudan
Expedition from its inception.
The Bulletin is the beacon-fire of national
progress; it is the forceful summons to the
crusade against the monopolies in land, wealth,
power, and privilege—the direct cause of half the
misery which now afflicts British humanity.
Against the clahns of avaricious clergy who claim
to monopolise salvation, against thedark despotism
of grasping plutocrats, and against the exercise of
any human authority which has not been granted
by the people over whom it is exercised, The
Bulletin will fearlessly and ruthlessly contend.
The Bulletin, unlike the ordinary weekly
journal, is not loaded with a weight of matter
transferred from the columns of a daily paper
issued from the same office. The Bulletin is
not connected with any other p«|>er, and the
matter it contains is all specially prepared by its
own unequalled staff.
The Bulletin is the journal which, of all Aus-
tralian journals, is most frequently quoted in Eng-
land, America, and the colonies.
The Bulletin, which is the only paper sold
by runners in the streets of every Australasian
capital, is the most widely-circulated of all papers
published south of the equator, a.id is the only
Australasian publication which enjoys a popu-
larity unhampered by the political boundaries
that so closely restrict other journals to the
colonies in which they are printed.
The Bulletin’s red cover is equally familiar
to the bush in an of the Far North,, the stockman
of Central Australia, the pearl-sheller of Torres
Straits, and the digger in the Maoriland
Ranges. A paper which is at once the moat
popular city publication and the organ of • the
intelligent bushman must indeed be broadly based.
Perhaps the strongest proof of The Bulletin’s
popularity is found in the unchallengeable fact—
easily ascertainable from any postal otficia'— that,
of all journals issued in Australasia, The Bul-
letin is the one most frequently remailed from
one reader to another.
The Bulletin is going ahead faster than any
other paper, because its vast mass of original and
specially-written reading matter is the brightest
and best, its illustrations the cleverest and most
artistic, and its policy the boldest and most
straightforward ever submitted to the Australian
public.

Business Announcements.
The Caligraph Writing Machine\
mu
THE CALIGRAPH embodies all the highest
developments of applied mechanics, and its
principle having stood the test of 15 years, its
success towers above the fantastic devices of mush-
room competitors.
“ I like Caligraph writing better than that of
any other typewriter because the type comes out
so sharply and clearly.” The prominent Anglican
clergyman, who used these words, had hit upon
the chief characteristic of the Caligraph without
exactly knowing what brought it about. The
result is attributable to the fact that the platen_ of
the Caligraph being bevelled the type strike
against a flat surface thus producing a full and
even impression. When you see typewriting with
only the middle of the letters clear you know that
the machine used was not a Caligraph, but one
fitted with the ordinary round platen. When you
read a typewritten letter, with some of the type
leaning to the right and the rest to the left, you
may be sure that your correspondent has a crude
machine, with curved typebars, which naturally
become more crooked, and thus twist their type
out of perpendicular. Then remember that the
Caligraph has straight typebars which, like every-
thing else in the machine, are fitted with adjust-
able bearings.
The Caligraph manifolds 15 carbon copies at
one operation, and used with the Edison Mimeo-
graph reduplicates 1500 copies from one stencil.

Australian Bards and Bush. Reviewers.
[To “Wombat” and Co.]
While you use your best endeavour to immortalise
in verse.
The gambling and the drink which are your coun-
try’s greatest curse,
While you glorify the bully and take the spieler’s
part—
You’re a clever southern writer, scarce inferior to
Bret Harte.
If you sing of waving grasses when the plains are
dry as bricks,
And discover shining rivers where there’s only
mud and sticks;
If you picture “ mighty forests ” where the
“ mulga ” spoils the view—
You’re superior to Kendall, and ahead of Gordon,
too.
If you swear that there’s no country like the land
that gave you birth,
And its sons are just the noblest and most glorious
chaps on earth ;
If in every girl a Venus your poetic eye discerns,
You are gracefully referred to as the “young
Australian Burns.”
But if you should find that bushmen —spite of all
the poets say—
Are just common brother-sinners, and you’re quite
as good as they, _
You’re a drunkard, and a liar, and a “cynic,”
and a sneak,
Your grammar’s simply awful and your intellect is
weak.
Wellington, Maoriland.
HENRY LAWSON.
The Melb. licensed victuallers complain
bitterly about the cheapness of “colonial wine”
licenses, and the absurd facility with which they
can be obtained. They say that the long-beer
drinker has become a “ colonial ” wine soaker (at
3d. a pint). Why not? The wine soaker gets
better accommodation at the wine-shop lior him-
self, and can, at a pinch, get a private room and
two pints of wine, for self and “missus,” all for
sixpence! One does not “ expect much for four-
pence,” but what can a man want more for six-
pence ?
The successful removal of Piles of th e
most stubborn description is being effected by
Sharp’s new “Cannalin” treatment, larjfe quan-
tities of which are being forwarded to isufferers
throughout the colonies. It is obtainable, with
advice, from 257 George-street, Sydney.
Dear Bulletin, — Be the many “ Land
Distributions” and “ Public Lotteries ” now being
advertised all over Australasia. Although great
harm may result from private gambling, and
greater still from public gaming-tables and race-
courses, the evil, after all, isn’t a millionth part
so great as the evil resulting from these “public
lotteries.’’ The persons who are ruined by ordinary
gambling are, after all, persons who would cer-
tainly be ruined by some vice or another. The
compound of avarice and excitement which makes
the attraction of hazard doesn’t allure the higher
kinds of character ; besides the vice does not
go to the player—the player goes to the vice.
Now, on the contrary, the lottery attacks openly,
and tries to allure in very despite of themselves
the much wider multitude that is the very sap
and support of a nation—it 'entices the people
themselves. It lures the workman to throw away
his wage—the student to spend his time in fanci-
ful dreams —the simple day-labourer to consume
his content in senseless calculation. The lottery
assails them in the street, is carried to them in
their homes, and perhaps drops them some poor
prize at first. It changes honesty to cunning,
peace to burning desire, industry to a perpetual
waiting on chance, and manly effort to an imbecile
abandonment to the dictates of signs and portents,
and the expectancy of a fortune which never
comes. Highborn gamblers are only the topmost
leaves of the tree of State, they may rot away
without detriment to the tree, but the lottery lays
the axe at the very trunk and root of it, because
it demoralises the people. —Yours, D. B. ROBIN-
SON, Cue, W.A., July Bth, ’94.

NOTICE.
By sending direct to The Bulletin Office, Twenty
Shillings sterling—with postage added ichere required,
which you may pay by Post Office Order, One Pound
Note, or stamps of any Australian province—you can
have Tub Bulletin posted regularly to you every week for
a year.
This offer is not open to residents of Sydney. In that
city The Bulletin is not delivered by mail, but is entirely
in the hands of our carriers, with whom we do not wish
to interfere.
Cheques, Drafts, etc., and all Business Communications
to be addressed to William Macleod, Managing Director
Literary Communications to be addressed J. F.
Archibald.
The Editor will carefully read and acknowledge in the
‘Correspondence” column all contributions submitted—
whether in the form of Political, Social, or other Articles,
Verse, Short Tales or Sketches (those dealing with Aus-
tralian subjects, and not exceeding two columns in
length, or say 3000 words, are specially acceptable).
Paragraphs, Letters, or Newspaper-Clippings. All
communications will be regarded as strictly confidential.
The Bulletin will return all unavailable MSS
when the address is specified and stamps are enclosed.
But we desire those sending MSS. to distinctly under-
stand that we are not responsible for the preservation or
transmission oj such MSS.
LONDON.
Copies of The Bulletin can be obtained in London,
after the arrival of each mail, at the office of the British
Australasian Co., Limited, SI Fleet Street, E.C.
Also in the Transvaal : — The Standard and Diggers
News Co., Limited, Johannesburg, South Africa.
Also: Jl. A. Thompson and Co., 3 Church Street,
Capetown, South Africa.
Notice to Subscribers In Victoria, Queens-
land and Soutb Australia.
In consequence of the imposition of One Halfpenny
postage by the Australian and Tasmanian Governments
on Inter provincial Newspapers, the annual subscription
outside N. S. Wales in future will be £l, with 2s. 2d.
added for postage.
No papers will be sent unless the subscription be pre-
paid. There will be no exception to this rule.
Notice to Subscribers.
Coloured wrapper denotes expiration of subscription.
Bulletin Newspaper Co., Ltd,
Notice to Agents.
MAORILAND, TASMANIA and WEST
AUSTRALIA.
Owing to Governments of the above-named
provinces having imposed Half-penny postage on
all papers from Ist July, 1894, agents are in-
formed from that date the wholesale rate for
The Bulletin will he altered from Four Shillings
to Four Shillings and Sixpence per dozen.
SATURDAY, AUGUST 18, 1894.
Premier Reid’s Policy.
The New South Wales Ministry, through
the mouth of Premier Reid, has announced
its policy, and The Bulletin bespeaks for it
a fair trial. Judging by the manner in
which the members ranged themselves at the
opening of Parliament, the new Government
has a good working majority. Even leaving
out Parkes —who probably sits behind the
Ministry chiefly because a kick from behind
is more unexpected and disastrous than one
from the front, and therefore the position of
a supporter is the one in which he can do the
most damage—and his following of two or
three irreconcileables, the Reid Ministry
should have a sufficient majority in the
Assembly for all practical purposes. Also,
the failure of ex-Preraier Dibbs’s unscru-
pulous—though no doubt strictly constitu-
tional—attempt to send seven of his own
partisans to the Council leaves some ten or
twelve nominations at the disposal of the
new Premier, and by filling the vacancies
with supporters of his Land-tax policy
he can probably come neai’er to secur-
ing a majority in the Tory Chamber
than any reformer who has preceded him.
Mr. Reid’s colleagues are, with hardly an
exception, feeble, methodical, well-meaning
men who will leave him to supply the
intellect of the Cabinet, and—unless he is
utterly devoid of all capacity for leadership
will give little trouble. And unless the
stormy and aggressive Dibbs, with his com-
pact following, goes in for a course of wanton
obstruction, the new rulers of New South
Wales should have a fair opportunity
to do all the good that is in them.
As the Opposition, however, like the
Opposition in every other country which
groans under the incubus of Party Govern-
ment, always does go in for a course of
wanton obstruction, the Ministry’s chance of
developing its policy in peace is strictly
limited. The first, last, and only purpose of
an Opposition is to get back to office at the
earliest possible moment, and the way to get
back to office is to discredit the existing
Government, and the way to discredit the
Government is to prevent it, by every
possible means, from doing anything, and
then to draw the country’s attention to
the fact that it hasn’t done anything.
Therefore, every new Premier enters on
his duties with a light heart and generally
with a large and comprehensive policy ; and
retires a while later with a damaged reputa-
tion, and a soured temper, and a political
record which consists of a few tattered little
statutes and a long list of failures.
• • •
Mr. Reid, being an entirely new Premier
and with hardly any experience of Ministerial
office, starts with an extensive programme
and a sanguine temperament. Whether he
has the constructive ability required to put
his theories into any political shape remains
to be seen. Parkes is absolutely devoid of
this power ; nine-tenths of the great schemes
which he presented so persistently for 25
years and more never came to anything,
merely because he never could write them
out in intelligible English, nor understand
them when they were written out for him.
Jennings had as little constructive capacity
as Parkes —if such a thing were possible.
Patterson and Carter, of Victoria, are
an absolute mental blank in the same
respect. Dibbs has the constructive
capacity to a limited degree. Reid —if his
programme is to be a reality and not merely a
programme —needs to have the brilliant crea-
tive faculty of John Parlance; and whether
he has any creative faculty or not is entirely
unknown. So far he has created nothing ;
but, on the other hand, he is not known to
have made an unsuccessful attempt to create
anything. His programme, at all events, is
much more democratic than his past career
would have led the public to expect. It
includes reform of the land laws—those land
laws which have been reformed more or less
by almost every Ministry for thirty years
past, and which still seem to need reform as
urgently as ever ; local government, a land
tax of a Id. in the £l, retrenchment—that
same old item without which no Ministerial
policy is complete—an eight-hour law, a
Factory Act, Federation, a State Bank, a
revenue tariff, and an income-tax. Premier
Reid is in favour of all these projects, and,
looking out through a sanguine eye-glass on
a ragged Ministry, and a solid Opposition,
and a troubled community, he seriously
expects to carry them all into effect.
♦ ♦ * *
As for the great Freetrade principle to
which, more than any other, Premier Reid
owes his accession to office, it is dead and
forgotten and utterly defunct. There never
has been any Freetrade in New South Wales,
and there is not going to be any. Premier
Reid, looking out on futurity through his
glazed optic, does not even pretend to see
the beginnings of it on the farthest horizon ;
more than that, he does not seriously profess
to desire to see it. His party has climbed
into office by false pretences on this occasion
as on every other, the false pretence con-
sisting in the assumption of the name of
Freetrade, and the impassioned advocacy of
Freetrade principles, while the reality which
lies behind these principles is entirely
neglected. The new Government announces
its intention of raising £1,500,000 per
annum by Customs duties on about half-a-
dozen articles, against the £2,100,000 raised
by its predecessors, therefore, so far as the
total is concerned, there is little difference
between them. To do this it must levy its
duties solely on articles of universal
consumption, so that the tax will fall
almost as heavily on the artizan as on
the millionaire. All the special duties
levied by the Dibbs Government on
wealth—the taxes upon imported carpets,
precious stones, porcelain, ivory, jewellery,
silks, laces, paintings, perfumery, pianos,
organs, harmoniums, gold and silver plate,
plush, and many other luxuries—are to be
abolished ; the Treasurer who undertakes to
get £1,500,000 per annum by taxing six
articles must choose articles of the com-
monest consumption to realise anything like
his expectations. Also, he must choose
articles which are not easily produced in the
province, else his duties will help local pro-
duction and thereby diminish imports and
wreck the Customs revenue, besides being
of a protective character, and he must tax
them more heavily than they have ever been
taxed before. Consequently, the blessed
features of the promised Reid tariff are that
it will not be Freetrade, that it will afford
no employment, help no Australian industry,
do nothing towards making the country in-
dependent of the outside world, and merely
increase prices and be an instrument of
oppression.
* * *
The new Premier refuses to commit him-
self to any scheme for the reformation of the
Legislative Council, and that unreformed
body will wreck his Land-tax Bill almost to
a certainty. It will also, in all probability,
wreck his Income-tax scheme as well. On
two separate occasions Dibbs attempted to
raise revenue by a tax on incomes, and in
each case the measure was thrown out by a
big majority. The cheerful obtuseness with
which the new Premier leaves this tremon-
dous snag out of his calculations says little
for his sagacity. The Council, if it is any-
thing like what it was in the days of the two
Dibbs Administrations, will gladly assist in
abolishing all the late Government’s indirect
taxes on wealth, and will then refuse to
replace them by any direct imposts, and leave
the new Premier in the lutch. It was one
of the constant assertions of the Reid party
that the two Dibbs Governments were both
profoundly gratified when their Income-tax
proposals were rejected, as they thereby
gained the reputation of having a desire to
tax wealth, and yet escaped the disagreeable
reality. Whether Premier Reid is any
more sincere in the matter remains to be
seen, there being nothing in his past career
which can serve as evidence either way.
* # *
As regards land reform, it is undoubtedly
much wanted, but as a long succession of
Governments tinkered miserably with
the land question for many years, and
only succeeded in making it somewhat
worse than it was before, it is too soon
for poeans upon this subject. The Local
Government Bill has been promised by the
same party almost every year since the early
sixties, and the fact that it is promised again
is a matter of course. With the exception
of one or two items which have been inserted
to bring it up to date, the whole Reid pro-
gramme—including the retrenchment—is
much the same programme which every
Ministry has presented as far back as the pre-
sent generation can remember. Even when
he points out that the difference between his
Ministry and its futile predecessors is that
he will carry out what they only promised, he
stands on exactly the same footing as the rest
—they all had the same difference. Every-
thing depends on whether Premier Reid is
sufficiently sincere and sufficiently capable
to enforce his policy ; if he is, then he will
be, in many respects, the best statesman
who has yet ruled New South Wales. The
country knows already that he is not a
sincere Freetrader, but as to the rest it is
ignorant. It doesn’t know what he can do,
for he has never done anything. He has no
bad record to live down, for he never estab-
lished any record. He had, until last week,
scarcely even any hasty or ill-judged sayings
to explain away, for no one took him seri-
ously enough to preserve a record of his
sayings. His whole history is that he is an
ex-civil servant, a rather weak barrister,
and a politician who never contributed one
new or valuable idea on any subject, and
that he was once, for a short time, a
very mediocre Minister in a very medio-
cre Ministry. This latter fact may mean,
as it did in the case of John Ballance, of
Maoriland, that he has the genius of a
leader and for that very reason makes an
ineffective subordinate ; or it may mean, as
in the case of tea-man Inglis, that he has no
genius at all, and was an ineffective sub-
ordinate because an all-seeing Providence
built him that way. However that may
be, The Bulletin trusts that he will be
met with no wanton opposition and no use-
less obstruction in any shape. Even on
purely selfish and party principles, obstruction
is a mistake. Many a hopelessly imbecile
politician has been blocked by an ill-guided
Opposition just when he was on the point of
giving the most complete evidence of his
imbecility, and has never again got a chance
to fully demonstrate it. The opportunity of
being a hopeless fool arrives to many men only
once in a lifetime, and many Parliamentary
obstructionists would do well to make a note
of the fact and not interrupt at the critical
moment.
Boom, and Borrow, and—Bust?
The writer once struck a Queensland town-
ship which gave him the most valuable
object lesson in finance he ever had in his
life. It was a town of insolvents, run by a
bank. Most of these insolvents were
non-producers—shopkeepers, , brokers, auc-
tioneers, agents, importers, and so on, but
they all lived in fine style, with fine houses,
horses and carriages, and consumed the very
finest brands of liquor. When the writer
came to compare local revenue with expendi-
ture he was amazed. “What do you live
on ?” he asked one. “ Oh, we live on the
Bank.” And the mayor of the township
added, by way of explanation ; “ You’re all
right as long as you get well into them.
I’m so far in now that they can’t shut
me up ; they have to keep me going,
or they’d lose the lot.” And the
way of operating was really very simple.
The town was a “boom” town, trading on
its “ magnificent future,” while three-fourths
of the population never did a hand’s turn in
the present. All they did was to buy blocks
of land at “ boom ” prices from Government
or from one another, and get an overdraft
from the bank on the security of the land to
pay for the land. Then it was only a matter
of “ getting far enough into the bank ” —an
easy matter. Those were the days of
Griffith’s Ten Million Loan—the most
tremendous curse that ever befell Queens-
land, and a curse which has now come home
to roost. The Government was borrowing
money which it did not know what to do
with, and pouring it into the banks, which lent
it out to anybody and everybody on good
security, bad security, and no security what-
ever.
m # «
So, in the particular township under
notice things went merrily. Everybody
bought land, got an overdraft, and put up a
house and a coach-house, lived expensively,
and continued to get overdraft and have a
jolly good time. The bank was quite happy,
relying on the “magnificent future,” while
the half-yearly returns showed an enormous
interest profit—on paper. It was simply
grand. The “ leading residents ” were like
one large family—a band of brothers, with
the beneficent bank as the protecting mother
of them all. You never needed money. If
Jones felt thirsty, he went into Brown’s bar
and had a whisky-and-seltzer, which was
“put down” in two senses. By-and-by
Brown would send Jones an order for
groceries—“ put down.” Smith sold Robin-
son a horse, and bought his ironmongery
—all “put down.” Once or twice a
year there was a rendering of accounts
and squaring of balances. The debtor
on balance gave the creditor a cheque on the
bank, and “justice was satisfied.” Occasion-
ally a note would come from head office say-
ing that, loss or no loss, Blank must not be
financed any further. Then Blank would
call a meeting of his creditors, who knew
that his plight might any day be their own ;
and speaker after speaker would rise with
tears in his voice to point out what a fine
fellow Blank was, and -what a splendid
citizen, and how much the town was indebted
to him for his energy in booming the
“magnificent future.” Then it v/ould be
unanimously decided that Blank’s affairs
should be wound up “ by liquidation, and not
in insolvency ” —that is to say, there would
not be an honest, out-and-out bankruptcy,
with Blank's bad debts written off for good
and all ; but Blank would undertake to pay
10s. or 15s. in the £ by bills up to 10 years",
which left his debt still figuring as an asset
in the bank ledger. Then Blank would be
unanimously voted his house and furniture,
and the incident would end in an adjourn-
ment to Brown’s bar, and whiskies all
round—“ put down” to Blank. The bank,
sinking under the weight of remorse and
the general indignation of the leading
citizens, and hoping that if Blank gob a
fresh chance he might try and meet his bills,
would start a fresh overdraft for him. and
things would proceed de novo .
• # #
By-and-by advances and interest ate up
all the security, even at boom-values—and
still the “magnificent future” had nut
arrived. The leading residents thought it
very remiss. They talked of nothing else
when they congregated in Brown’s bar—
added up their marvellous soil and their
valuable minerals, and their fine rainfall, anl
their splendid climate, and wondered where
on earth the “ future ” could be, and why on
earth it did not turn up. Every stranger
who came to the town was met with the
query—in essence, if not in so many words :
“Have you seen our future?” No ; the
stranger would assure them—he had not
seen their future ; was it a good future ?
“Magnificent!” they replied; and often
the stranger was so impressed that
he would stay and become a leading
resident himself, and assist the others
to sit down and look for it. One
stranger sold out a fine business and took
up £ll,OOO cash to invest in the future.
Where is that £ll,OOO now? “ Yhere ish
der lufly colden gloud dat float on der moun-
tain’s prow?” Interest has made but a
mouthful of it ; and the stranger is back
again at business, working hard for a living
in the present.
* * *
Now, from that township, one may learn
the condition of all Queensland, for the same
process has been followed, in greater or less
degree, in every town in the province. In
1801, came the beginning of the end.
Government credit stopped. Treasurer
MTlwraith tried to float a loan of 2|
millions in London and was offered £311,500.
After that, the deluge. When the bank
directors sat up to consider the situation,
they found they held immense quantities of
land, values steadily falling. They had used
not only Government deposits to extend their
monopoly. As the Maryborough News
says :■ —
The influx of the ten-million loan created a false
boom, which brought more grist in the shape of
deposits of English money. These deposits were used
to consolidate a banking monopoly, and tighten the grip
on the land and the great wealth-producing industries <1
the province. The great desire of the bank seemed to be
to get all the resources of the province into its own h ends.
It succeeded, alas, too well. It has gorged itself until it
is beyond its power to extricate itself from the tight place
in which it is so firmly wedged. Its creditors in England
are exacting. The produce of the whole province is no
more than sufficient to meet these demands ; and, mean-
while, the residents everywhere, as in our own district,
are being impoverished and oppressed beyond endurance.
But the bank does not dare to drive its
debtors to desperation. It is adopting every
expedient to raise values, and in the mean-
time it must keep clients going and pay
interest to its own creditors. Some ready
cash is indispensable. This is why the credit
of the people of Queensland is to be pledged
in a new public loan.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
Things that are not Explained.
The Bulletin rises to draw the attention of
the New South Wales authorities to the fact
that the tote-man and the unlawful layer of
doubles are again flourishing in Sydney without
even a semblance of disguise. Some time ago
the Sydney police department, after a long
period of quiescence, took proceedings against
certain especially notorious law-breakers of this
order—why the proceedings were not taken
earlier is not explained. One gentleman—the
one whose case was first called—was sentenced
to a short term of imprisonment, and thereupon'
the alarmed horde of bookmakers arose and fled


In confusion from the Court, leaving instruc-
-3 w ith their solicitors to get the cases
Jimirned They were adjourned accordingly,
thJuU why the y adjournment was granted so
nnammously, and for no visible cause, is
her thing that hasn’t been explained.
When the cases came on again for hearing, it
discovered that the prosecutor had some-
how away in the interim ; no one
beared and no one made any special com-
ment on’his absence, or seemed in the least
anrorised, or appeared to take any further
interest in the matter. Why the prosecutor
had vanished, and where he vanished to, were
T so left wholly unexplained. The bookmaker
who was sentenced to a term in gaol promptly
£fot out on bail pending an appeal, and Ihe
bulletin has heard nothing ot him or of his
. since. While this trouble was on the whole
tribe of tote-keepers and layers of doubles put
the shutters almost unanimously, and went
to lie low in parts unknown, leaving a notice
on the front-door that the business of Moss
Uoobstein was temporarily suspended, owing
to domestic calamity. But within the last few
davs many of these notices have been taken
down and the shops have been re-opened, and
announcements have appeared in the papers
that Moss Jacobstein is once more carrying on
tablets at the old .land, and will lay the
double on the most liberal terms. And why
the police take no notice of his doings, and
allow him to thus openly insult the alleged
majesty of the law, is still another thing about
which no one has offered any explanation what-
ever.
The Borrowing Disease.
The old borrowing disease has broken out once
more in Maorilaud, the only province which
has ever had the courage to swear off—-however
ineffectually—the curse of British capital. For
a number of years Maoriland has struggled
manfully to get back to solvency after the
frantic finance of the Vogel and Grev Minis-
tries ; and, having got back, the Seddon
Government now proposes to retrograde with
all possible velocity to the old, ruinous policy,
and have another frantic “ tear,” according to
the approved Vogel fashion. This lamentable
idea confirms the impression, which has been
crowing for some time past, that John Bal-
ance "was the only genius, the only sincere
democrat, the only patriot, and the only states-
man of the late Ministry. After his death his
colleagues for a time maintained his policy from
sheer force of habit; but as his memory recedes
into the distance they must needs initiate a
policy of their own, and then the fact obtrudes
itself that Ballance’s colleagues, without Bal-
lance, are very common clay at the best. The
old longing for a boom and a violent outbreak
of “ prosperity ” lias come upon them. There-
fore, that beautiful and nicely hair-oiled person,
Treasurer Ward, has formulated a boom budget
which promises favourably to add ,£5,000,000
to the public debt in one fatal act. He pro-
poses to borrow £250,000 to buy back private
lauds for purposes of settlement; £250,000 for
road-making purposes; £1,500,000 to supply
capital for the new State Land Bank; and
£250,000 for the purpose of acquiring native
lands. Another £1,000,000 is to be raised by
the local issue, in small amounts, of 4 per cent,
consols. Insurance companies, especially
foreign companies, are to be required to make
deposits with the Treasury for the better pro-
tection of the public against bogus institutions,
and this is expected to provide the Treasurer
with a perpetual loan of £450.000 at 4 per cent.
And, in addition, £1,000,000 of the Bank of
New Zealand’s State-guaranteed capital is to be
safely invested at the discretion of the fatally-
beautiful Ward, which probably means that
it will also be put into Maorilaud consols,
and form another addition to the public
debt. Further, and over and above all this
garish finance, Treasurer Ward has suc-
cessfully “converted” certain old loans
bearing high interest into per cent,
stock, whereby the gross amount of the public
debt is materially increased while the annual
charge for interest is considerably diminished,
and, having done this, he has laid violent hands
on the sinking fund of £260,000 provided for
the redemption of these old loans, and added it
to the Public Works fund. Nothing is said
about setting aside the interest saved by the
conversion operations to form a new sinking
fund. The ghastly total of all this is
£5,000,000 more debt. The insurance scheme
is undoubtedly a much-needed reform. The
local issue of 4 per cent, consols, in sums of £5
and upwards, with the interest payable at any
post-office throughout the country, is another
excellent device, inasmuch as debentures sold on
such terms will mostly remain in the country,
instead of drifting into the hands of the foreign
investor ; and if the money so_ obtained was
employed to pay off old liabilities, instead of to
create new ones, it would not matter if ten
millions were issued instead of one. The land
policy of the Seddon Ministry is equally com-
mendable in itself ; and the State Land Bank
would be a magnificent device if the Government
had the courage to start, it with millions
of State notes, instead of I 2 millions of
J. Bull’s capital. But every one of these
projects, from the Land Bank down to the
sinking fund swindle, means more borrowing,
either openly or under a thin disguise, and
though the ends in view are excellent the means
are foolish and utterly disastrous. When the
only province which ever attempted to teach
Australasia a lesson in self-reliance falls away
so miserably, it would seem that the borrowing
disease is really hopeless and utterly incurable.
Toujours Parkes.
A use has been found for the stranded hulks on
the beach of politics. It is discovered that
there is an excellent market for banalities or
trivialities attached to the names of ex-states-
meu or other notorieties, who good-naturedly
advertise themselves while putting dollars in
the pockets of their enterprising exploiters.
The Bulletin has nothing whatever to urge
against the traffic ; but thinks it, on the con-
trary, a highly desirable thing that imperial
Cassar, politically dead and turned to clay,
should assist to patcli the breeches of contem.
porary journalism. Yet is it too much to ask
the responsible persons to respect the notice in
Australian grass, and “ Keep off the Parkes !”
Lately we have had Parkes ad nauseam;
Parses for breakfast, Parses for dinner, and
Parses for tea ; Parses as a philanthropist,
Parses as a federationist, Parses as a
grand old man, and Parses among the
ladies. He is like a recurring decimal;
and there is really nothing to be gained by
carrying the calculation of him further. We
thought we had lost him for a time ; but last
week there was an unexpected recrudescence of
Parses as a humourist advising young men to
“ put their money in safe banks,” and that
tired feeling came over us again. Indeed,
Parses is almost as bad as Sydney Telegraph
engravings. Once a politician neglects proper
health precautions, and gets Sydney-TELE-
GRAPiI-engravings, he is doomed; for it will
follow him till he dies, and after that. It
fastens on him as a candidate for Parliament, is
cast up at him as a member, and strikes him
hard when he goes up for re-election as a
Minister, and again when he is elected.
After that it lies in wait till he
makes a speech, or is married, or is a
candidate for the next Parliament, and
then it attacks him again. It is as relent-
less as the wrath of God —or as Parkes. Now,
we know the high qualities of Parkes as poet
and moralist and man ; we are quite prepared
to gaze at him, admire him, walk round him
and fool him a little—though not, emphatically
not, to the top of his bent; we are willing to
take an interest in his “declining years” on
special occasions—birthdays, say ; but we can-
not conscientiously say, “Give us this day our
daily Parkes ! ” It should be remembered that
Parkes is strong medicine—in large quantities
he enervates and cloys. Time there was a
change of venue. The Parkes want shuffling.
Permit us to pass Parkes.
Carter’s Tariff.
The new Victorian tariff—that weird and pic-
turesque curiosity which represented the com-
bined intelligence of Carter and Patterson,
and which the Sydney press hailed as evidence
of a glorious Freetrade resurrection —led a
tumultuous existence for one week, and was
then iguominiously suspended. It was probably
the most wildly-exciting week in all Vic-
toria’s history. The Protectionist organisations
in the country held meetings and denounced
the new tariff. The Freetrade organisations
did likewise, and cursed it with a whole-
souled unanimity never before witnessed. The
importers, who were supposed to be especially
benefited, protested violently almost to a
man. The Opposition leader gave notice of
a want-of-confidence motion; and many of
the Ministerialists made no secret of their
disgust and astonishment. At least two
members of the Cabinet let it be known, in a
vague, non-committal fashion, that they were
in no way responsible for Carter’s strange and
unlooked-for inspiration. Every paper in the
country blasted the new proposals more or less,
and for once the Age and the Freetrade Argus
were unanimous. The manufacturers protested
as one infuriated citizen ; so did the Chamber
of Commerce. The miners insurrected against
the 10 per cent, revenue duty on quicksilver,
wire rope, and many other articles used in their
trade. The Melbourne tobacco factories, to
which the new tariff meant absolute ruin, all
closed up and threw over 500 employes out on a
cold world. The local cigar makers, whose
goods were subjected to a duty of 2s. 6d. per
lb. (instead of 6d.) in order to boom the trade
in cheap German “smokes” made, in many
cases, by prison labour, and to help on the
Chinese manufacturers of Chicago, gave notice
of their intention to close. A meat preserving
factory, employing 200 hands, did likewise,
and so did a big clothing factory of almost
equal dimensions. Most of the other clothing,
woollen and meat factories followed suit im-
mediately afterwards. The Trades Hall, the
Warehousemen’s Association and the Vine-
growers’ Board all rose in execration. The
whole month’s import of English and American
magazines was blocked at the Customs House,
it being found that a duty had been put on
them by accident. It was further discovered
that a duty had been placed on imported
coin by accident. And then, just as the
new Freetrade development was at its most
interesting stage, the Government backed
down and the new tariff was suddenly sus-
pended, after providing a week of the wildest
comedy and tragedy ever seen in Victoria.
Any reputation which formerly remained to
Carter and James Beef Patterson has gone
in this awful experiment ; they stand revealed
as two irresponsible infants fooling around with
a candle in a powder-magazine, or two un-
lettered aboriginals trying to run a bank with
a boomerang. And the Legislature would
gladly get rid of them both without delay,
only, under the beautiful system of Government
by Contradiction, if it does so it thereby puts
Shiels into office, and it begins to vaguely
suspect that Shiels is almost as ghastly a
failure as Carter and Patterson. No doubt
he would be an improvement on that awful
pair, but he isn’t sufficient of an im-
provement to be worth the trouble of an
exchange. So the above-mentioned pair retire
to patch their gruesome budget, and will be
heard from again shortly. Whether they will
be heard fiom again as tariff-reformers, or
whether they will paint themselves bright green
and do a scalp-dance on the front bench, isn’t
known.
More Brummagem!
The reign of Brummagem continues. One of
the last official acts of the Hires Government
was to appoint to the vacant editorship of the
“Official History of New South Wales” the
dapper ex-secretary and relative of Governor
Duff, Mr. Arthur Galton. This more or less
young gentleman has now honoured Australia
with his presence for something over a year,
and, no doubt, with the assurance of the H. H.
Chamfion-Christie Murray type of British
tourist, has obtained that firm and rapid grip
of our history and traditions which marks him
above all the native-born litterateurs as the
most fitted for carrying out the task of selecting
the documents to be included in our official
history. That this appointment should be the
work of ex-Republican Dibbs is strangely in-
consistent with that gentleman’s attitude
towards Governor Duff’s alien interference
with constitutional precedent. Ihe Im-
perial wayfarer who happens to tem-
porarily occupy New South Wales Government
House has no more effect upon our history than
the regular arrival and departure of the mail
steamers. He merely conies, eats so many
meals, draws so much salary, gives so many
balls and departs. He is discounted always by
the inevitable sense of the sentiment —“ Thou
art passing hence, my brother.” So that
Dibbs’ quarrel may have little effect on history.
But Dibbs gives the power of meddling with
our historical documents into the hands of the
literary wayfarer from Britain, and with it the
power of selection of documents for publication
in the official history of New South Wales.
The power of selection includes the power of
suppression as well as of publication. There
are many passages in our history which are dis-
creditable to the authorities of Downing-street,
London. Yet a protege of those authorities, a
man enveloped with their traditions, is entrusted
with the selection and handling of all our histori-
cal documents. This work, unlike the ephemeral
acts of individual Governors, is permanent.
Now, as to the qualifications requisite for this
task of editing these documents. The public
are led to believe that Mr. Gabion has special
attributes which mark him as a fit and proper
person. It is rumoured that he once wrote a
life of Cromwell. So did Carlyle. There
were tw T o Cromwells, and as Carlyle had
used up one, Galton took the other to show
that there were not two Carlyles. He also
lectured on the Ancient Romans, with extracts
from Gibbon, and was reported verbatim in the
S.M. Herald, extracts and all. These are his
literarv records. He is reported to share with
Dibbs a coolness towards his viceregal relative,
a coldness akin to that felt by that other dis-
tinguished scholar, Hamlet, the Dane, for his
uncle, Claudius. In general, he has observed
Australia from the deck of an ocean
steamer, the windows of a first-class rail-
way carriage, and the stately towers of
Government House. As this observation has
been going on for about a year, he is, therefore,
highly qualified, according to Dibbs, to compile
our official history. Added to these towering
qualifications, he has the additional merit of
being cheap, having accepted .£5OO a year to do
the work for which <£l2oo, or thereabouts, was
previously paid. This appointment can only
be regarded as an insult to Australians, as a
degrading assumption that there is no Aus-
tralian competent to perform the task of judging
the relative importance of the historical
documents of his own native land. Dibbs
has strained at gnats and swallowed camels.
What was the refusal to appoint any number
of political deadbeats to the Upper House
in comparison with the appointment of an
Imperial straggler to manipulate Australian
documents? Perhaps, after all, the Australian
gets what he deserves. If he is content to see
all the fat billets, political and otherwise, fall
to the enterprising foreigner, if he is satisfied
to be protected by a subsidised fleet, to be
clothed with British shoddy, to have his highest
court of appellate jurisdiction in a dingy office
in Fleet-street, London —then he ought not to
grumble if his history, which he allows out-
siders to make, is also written by an outsider
of one year’s residence. The only outlook for
Jiim is to disguise himself in an English accent
and London clothing, when he may hope to
have some chance in the land of his birth
provided his identity is never betrayed.
The S.M. Herald leader which tried to prove
that, in our present system of party politics,
there is no useful place for the independent
M.P., not only urged an opinion which Parkes
holds strongl} 7 , but introduced certain extracts
from Edmund Burke which everybody knows
Parkes has by heart. In fact, the article is
just such a one as Parkes has spoken, when in
power or in opposition, fully a hundred times.
But, withal, it, if sound, only amounts to a
deadly arraignment of “ the present system of
party politics.” “Party loyalty,” says Herbert
Spencer, “ has come to be a fancied virtue to
which the real virtue of veracity is sacrificed.”
What is more contemptible than for men, as in
last New South Wales Parliament and any
number of Parliaments before it, to feign illness,
to feign business, or to invent some other false-
hood to justify absence from a division in which
they should have had to vote either for what
they knew to be wrong or against the behests
of the leaders of their party ?
Queensland has a small but increasing tobacco-
industry, which has gradually grown up with
the aid of a protective tariff. It is now pro-
posed to strangle this industry in its infancy
by imposing an excise duty of Is. per lb. in
order to raise revenue. The proposition is open
to only three objections—it taxes enterprise, it
taxes agriculture, and it taxes the poor—since
tobacco, and especially home-grown tobacco, is
particularly a poor man’s luxury. And, of
course, the tobacco-growers, being small farmers
scattered all over the country, cannot bring
such pressure to bear on the Treasury as the
ring in Brisbane, and the gorged land-mono-
polists whose aim it is to evade taxation. The
Treasurer is quite willing to impoverish the
country districts as long as he secures the sup-
port of Brisbane vested interests.
When Maloney’s Woman’s Suffrage Bill was
under discussion in the Victorian Assembly,
Frank Madden, M.L.A., delivered himself of
an original argument. “It is a well-known
fact,” said Frank, “ that when once a woman
falls she falls, like Lucifer, never to rise
again,” and on this ground F. Madden con-
siders that female suffrage is a general, all-
round foolishness and a snare. Which, by the
way, it may or may not be, but whether it is or
is not, Madden’s argument is only worthy of an
aboriginal, and not a very bright aboriginal at
that. For Madden’s theory about Lucifer rests
entirely on the Scripture, and the same Scrip-
ture tells ot a fallen woman who did rise again.
Moreover, though Madden’s theoretical ac-
quaintance with fallen women is evidently
something tremendous, he doesn’t know all the
race, and if those whom he does know never
rose any more, that might be owing to a variety
of causes. In any case, there is no “ well-
known fact ” in the matter ; all society does
know is that almost every fallen woman whom
it knows has remained down, one sufficient
reason being that society has unanimously sat
on her head and kept her down. As for the
fallen woman whom society doesn’t know about,
she probably rose again, which is most likely
the reason why society continues not to know
about her even to this day. Anyhow, the
horsey Madden seems a grotesque, not to say
coarse and hog-like, authority on such a sub-
ject, and the connection between the fallen
woman and the suffrage isn’t by any means so
obvious as it might be.
Queensland Parliamentary salaries are likely
to be increased to the old figure of .£3OO a year.
The hard-up Government supporters have got
tired of living on £l5O in the hope that it would
kill the Labour-party, and unless they can be
bribed to drop the agitation, the Ministry will
have to yield. The Labour men are used to £3
a week; but the Government tail can’t do
without the fat of the land, and it is Jack
Hamilton, a most “reliable” member, who
leads the rebellion. Premier Nelson professed
to be much shocked at the iniquity of men
voting to increase their own salaries (though he
made no objection to the Griffith job or the
Robb scoop), and said Ministers would work for
nothing “ rather than so soil the reputation of
the province.” The tail, however, who have
not the Treasury and the bank to fall back
upon, couldn’t see how this would help them ;
and Nelson’s pathos fell on flinty ground. It
was mighty foolish pathos, anyhow, since if
Parliament is competent to reduce salaries it is
competent to raise them ; and as for the reputa-
tion of the province, after the proceedings of
the gang in office for the last four years, Nelson
might as well talk of soiling the reputation of
Messalina.
Premier Patterson, at the Mayor of Mel-
bourne’s quarterly guzzle, said people who
formed their impressions of Parliament from
the newspapers were likely to be mistaken.
They can’t be more mistaken than the people
who" get their ideas of these little “ representa-
tive gatherings ” at the Town Hall, or any-
where else, from a printed report of the
proceedings. No outsider would suppose that
Mayor Snowden, rising to propose the health
of Her Majesty’s Ministers in Victoria, had to
be reminded by Patterson that he was propos-
ing that awful toast, and not responding to a
speech already made. Neither would the
outsider assume that Patterson obligingly
prompted the speaker at every fifth word,
striking himself the chief notes of the
Mayor’s oration for which he had a con-
ventional reply on the tip of his long tongue.
The reporters, those “ scribbling ” objects of
J.B.P.’s historic scorn, are silent on such
points. With their tongues in their cheeks
they employ themselves in covering these pain-
fully absurd functions with a cloak of import-
ance. The public don’t hear of L. L. Smith
shouting to His Worship—“ Whether you’re
mayor again, or not, we’ll get you into the
Legislative Council, and you’ll be one of us."
One of us ! One of the little mob that works
the Victorian oracle, and fattens upon the ignor-
ance of those who read newspapers and are so
mistaken in consequence. If Patterson had a
grain of modesty in his composition he wouldn’t
try to be funny at the expense of the grea* Sup-
Press.
Premier Patterson has performed a char-
acteristic “lightning change” of sentiment as
to the position of the Victorian Judges. Four
or five months ago, he snorted publicly at the
monstrous absurdity of expecting the Bench to
be spared retrenchment. The notion of a
Judge drawing more salary than Patterson,
for instance, brought an indignant flush to his
iron cheek. He desired to know whether these
land-boom prices for mere legal judgments and
sentences, and so forth, were to be maintained in
times of depression. The meeting didn’t seem
to respond in the manner he had expected,
neither did the country begin to whoop for this
statesmanlike suggestion, so in time it dawned
upon the Pride of Castlemaine that his intellect
had led him astray. Hear him now in reply
to an interviewer : “The Premier agreed with
Mr. Pdrves that a high, courageous and in-
dependent Bench (! !) was the very centre of
our national existence. The Judges were
altogether different from even other servants of
the State, and high above any political action.
There was no intention to propose a reduction
of the salaries of the present occupants of the
Bench.” Nor of future occupants. There are
no intentions of any kind left in Patterson.
A prime cause of the current quarrel between
squatters and shearers is the new agree-
ment which makes the squatters’ repre*
sentative sole judge of the fitness of the sheep
to be shorn. After working amicably for two
years under an agreement approved by repre-
sentatives of both masters and men in conference,
the united squatters suddenly substituted another
w r hich they are endeavouring to run down the
men’sthroatsat thepointoftheshears. Thisarbi-
rary and discreditable action has met with the re-
sistance which rnighthave been expected. Human
nature has not yet learnt under such provoca-
tion to turn the other cheek to the smiter ; and
though The Bulletin concedes that the
squatters are, like the usurer who charges 200
per cent, whenever he can get it, within their
legal rights, it denies them the ethical right to
exercise their rights. Now, to shear wet sheep
is unprofitable and most unhealthy, but there
is a constant tendency on the part of some
squatters to hurry through shearing even when
the wool is dripping; so that, as the men
tersely put it, under the objectionable clause in
the new agreement, if the sheep have just
swum across a river, and the shed boss says
they are dry, they must be shorn, or the men
are liable to lose their wages for breach of con-
tract. The squatters’ representatives have con-
sistently contended that this was all moonshine
—no employer would dream of asking his hands
to shear wet sheep. Unluckily decisive facts

August 14, 1894.
My Dear Moorabinda—
What Melbourne society wants to know is why
Miss Asterisk, from down South, who became
Mrs. Blank, went ’Ome, and lately returned a
grass-widow to visit the scenes of her youth —why
this former Baratarian belle didn’t go through
the usual treadmill of presentation, etc., after she
landed her bridegroom. It has gone round like
wildfire that Mrs. Blank has not peeled her
shoulders and lengthened her skirts to kiss the
Royal hand, as most Baratariennes do who go
“ home ” and marry above a shop, and people
want to know whether the lady’s alleged parti-
cipation in a certain historical society kick-up,
which froze her reception at the local Guvmentouse
for some time afterwards, has anything to do with
it?
The written laws of justice ukase that a man
condones any sort of a “ past ” his wife may have
when he marries her—but are the unwritten laws
of society built on the same broad gauge, I
wonder? What I mean to say is, will some one
giddy and, after all, harmless frolic, in a remote
part of her Majesty’s dominions, keep the frisky
damsel out of H.M.’s front-parlour even when
■aid damsel has settled down into young matron-
hood ? If it will I can’t help thinking that the
“subject of this notice” must pose as an awful
warning to young, and generally well-meaning,
society maidens who now and then experience a
longing to kick over the splash-board.
Everything that a ball-committee could do-
good-looking decorations in profusion, a satin-like
floor, lively music, ton-weight bouquets all round
to the patronesses, and a regular banquet at
11 p.m.—was done by the engineers of the ball in
aid of St. Vincent’s Hospital, at Sydney Town
Hall, the other Tuesday. The people responsible
for its frostiness are t!ie ball-going citizens who
kept away perseveringly from one of the most
deserving annual charity balls, and the oldest ball
of the lot, too, now that the School of Industry
relies on a cake-fair to fill its little bag. Light-
heeled Sydney hasn’t any sympathy with antique
philanthropies, and people are saving themselves,
perhaps, for the Hospital’s ball next month, which
will nrobably be a replica of the over-crowded
F. A.B. ball when people danced the lancers on as
much floor as you could cover with your hat, and
only footballers waltzed.
But St. Vincent’s ball was only a disappoint-
ment in a financial sense—the small attendance
made the dancing pleasant, and all the dresses got
fair play. Lady Duff (in black silk draped in
cream lace) came early and was accompanied by
her two eldest daughters and the new vice regal
secretary, Mr. Pennyman, vice Mr. Galton, who,
having become N.S. Wales’stipendiary historian,
has given up the glorious privilege of being the
final authority on “ Who’s Who.” Mr. Penny-
man is a slight youth with a somewhat pale
countenance trimmed with an unimpressive
darkish moustache. He would make an ideal
bank-clerk. I can’t tell you yet whether the blue
facings suit him or not, for, squiring Lady Duff
only (by the way, they played Her Ex. in to the
Anathema which belongs properly to the Governor
alone) he didn’t wear full official fig.
Later in the evening His Ex. arrived with that
particular aide-de-camp who walks like a native
companion, and I couldn’t help noticing what
different styles there are of wearing the striped
ribbon and star that betoken the K.O.M.G.
Nearly all begin by wearing the ribbon as if it
were a locket and chain. It is only when they
get over the first fearful joy of being decorated
like a horse that has won three prizes at the show
that they begin to drape it showily about their
persons. Diagonally across the shirt-buzzum,
with the star pinned on to the coat-collar, is where
the majority pull up.
There was a fair sprinkling of pretty dresses at
St. Vincent’s ball. Miss Heather Duff wore the
nicest V.R. frock —pink silk trimmed with silver
gimp ; Mrs. Robert Peudergast, in ivory brocade
with dark-green sleeves covered in cream lace, a
cluster of crimson roses on the right shoulder and
handsome diamonds, out a neat figure in the
kaleidoscopic throng;golden-haired Mrs. (General)
Hutton was in blue silk. The severity of the
dresses assumed by one or two socially prominent
ladies looked as if they had progressed in taste
from the days when they used to get costumes
made out of violently-brocaded window curtains ;
yet to wear all white or all black at a time when
rainbow-tints are de rigueur always looks to me
like a confession of weakness—as if the wearer
were playing for safety 1 There were several sar-
torial enormities on the floor, and I can’t think
why a society girl in white satin had trimmed
herself in a fitful trickling sort of way from the
throat downwards with bright pink flowers. I
never saw anything more suggestive of pulmonary
hemorrhage in the whole course of my life.
Can anybody tell me whether it’s the latest snip
to sit on the floor? or are chairs still fashionable as
media for the support of one’s person? because, at
St. Vincent’s Ball, first His Ex. sat nursing his
knee on the shallow steps of the dais, and then I
observed Mrs. Hutton doing exactly the same
thing—no, not exactly, for she nursed her bouquet
instead. I have seen young and skittish society
maidens sprawling on the dais steps before
now—but when it comes to the Governor and the
leading society lady kicking over the traces of
official deportment, I wonder “like the old ladies
do, as to what we’re all a-comin’ to.”
And yet, in other ways, local society (I mean the
real Jamaica with a dash of officialdom about it)
becomes more punctilious every day. For example,
some itinerant female cordon bleu gave a culinary
seance in Sydney the other day at which Lady
Duff was present and “Miss Granville represented
Mrs. Hutton.” Though, mind you, I don’t regard
this as a sample instance of nice punctilio now I
come to look at it. Most society women who value
their digestions would prefer to send an under-
study to a cookery show, from which the front
row of patronage never can escape without tasting
the exhibits on the end of the fork.
What do you think is the very latest frisky
notion in hosen ? Stockings with pockets in them.
Little pockets up near the knee, and bearing
mottoes of a more or less familiar and encouraging
character woven round about, none of which I
would venture to repeat, for the world is always
so suspicious of double-meanings in everything.
Of course, the pocketed stockings won’t catch on
to any great extent —at least, I hope not. The
models I saw were made of silk—red and dark-
blue, with a little red pocket—and if any average
woman innocently buys them, with the idea that
they’d be handy for tram-tickets, the result will
be better than the ballet when the guard cornea
round.
Talking about stockings (a thing no well-regu*
lated girl ever does in public), I want to say that
the new Sterling Stainless hose are the neatest
things in leg-wear I have' yet come across. I
couldn’t oorne across them sooner, by the way,
because they are the very latest thing—a latter-
day boon in hosiery, doing away with the danger
to the skin, and discomfort of having your “ daisy
roots ” dyed the hue of your coloured stockings.
’Tvvas a medical man who “drew my attention,”
as Mr. Parkes says, to the Sterling Stainless
stockings, which are obtainable, I suppose, from
all decent Australian drapers—l know I got them
easily enough. You can get stainless silk gloves,
too, from long eights to babies’ noughts, and the
mere idea of a cool glove that won’t dye your
hands is a pleasing thing, when you reflect how
the vast Australian continent perspires as one
individual for six months out of the twelve.
“Ma’mzelle Nitouche,” at Sydney Lyceum last
Saturday, must have kept Nellie Stewart’s dresser
on the kangaroo jump, for Nellie’s changes of
costume were frequent and free, so to speak, all
through the Frenohy little mixture—a veritable
theatrical souffle, bothering nobody’s mental diges-
tion. Between her first blue frock of a oonvent-
s-'hool girl (when any intelligent observer spotted
Nellie Stewart at once by reason of her petticoats
being ten tucks shorter than any of the others)
and her return to that becoming garment in the
last scene), Nellie’s most effective dress wasn’t a
dress at all in the paper-pattern sense; only an
oriental drapery of rainbow silk and sequins over
the wearer’s birthday suit, apparently. Every-
body knew it was silk fleshings, of course, but,
still, the effect was very nautchy and also very
nice.
Florence Young, in a lobster-salad costume
split up to her hip, and with her garters tied round
her elbows, made her first appearance this season
as “Oorinne, an actress,” in the green-room
scene. I would like to point out, by the way, for
the benefit of gilded and very young youth, that
in many Australian green-rooms I have been but
never in one like that. The green-room of real
stage-life is generally a stuffy cavity about the
size of an adult packing-case, and in it the chorus-
girls off the chain for the moment sit knitting
babies’ socks and conversing in stifled whispers, as
if the corpse were lying in the next room. I have
often noticed that green-rooms as big as ball-
rooms, conveying the idea of perpetual champagne-
and-oysters, and diamonds as big as saucers for
the golden-haired habitues thereof, are peculiar to
the stage—as peculiar as the preternaturally
virtuous heroine. Like the girl with the curl on
her forehead—when the stage is good it is very
good indeed, and when it is bad it is horrid.
That somewhat diffident baritone, Mr. Wallace
Brownlow, who chortles bravely on in spite of a
cold which keeps him speechless all day, made a
gorgeous appearance as what Talmage would call
a lootenant, and added a boarding-girl’s dream of
a black moustache to his other fatal attractions.
By the way, another difference separating t’other
from which is that, whereas Ryley’s Byronic eye
never could tear itself away from the boxes, the
eagle glance of W.B. soars past them to the gods.
Perhaps, though, when Paul Jones’ medicine-
man lets him go loose among society tea-oar ties,
his wink will stop half-way, too.
After the musical absurdity came what every-
body was waiting for —the living pictures, and I
couldn’t help thinking that anybody on the war-
path after frocks would find a great absence of
material in the tableaux. But don’t let Sydney
society dai’e to say they’re improper 1 The big
circle at Sydney Lyceum was filled from end to
end with the very best we’ve got (including Mrs.
(Captain) le Poer Trench, nie Miss Nellie Cox
(who is taking her little sister back to England
with her on a six months’ trip), and all were abso-
lutely glued to their chairs until “ God Save the
Queen.” Not an opera-glass flinched, nor did a
blushing damsel take refuge in reading her pro-
gramme upside-down after the manner of the
genuinely embarrassed, so if Darling Point says
it’s dreadful after that everybody will be forced to
the conclusion that Darling Point prefers it close
to the knuckle.
From a sartorial standpoint, there is little to say
on the pictures ; for poseuses must certainly fly
light in the matter of wardrobe. One layer of
extensile fabric, either pink or pipe-clayed, and a
yard or so of gauze, was the drapery of most of
the poseuses, who were mostly shapely beauties
unadorned in almost the literal sense of the
phrase. All the same, I wasn’t completely para-
lysed at the latest London novelty. “Venus”
hadn’t a goddess’s profile, and some of her real hair
was revealed under the whitewash. Then, the
handsome Mona Rathbone was unsuitably placed
as Will-o’-the-wisp. Imagine a Will-o’-the-wisp
weighing about 11-stone, and lying back on the
top of feathery grasses with a mocking smile and
sleepy, disdainful eyes ! Miss Mona, as a woman,
looked all that was seductive and alluring, but I
must confess that I thought her overweight for a
Jaok-o’-Lantern. Marie Rathbone’s prettiest
poses were Psyche (in a singlet) at the well, and
the Fairy of the Moon, with stars twinkling in
the blue —though, in my barbarian ignorance, I
thought no movement was allowed in living
pictures. Most of the models stood like pillars
of salt for immobility. Nobody sneezed. Faint
music murmured, and a fat man behind me said,
with a contented sigh, that it reminded him of
Paree._ From his remarks he seemed to have,
while in Lutetia, spent most of his pocket-money
at the Moulin Rouge.
East Sydney Rowing Club annual ball at West’s
rooms, the other evening, was a most enjoyable
affair. The secretaries, Messrs. Aokland and Hill,
kept things bouncing all the evening, and Mr.
Terne, as hon. sec., must have viewed his work
with pride. Most of the dresses were new and
neat, and the supper, d la fourchette, was very
artistically served up. And, talking of hops,
reminds me that the Sydney half-squadron cavalry
hold their annual ball in the Paddington Town
Hall on 22nd inst., which the secretary informs
me Gov. and Lady Duff and suite, and Major-
General and Mrs. Hutton, are patronising, and
will attend.
The ceremonious opening of the new and palatial
Sydney Hospital, in Macquarie-street, by ex-
Premier Dibbs on Friday, 10th instant (aren’t the
Hospital authorities superstitious?), wasn’t quite
the usual sort of opening, because it was the first
one I, at all events, remember when a Governor
in full working order, with his flag flying, wasn’t
even asked to do the golden-key trick. Vice-regal
matters must be in a perilous state when an ex-
Premier is unanimously accorded the star part
and his Ex. isn’t encouraged to come along and
cheer with the rest of the audience ! I under-
stand that Gov. Duff is “not at home” when Mr.
Dibbs calls; likewise, that Mr. Dibbs is now
always “hout” to his Ex.
Otherwise the opening was common-place enough
—a pushing crowd—optimistic speeches from the
broad and easy flight of steps—and the opening of
the big door which almost every Sydneyite sound
in wind and limb frequently hopes will never
open unto him, nicely carved (allegorical of what
will happen to some of the patients, I thought 1)
and frescoed as it is. A bit of premeditated side-
play was when Mr. Dibbs before turning the
golden key asked the two ladies nearest him to
kiss it for luck. But the crowd is never satisfied.
“Kissing the key indeed,” said a disgusted
feminine voice behind me, “I’d rather kiss the
Premier.”
Yours affectionately,
SAPPHO SMITH.
Melbourne Confidences.
i _ Melbourne, August 14.
The widow of a munificent Melbournian, who
perpetuated his high-sounding surname by certain
University endowments, is said to be again turn-
ing her fancy to thoughts of honeymoon. The
departed one was a widower when she married
him, as testified by the gorgeous stained-glass
window in a Toorak tabernacle to the memory of
his First Love, which used to impart a distressing
yellow tone to the complexion of his second when
she prayed in its vicinity.
No one expected, perhaps, that the good man’s
loss would prove irreparable, but it is a coinci-
dence that his successor-elect, a pious bank-
inspector, is not only another widower but one of
doubly-dyed disadvantages, inasmuch as twa
angel brides await his coming in the Spirit World.
Some people are hardly treated by Fate. Why
should an attractive woman play second fiddle to
the end of the chapter? In a novel, a widow so
winsome and well-dressed, would bestow herself
upon an impecunious hero of her girlish days and
live happily ever afterwards.
Old ladies say that widowers make most con-
siderate husbands, on the “ wish-I’d-been-nicer ”
principle. Does the same recommendation apply
to widows, I wonder? Someone, who has tried
the experiment, says “iVb; they’re always com
paring you with the other fellow 1 ” And hi a
weary air suggests that he envied t’other fellow the
distance which lends enchantment to the lady’s
view. Of course, some people don’t shrink from
the thought of a matrimonial predecessor. No
sooner does a visitor cross the threshold of a cer-
tain fashionable suburban villa than the Man of
the_ House leads him off to see the valuable theo-
logical library, “ willed to my wife by her first
husband, the Rev. Blanky Blank.”
A similar case is that of a certain northern lady
addicted to open-air preaching. Guests at her
hospitable board are entertained (I believe) by
minute accounts of the sayings and doings of
“my husband in Heaven.” Her fleshy spouse
occasionally interrupting with meek ejaculations
such as “Did he, Ma?” “Just like him, Ma!”
“ You don’t say so, Ma 1 ’’ etc.
One afternoon this vassal and his liege lady
were holding a temperance meeting at a street
corner. Someone in the crowd shouted—“ What
would you do if you heard Mac was in a pub?”
“I'd follow him,” thundered “ Ma,” fixing an
awful glance on the imaginary culprit, “and in
the presence of his drunken companions I’d catch
him by the hair of his head and drag him to hia
home—that’s what I’d do, and what every wife
worthy of the name would do 1”
At the Salon, the other day, a lady read a paper
(by Professor Somebody, or other) on Luxury.
Luxury, it seems, is all a matter of circumstance.
To a millionaire railway-tourist, a footwarmer, by
force of habit, has become a necessity, and to
deprive him of it would be more reprehensible
than to rob a pauper of his blanket. The latter
(poor wretch) hasn’t invariably possessed the extra
covering, so it is really a luxury to him and, as
such, should be dispensed with.
To the beggar brat, a penn’orth of lolly-pops is
more of a luxury than a 10-guinea bonne-bouche
to the gourmand. Hence, if you’re going to do
away with superfluities, retrench the gutter-snipe
first. Them’s Salong sentiments.
The “ rational-dress ” scheme fights for life, and
in spite of the brutal revelations of the Maoriland
wedding-portrait, some women still advocate
divided skirts. The latter, they say, are more
secretive as regards ankles when their wearers are
getting in or out of vehicles, &c. But, after all,
when it comes to a regular upset, it’s six of one
and half-a-dozen of the other. This was apparent
in a recent ladies’ bicycle race. As the competi-
tors tripped on to the course they all looked
palatable (as I heard a man express it) in neat
fcnickerbocker suits, while to one of their number,
a tall, fair girl, the costume was pre-eminently
becoming. They had scarcely started, however,
when “ Beauty ” came to grief. Her machine
capsized, and she was involved in its fall, though
she hadn’t the luck to be buried from sight
beneath the ruins. A woman, astride on a fallen
bicycle, with her boots wildly beckoning for
assistance, is not a pleasing spectacle, whether she
wears petticoats or bloomers.
The original Dion B.’s brother, old editor Bour-
oicault, who died in Sydney lately, leaves several
charming daughters, all well known in the most
select Melb. circles and remarkable for good looks
inherited from their still handsomer mother. The
latter, who is exactly like one’s conception of a
dowager duchess, last came into notice as witness
in a court case, arising out of a dispute with a
fashionable Melb. oostumi&re over the fit of a
Derby gown.
Long ago, when pater Bourcicault ran a paper
up North, every man who came to the district
succumbed to the charms of his graceful daugh-
ters. But ambition was a family failing, and few
of the bachelors who strayed as far as the back-
blocks township came up to the standard of
elegibility. Two of the sisters are now dashing
Melb. matrons, while a younger one (a protegde of
Chief Justioess Madden’s) is at present cultivating
her voice in Europe.
The petitioner in a sensational divorce-case is
remembered as a pretty school-girl, with large
black eyes, rosy cheeks and the extensively-curled
fringe of ten years ago. Though brought up in
the Wesleyan way she should go, she turned a
deaf ear to any number of good young men who
taught in Sunday-schools, and at “ silly seven-
teen,” contrary to parental advice, wedded the
medical gentleman who is said to have prescribed
such large doses of beer for his nerves.
On the first night of the Madden tableaux, the
Alexandra scintillated with fashion from stalls to
gallery, and reeked with the odour of scented
programmes. The Governor, in gold buttons,
managed to sustain a wooden smile until the final
bar of “God save the Queen.” That stimulus
removed, he relapsed into reverie. Even the loyal
beams of Judge Madden failed to warm his Ex.
up again, and the Mayoress, hovering timidly
near in cream brocade, only accentuated the lone
lornity of his vice-regal state.
The tableaux were effective in their little way,
and less suggestive of the waxworks than most
shows of the kind.
In the “Belle of the Ball,” a dashing society
matron posed in the name - part, and very
much at home she looked ; though the “ Ball,” or
that section of it presented in the picture, was a
more staid and stately function than some she
wotted of. Which recalls another dame (of the
same olan as the tableau heroine) who, when
Kitchen Lancers were popular, won general
acclamation for her agility in those terpsichorean
high-jinks. It was a sicht for sair een to see her
pale blue satin train madly sweeping the rear
distance like a tidal-wave, while in the foreground,
where all drapery encumbrances were gathered up
well out of the way, the dancer’s satin shoes and
silken hose, matching her gown in colour, careered
and whirled until, to the dazed onlooker, they
conveyed the impression of two blue devils run-
ning amuck.
Dr. O’Hara sang the “Three Fishers” with
good effect. He even performed the feat of put-
ting some quite new wine into his ancient bottle,
(meaning the ballad, not the doctor) and rendered
the “harbour bar’s moaning ” with eerie pathos.
Talking of antiques, the potentate of an Aus-
tralian colony is said to cherish a grudge against
a local dame, who incessantly warbles that dole-
ful ditty, “Oh fair dove, oh fond dove 1” The
culprit is death on “expression,” but fails in
■enunciation, and at the end of every verse wails
in a throaty voice something like “But it Zoned
and moaned and GROANED!” Her distin-
guished auditor has come to loathe the perennial

At a recent luncheon, Lord Hopetoun declared
that no one would be better pleased than he when
the Countess was once more performing hersocial
duties All of which Ido verily believe. What s

A Scottish sculptor, who lost an infant child,
relieved his feelings by carving a marble figure of
a sleeping baby. The little statue so truthfully
expressed the idea of dead innocence that sad
mothers often wept over it in its niche in the
church porch. Time passed on, and the father
sold the original to a connoisseur for 100 guineas,
and made a replica for the vacant corner. ( Vide
Nat. Hawthorne in “Our Old Home.”)
The first of the female amateurs, who sang at the
Madden entertainment, was chiefly remarkable
for the contrast between her fair fresh girlish face
and her matronly figure and sombre garb. In the
ease of a maiden who always dresses ten years
older than her mother, mourning is woful beyond
expression.
In the “Reconstruction ballet ("Vicar of
Bray”), by Mrs. Green’s pupils, lingerie consider-
ations obtruded themselves more aggressively
than with an ordinary professional ballet. Per-
haps amateurs are not quite so expeditious as the
latter, but the other night, instead of merely being
conscious of a sort of giddy whirl of underlying
lace skirts, I found myself idly wondering why
the dancers, like the Pharisees, made so broad
their phylacteries, while two girls on my right
eagerly discussed the point as to whether torchon
lace or nainsook frills predominated amongst the
high-toned coryphees.
Apropos , the most expensive of Melb. drapery
firms resoundat present with the joyous clack of
thimbles and the delirious whirr of sewing-
machines. The raison d’itre of this unwonted
activity is a gorgeous trousseau, in course of con-
struction for Miss Thornley, daughter of Hon.
Nathan Thornley, M.L.O.
The last bridal outfit of any consequence made
at the big shop was for Miss Bell, of South Melb.,
now Mrs. Groom, of Bananaland. Times are not
what they were, and the glory of outfits has
departed.
