The opera of “ Mdlle. Nitouohe ” and the
tableaux vivants which followed ib made up the
brightest, giddiest show which Sydney has seen
for a long, long time. The Lyceum was crowded
to the uttermost, and when a vague rumour went
about town that the tableaux were simply too
awful for anything, and that the police would
probably shut down on the show, the rush became
so awful that some owners of reserved stalls were
offered 15s. each for their tickets. A large police
inspector made intermittent appearances in the
circle as a guarantee that the h’i of the law was on
the look-out; the Social Gimlet brigade was well-
represented, and the supply of respectable elderly
gentlemen who had grown up through their hair
was tremendous.
• • •
“ Mdlle. Nitouche ” proved to be a bright
little musical comedy, which is a little “ steep ”
in places, bub contains nothing absolutely dread-
ful. It concerns a serious-minded organist em-
ployed in a convent, who writes a wicked comic
opera on the quiet; and a demure young lady
pupil who insists on being taken to the theatre to
see ib. The wretched organ-grinder, under threats
of exposure, consents, and the pair have a
hilarious orgy. The young lady gets rid of
the prima-donna and takes to the stage herself,
and drinks champagne, and winds up by having
supper with the local military, and dresses herself
in uniform; while the organist gets drunk, and
being mistaken —by an inebriated corporal who
has just emerged from among the coals—for a
recruit, has his hair cut short and is otherwise
maltreated. The pair get back to the convent in
their uniforms early next morning, and explain
things feebly and ineffectually to the boss nun.
‘‘Mdlle. Nitouohe” is a farcical comedy of the
wildest order, set here and there to little bits of
exceedingly pretty music. Miss Nellie Stewart
and Lauri —as the organist—are the life and soul
of the play. Miss Stewart’s drum solo shows that
the fingers of the little drummer-boy of old have
lost none of their cunning, and a particularly
desperate song and dance, or rather song and
rush around, in which she and the musician first
fall away from the holy calm of their past life, is
a matter to be remembered. Wallace Brownlow
was hoarse on Saturday night but is otherwise
acceptable as a beautifully upholstered lover ; and
Howard Vernon has no music in his part hub
pervades the piece generally. Also, Miss
Florence Young looks very fetching. A dance,
in which four ladies throw their adorable feet to
dizzy heights, and reveal more underwear than
was ever seen before in the Lyceum since that
respectable house was built, is thrown in without
any extra charge.
# # *
The living pictures were a dream of loveli-
ness Set in a huge gilt frame. They went up in
an ascending scale of dazzle until they culminated
in a maiden “ mid nodings on ” sitting on a rustic
bridge along with a dog. Her back was to the
audience, and it counted her vertebrae with dread-
ful intentness. Presumably this vision’s back was
attired in flesh-coloured tights, but they looked so
real that the audience had to take them for
granted, and there was always a weird possi-
bility that the vision, knowing the audience
couldn’t really tell whether they were there or not,
and being pressed for time, had taken them for
granted herself. The other visions had more or
lesson, and were equally beautiful in their various
ways. When they were over, the audience felt
that, for once, it had put in a really satisfying
evening, and was filled up with assorted loveli-
ness. Also, there was a vague regret that the
police hadn’t risen up and stopped the show just
when the revelations were all over, and there was
no more to be seen.
* * * *
Mr. Arthur Garner’s co. started the second
week of a rather gaunt and bony season at Sydney
Criterion with “Friends,” which is a good deal
fresher than its predecessor, “Dr. Bill.” More-
over the 00. has settled down to its work, and
plays much more effectively, and it is considerably
strengthened by the reappearance of the stupen-
dous Garner himself, whose 2601b5. of meat looms
up in the part of Dawson, and blots out the land-
scape with its vastness. Mr. Garner hasn’t grown
in the least rusty during his long absence, nor
does he seem to have become any older than he
was a generation or two ago. He is still an
excellent comedian, and but for the greatness of
his circumference he looks as if he could shine in
any line of stage business. But he is too im-
pressive for knockabout comedy; Garner in
an exciting part which involved much rush-
ing around, would tread all the stage furni-
ture into matchwood. He would make good
avalanche, however, or a most convincing
landslip, and he could represent the downfall of
Poland to perfection. The rest of the little com-
pany is satisfactory, save that the imposing Miss
Edith Blande—one of the few women on the Aus-
tralian stage who can profess to be Garner’s
wife without looking ridiculously like his infant
daughter—plays her part in rather a ragged
fashion. Messrs. Diver, Kelly and Hans Phillips
are all equal to their parts, and Miss Hilda Fraser
gives a pleasant rendering of a character which
is mainly smile and white frock. Altogether,
Garner’s show deserves far better luck than it has
been blessed with so far.
* * * %
The dismal weep of Miss Watts-Phillips
still pervades the “Lion’s Heart” at Her
Majesty’s, and she casts off the wicked colonel
nightly, and retires to her dungeon in a manner
that reflects the greatest credit upon everybody
concerned. The principal outrage on probability
is the downfall of the wicked colonel in the last
act, which collapse is brought about through the
unsupported assertion of a dying cripple. The
cripple is carried in by Geo. Rignold, and
states that the colonel is really a notorious
criminal who made away with the real officer
many years previously, and has_ person-
ated him ever since. This statement is listened
to with breathless interest, and then, after
an awful pause, the colonel is arrested and run
in. The situation is as impressive as if a Domain
loafer entered N.S.W\ Parliament and stated that
Speaker Abbott wasn’t himself but another person,
who had secretly murdered the real Abbott and
assumed his chair and his wig without anybody
noticing the difference. Then the Speaker would
naturally be seized on the spot, and Abigail would
be instantly released on general principles, and
Major-General Hutton would strike an imposing
attitude in the background. “ My Jack ” is
promised next Saturday.
* % * *
Miss Hilda Spong, who is steadily making
her way in popular favour, had a benefit last week
at Sydney Criterion. The event came off too late
for any serious notice in this issue, but, according
to popular report, the financial results promised to
be satisfactory to the young lady’s exchequer.
The Spong season at the Gri. had the misfortune
to leave off just when it began to be a paying con-
cern, and also just about the time when
Miss Hilda’s resemblance to Essie Jenyns
began to be apparent, so the benefit pro-
bably came in at the right time. By way of
further accentuating her resemblance to the
vanished Essie, Miss Spong billed herself for the
balcony-scene in “Romeo and Juliet, with Mr.
Plimmer, instead of Plain Bill Holloway, as
Romeo. The new man was a decided improve-
ment ; still, a really beautiful and dreamy-looking
Romeo with a soulful expression is one of the
long-felt wants of the Australian stage just now.

The Bulletin prints on page 14 ot
this issue a picture of Miss Ada Baker. When
Miss Baker played Hollee Reetee in “ The Nautch
Girl,” in India, she managed to surround that
unattractive name with much magic. Of course,
India is a long way off ; but Miss Baker is going
to try and be charming in Australia also.

Sydney School of Arts hall, which suffers
from so many erratic openings and shuttings, _ is
occupied at present by Harry Barrington s min-
strels, who look moderately permanent, so far.
The 00. includes many names which recalls the
old days of Colonel Fred. Hiscocks’burnt-oorkists,
among them Horace Bent —he of the oleaginous
voice—Dave Gardiner, Martyn Hagan, and Miss
Priscilla Verne. A few years ago it would have
been an excellent spec., but the best days of
nigger-minstrelsy are past in Sydney, and the
African humorist is an uncertain investment in
these times.
* * * *
The enterprising Darrell reproduced him-
self at Melbourne Royal on Saturday, as the hero
of his latest drama, “ Crimson Thread.’ George,
in this instance, is a pretty tinted detective from
Scotland Yard who comes over to Chicago for the
purpose of avenging a murder committed 23 years
earlier. He is likewise on the track of a long lost
heiress, but the heiress seems a small matter com-
pared with George’s solemn duty to the dead. His
immediate business is to obtain proofs of Charles
Holloway’s guilt, wherefore he pervades the
vicinity of that desperate looking man and lights a
cigar with calm Darrellian deliberation every time
he picks up another link in the chain of evidence.
Whilst George is collecting links Holloway
slouches around with two blood-stained hands
in the pockets of his pants threatening to kill
the first dog that plays him false. On one
occasion a member of Holloway s gang stabs
George through the lungs and liver and
George pretends that he is by falling
lifeless on the margin of a tank-river, for-
tunately the heiress strays on to a railway-bridge,
in order that she may avoid a passing tram by
dropping herself carefully into the 10,700 gallons
of tank water. Then George plunges into the wet
and rescues the dripping lady amidst wild
applause, although he might more easily have
hked her ashore with an umbrella-handle. One
of tho Chicago constabulary fires skywards and
lodges a bullet in Holloway, with the result that
he removes his hands from his pockets and con-
fesses that the real heiress is not somebody else, so
George, from Scotland Yard, betroths himself to
.£lO,OOO a year and explains that the Crimson
Thread bears some mysterious relation to the
famous Silken Bonds.
• * *
“ The Crimson Thread ” is not the worst
of George Darrell’s contributions to dramatic
literature, but it is one of the leastexciting. His
minor characters are burdened with more than
their usual allowance of ancient dialogue, and
George himself is guilty of some terrible attempts
at philosophy when he should be doing great deeds
on horseback, or crawling up to the enemies guns.
He certainly clambers down a fire-escape, and
soaks himself in the tank, and kicks innumerable
desperadoes in the stomach; nevertheless,
there is a lot of George wasted, so to
speak. Miss Maud Williamson gives an air
of reality to a decoy lady, although her
fetching American accent breaks down completely
when she turns penitent, which makes her reforma-
tion a matter for regret. Miss Gladys Heigh
speaks her lines and tumbles into the WVUO
gallons with much sincerity ; also, George Mel-
ville, J. Sweeney, Cathcart and others put in
claims for charitable consideration. Miss Alice
Deorwyn talks freely in an irrelevant character
which Miss Polly Emery might have rescued from
derision.

At the Alexandra they are renewing old
bills for brief terms, preparatory to setting up
“ Missing at Lloyd’s,” a coffin-ship story of thril-
ling interest, on Saturday next.
** * *
“Mrs. Tanqueray” is about to close her
esteemed season at the Princess’s. Her last tussle
with Saint Ellean, and her final impulse to give
Tanqueray a good, hard knock, will occur on
Friday, and it is hoped that the management will
then take the opportunity to mention what Paula
died of. The cause of decease is bound to come
out at the inquest, anyway, unless the doctor,
who figures in the first act only, can be persuaded
to inaugurate another friendship with the widower
by giving him a certificate. On Saturday the B.
and B. company appear in “Aunt Jack,” a farcical
comedy new to Australia.
** * *
Talmage is announced to deliver three
addresses at Melb. Town Hall, commencing to-
night (Thursday), and Brother R. S. Smythe
wishes to impress upon the ungodly the fact that
these addresses are not sermons. Yet there seems
to be a certain religious significance about the
hasty withdrawal, pro. tern., of two wandering
evangelists, one of whom has retired into the
interior, whilst the other has become indisposed.
Evidently they suspect Smythe’s apostle of a
tendency to lecture the Wor-r-d.
** * *
Also, at the Town Hall, on Tuesday (21st),
pretty Miss Ethel Haydon will take a complimen-
tary concert to cheer her on her way to Europe.
The same building deserves to be crowded a week
later (28th), when Mrs. Palmer, who has never
grudged her services in aid of any other good
cause, will figure as the beneficiaire in a specially
large and hearty entertainment. After 29 years
of professional fame and prosperity, this lady is
now suffering the fate, it is said, of all honest
people who placed their trust in Melbourne banks.
* Jit * *
The Melbourne Opera Club amateurs
intend to repeat their respectable “Gondoliers”
performance at the Bijou on Saturday, and two
more nights next week. Compared with the
Lyric Club displays this is a highly finished enter-
tainment, and it will probably yield a profit. The
Cogill Bros., after a fruitful season here, return to
the little Gaiety Theatre below on Saturday, the
aforesaid Gaiety having been re-decorated and re-
christened as the Oxford. The minstrel mind
seems to discover hidden virtues in such changes
of name. At the Alhambra they have arranged
to produce Harry Rickards on the same evening.
Rickards, it seems, is “resting” after an arduous
season at Sydney Tivoli, but why he should come
to the Alhambra to find rest is not very apparent.
The Lynch Bellringers are likewise resting at th“
Athenaeum and suburban halls.
** * *
The stage- morality discussion in Melbourne
has been done to death by the advts. B. and B.
published a greasy quotation from Rev. George
Walters’ sermon on Mrs. Tanqueray, and followed
it up with another religious notice: “ The Last
Words of this marvellous work are, ‘lf I’d only
been merciful!’ ” At the same time time, George
Coppin was commending “ George Barnwell ” to
parents and guardians, and Dan Barry dilated
upon the blessedness of the maxim, “ It’s never
Too Late to Mend.” Then Dan crowned the
edifice of managerial humbug with a solemn re-
ference to poor old “East Lynne,” which was
intended by Providence to teach “ the terrible
retribution that overtakes the sinner who dares
to break the solemn mandate of the Seventh
Commandment.” Coghill Bros, are cudgelling
their brains to discover a reason why every clergy-
man should recommend their variety show to
mothers and daughters. Also Frank Clark would
like to know whether a blackened corner-man has
any Bible application to the widow and tho
orphan.
** # *
The irrepressible L. L. Lewis, of the
“ Helping Hands ” amateur entertainment co.,
wrote to the Melb. newspapers lately, stating that
the difficulties which threatened to interfere with
his final performance had been “ happily over-
come.” Also he announced that there would be a
grand triple bill for this great occasion, a bill that
“should fill the Temperance Hall.” The combina-
tion of charity and conceit in this “Helping
Hands ” business has been remarkable throughout.
But all amateur shows for a good cause are full of
vanity. Disengaged pros, would be quite willing
to contribute a far more attractive performance if
the hon. sec. and the hon. director and the rest of
the hon. Samari tans were willing to work for them
as they now do for the glorification of a company
of sticks —or “sticks” mostly. Take the case
of the Maddens the other day. Their influence
and push accomplished two splendid audiences at
Melb. Alexandra. A fairly good programme
would have ensured a liberal attendance of out-
siders and assisted the “cause” even further.
But it wouldn’t have gratified the desire of a few
awkward girls and weak-minded men to appear at
a real theatre before people who had really paid
for the infliction.
At Poverty Point.
Grace Noble goes to England at the conclusion of the
present B. and B. Melb. season. She is a pretty girl, and
would be a very good actress if she wouldn’t always talk

A Maoriland paper thus moralises; “I am reminded of
this by a story told me as having happened not a thou-
sand miles from Christchurch, where a great artiste
performed before a vast audience, and was afterwards
twitted that the performance lacked its usual fire. Few,
indeed, knew that the heart of the performer was swell-
ing almost to bursting owing to mental pain and anguish
at having been treated in a dastardly manner, and that
physical pain was added through an attempt to physically
incapacitate the victim of a scoundrel’s brutality.” This
is tantalising. Who was the great artiste, and who was
the scoundrel, and what had he been doing to her .
Guess 1

Effigies of Mrs. Needle have been withdrawn, by order,
from the two Waxworks shows in Melb. They were not
very libellous effigies, the lady wearing an expression of
abnormal guilelessness.

ance of George Barnwell at the Melbourne Royal, is only

the famous ‘‘Constantinople” show now running in
London. The ballet dances are all very catchy, and the
Signor, save for hair to his shoulders and a bit of extra
stoutness, is just the same old maestro.
Melbournian“ At * George Barnwell,’ the other night,
I saw the rowdiest house, all over, in my recollection.
Hope it did ’em good. Reminded me of the old-time
London executions. The last tableau is horrid. Maud
Williamson, in black, and Majeroni, in prison garb, stand-
ing on the gallows, with the ropes round their necks, and
the chaplain and executioner standing by. These per-
formers—i.e.. Miss Williamson and Majeroni, took a call
in a disgusted kind of way, but Coppin ought to insist
that they keep their ropes on, or otherwise half the
moral is lost.”
Several professional dramatic companies are playing
round Melb. suburbs, and are making bread and butter.
“ Admission, silver coin.”
Miss Helen Rowe, of Melbourne, writes a London cor-
respondent to Tub Bulletin, seems to be devoting herself
to private concert work. She recently sang at Devonshire
House before a select party of six or seven ladies, includ-
ing the Princess of Wales, and achieved a great success.
The Princess, after the performance, said to her that of
course what took place that evening would not be men-
tioned in the Press, but she was quite at liberty to
say that she (the Princess) was much charmed by her
singing. As the Princess is almost stone-deaf, this certifi-
cate may not carry much weight; but that is a mere
detail. However, the charming contralto is being taken
up by the “ nobs.” It is to be hoped it won’t turn her
head. One great bar to Miss Rowe’s success is her exces-
sive nervousness, which she cannot overcome in public.
0 Chief Justicess Madden might have engaged some out-
of-work actor or actress (there are plenty just now) to
"make-up” the society belles for her tableaux. There
they sat in rows, with cheeks like mammoth prize apples,
and with nets like bags of tow hiding their foreheads.
Spagnoletti: ‘‘l wrote of the boys and girls minstrel
company, ‘managed entirely by juveniles,’ which startled
my Melbourne suburb. These were the Juvenile Eagle
Minstrels. They have just come out with a fresh develop-
ment, in the Wellington Hall, Collingwood—a ‘Rational
Sunday Evening,’ as the bills term it, on the model of
Frank Clark, at the Alhambra, and Cogill Bros, it the
Bijou. Even agnostic parents like to send their children
to Sundav-school, just for the spirit of the thing ; but
what is the spirit of this thing? There are 24 boys and
girls on the bill, with a Sunday concert of nigger melodies
and slangy songs. It strikes me there must be some
speculator behind this 'entirely juvenile mangement ’
like the Padrones who exploit the Italian music boys.”

A tale of the real Mdme. Sans-G6ne (Th6rfese Figueur)
She speaks herself: “1 had such a fright just now. I
fancied I had lost my big diamond. I left it in ray room.
1 came back and it was gone. They tell me no one has
been in my room but the/ro(feur (floor-polisher). I have
him in at once—a big blackamoor. He swears he has not
got it. How can I tell 7 A nigger can lie as he likes, but
you cannot see whether he blushes. I just shout out that
I mean to have it back. He turns his pockets inside out.
Then he shows me his hands. He sniggers and chuckles,
as he says, ‘Nothing anywhere, madame.’ Then said 1,
' I’ll soon see. Strip,’ I cried, * strip, you black beast, as
bare as God or the Devil made you. If you don’t I’ll have
you thrashed to death.’ Off came his clothes. I got
my diamond. Here it is. Your prim ladies of honour
would have lost the diamond. They are afraid of looking
at man in his natural state. lam not. I win.”

husband, Herr Goldenstedt, with a few lessons from
Mndame Lucy Chambers, and that she was never, as
alleged, the pupil of Miss Annis Montague.

Personal Items.
N.S.W. Premier Reid has entered his
60th year.
A brother of Sir Robert Ball, the literary
astrornomer, practises as a dentist in the Bendigo
district.
Sydney E. News is now represented in
N.S.W. Parliament by two of its staff —Editor
Hogue and John Haynes.
Four members of the Reid (N.S.W.)
Ministry are teetotallers—Cook, Brunker, Smith,
and—find t he missing name.
Mr. Pennyman, Gov. Duffs new secretary,
is a very good young man, who devotes much of
his time to delivering tracts.
“Archibald Forbes, London,” is a share-
holder in the National Bank, Melbourne. So
good a journalist deserves a better fate.
According to a correspondent in a Sydney
weekly paper, N.S.W. Gov. Duff was Orange
Grand Deputy Master of a Scotch county.
Townsend M‘Dermott appears to be the
father of the Victorian Bar, as the law-list gives
the date of his admission as far back as 1840.
The Windeyers, like the Stephens, promise
to be represented for all time in the direct line o n
the judicial Bench, or at the Bar, of N.S. Wales-
The Deeming curse still works. Cawsey,
the detective who brought Deeming from West-
ralia, is just now dangerously ill from brain-
abscess.
Mrs. Doctor Dutton, plaintiff in the
hideous Melb. divorce-suit, is a daughter of the
late R. D. Oswald, the Maldon (Vic.) millionaire
mine-owner.
Pastor M'Neil and his henchman, howler
Burke, know the value of advertising. They
parade Collins-street every day on most dis-
tinguished-looking white horses.
Pastor Talmage says that whenever Henry
Clay received a young man visitor he behaved
with as much cordiality as if he were the young
man’s father. Perhaps he was.
Fleet paymaster W. W. Perry, who sucked
the poisoned-arrow wounds which proved fatal to
Commodore Goodenough in the South Sea Islands
in ’75, died last June at Caterham (Eng.), aged 48
years.
Ex-M.L.A. Turner, whose red waistcoat
excited so much attention in N.S.W. Parliament,
a few years ago, is at present boss of about 150
men on a Salvation Army labour - home in
Victoria.
N.S.W. ex-Chief Justice Stephen will be
92 on Monday, August 20 ; Premier Forrest will
be 47 on Wednesday, August 22 ; while Dr. Cock-
burn will be 44, and J. L. Purves, Q. 0., will be 51
on Thursday, August 23.
Primrose League ladies actually waited on
the widow of the late Maoriland Premier Ballance,
in London, and asked her to join their throne-and-
altar-worshipping sisterhood ! They literally
tumbled down the steps.
There are five Australian natives in the
Reid Ministry—Brunker, Gould, Suttor, Car-
ruthers, and Sydney Smith ; and Reid is so nearly
a native that his anguish that he was not born in
Wooloomooloo quite atones for the defect.
Mr. J. C. White, father of the N.S.W.
Western press, now residing at Bathurst, entered
his 85th year on 7th inst. He arrived in S.A. in
the days of Gov. Hindmarsh, and erected with
his own hands the first Wesleyan church building
in Adelaide.
Editor Curnow, of S.M. Herald, might
have been N.S. W. official historiographer on two
occasions had he chosen to put forth bis hand,
and at a much higher rate of remuneration, too,
than that at which the “records” are now to
be turned over.
Lady Hopetoun’s nose is out of splints.
She smells again. It is significant that directly
this event happened, her ladyship fled from
Smellbourne on a visit to Lord Kintore at
Adelaide. She really couldn’t stand it bursting
in upon her all at once.
While Bertrand lay under death-sentence
for a whole year in Darlinghnrst prison he had,
concealed in the roof of his mouth, a fin© dentist’s
saw with which ho intended to commit suicide if
his appeal failed. When he found his neck saved
he gave up the tiny instrument.
Mrs. Curnow, founder of the Women’s
Literary Society of Sydney, and perpetual vice-
president of that mildly - aggressive body, is
credited with being even more advanced in the
democratic sentiments she maintains than are any
of her pen and platform associates.
Curios from N.S. Wales pension-list. In
1888, J. C. Read, Governor of Darlinghurst Gaol,
retired at the age of 68 upon an annual pension of
£372 4s. 5d., and his son, W. V. Read, Traffic
Manager for Railways, retired in ’9O at the age of
50 years, with an annual pension of £546 10s.
E. M. Young is in Melbourne. He used
to manage the National Bank, at £2OOO a year,
but returns as manager of a London company for
financing Australian squattages, at £7OOO a year.
Young is a financial genius who might be profit-
ably utilised to straiten up the public finances.
It is now generally understood in N.S.W.
legal circles that two Supreme Court Judges who
are suffering from chronic illness, will retire
upon pensions. Neither has yet served the full
allotted time (15 years), after which a Judge, in
the ordinary course of things, can claim pension.
Duncan Gillies’ trip ’Orae cost the Vic.
Treasury £250. So that Dunky lived at the rate
of about £5O a week. This is the frugal little
man who, on a historical occasion, gave Id. to a
Echuca “ boots.” By the way, a number of “ the
Firm’s ” imported ballet-girls travelled per same
mail.
The cook at the Hotel Australia, Sydney,
Is a mad wag. At the dinner to Premier Geo.
Houston Reid, he put on the bill, not as might
have been reasonably expected, “ Puddeney-pie k
la Georgey Porgey,” but “ k la Houston ”
indicative, The Bulletin presumes, of the stab-
ility of the new Ministry.
Mark Jeffrey, pugilist, burglar, convict,
murderer and author of “ A Burglar’s Confessions,”
died the other day in the Launceston (Tas.) Depot.
He was an eccentric individual and when not in
gaol spent most of his time in violently assaulting
his fellow man. He blamed his “ungovernable
temper ” for his various scrapes.
At Narrara (Brisbane Water, N.S.W.), of
which Kendall has so sweetly written, died on
Thursday last Mrs. Medhurst, at the age of 94
years. She was born in Sydney in the reign of
Captain Hunter, and before the century com-
menced, She was a great-great-grandmother, and
settled at Narrara 60 years ago.
N.S.W, Premier Reid was walking along
the street in front of a Bulletin man the other
day, and as he passed a newly-painted building he
touched it meditatively to see if it was dry. This
paper doesn’t hope for much from Reid after that.
It is only the weak and trivial man who is always
wanting to find out if the paint is dry.
The 16th anniversary of the death of Dr,
John Dunmore Lang occurred on 7th inst., the
opening day of the 16th Parliament of N.S.
Wales. At the Doctor’s funeral W. B. Dailey,
John Robertson, William Neill, and Henry Parkes
were pall bearers. Three of the four have fol-
lowed the valiant Doctor into shadowland.
Ernest Williams, third son of the Vic-
torian Judge, seems to have passed an exam, at
Sandhurst (Eng.) College, which will mean—The
Bulletin sadly supposes—an army-commission
for the young man. Hartley Williams, with his
knighthood and an epauletted offspring, is not
much like the once “democratic” Judge who rode
a bicycle.
Ashton, M.P. for Hay, N.S.W., is a push-
ing young man, who has progressed from a com-
positor’s frame to the editorship of his own news-
paper in Narrandera. As an orator he is aggra-
vatingly deliberate, and hangs so long on a word,
before adding the next, that his hearers have
time to go out and have a drink without losing
the thread of his argument.
J. A. Cook, secretary of Adelaide branch
of the shearers union, as a literary artist; —
I regret to say that 1 have seen professional vipers who
live on the fruit of other men’s labours—the coward who
sneaks in the dark, snaps a shed under any agreement at
any price. . . . Who is a scab that his feelings should
be considered ? Wherefore was he made ? He is not as
other men are. He is a cheat by profession, a blackmailer
by instinct, an alien to all high and manly attributes, an
outlaw even among the blackguards’ brigade that batten
on society. Thieves will not call him kin, honest men
shun him. ... A number of these vile creatures
passed through Adelaide en route for Wilgena from
Gee'ong and Warrnambool. The looks of the lot may be
summed up as follows :—Mouths that looked like a black-
guard’s benediction, their brows as narrow as a lawyer’s
conscience, bleared eyes are the windows out of which a
shrivelled and debauched soul peeps furtively. A dog
would not defile their graves, and the devil would not
foul Hades with souls so debauched.

of wood) : “Say whin, Patrick.”

Captain Standish, Yic. Chief of Police
before Cliomley, was at one time an ardent Roman
Catholic, and only relaxed in his devotions from
a feeling that they were incompatible with mem-
bership of the Melbourne Club. Of two evils he
chose the devil, but he never lost his love for
Mother Church, and, as the principal fast-days
came round, he used to send a sovereign by a
clerk in his office for bestowal in the collection-
plate.
When Shiels (Vic.) drew his funny picture
of Treas. Carter going to the tomb in which he
laid the Income-tax last year, and saying, “Like
Lazarus, come forth ” —Dan Lazarus, the new
member for Bendigo, was sitting opposite to him.
Lazarus is the best-dressed, and the most-dressed
—man in Vic. Parliament. Everybody looked
towards him when Shiels said this ; and for a few
minutes ha longed for the retirement of that
tomb.
The parson as a species is dull, dreary,
and humourless. A sense of humour, a perfect
gulch of smile, and a long nose like an ant-eater’s,
differentiate Talmage from his brother parsons,
and account for his phenomenal success. Who
has heard Talmage assert, with the solemnity of
an Elton, that the Prophet Jonah-in-whale’s-belly
story is true, without feeling inclined to poke the
waggish divine in the ribs and chirp, “Yah!
Murkan ! who are yer stuffin’?”
Alfred Bennett, of Sydney Evening
News, is said to bo a candidate for N.S.W. Leg.
Council. By the way. Proprietor Evans, of
Sydney Sunday Times, whose son was lately
appointed Inspector of Bores, reckoned on being
hoisted into the Council by Dibbs. He hired a
room overlooking the Evening News telegraph-
board on polling-night, and his manifestations of
anxiety as his own supposed chances went up and
down with the party numbers were most enter-
taining.
Henry Gyles Turner may be immortalised,
after all, by his connection with Victoria’s first
crematorium. As chairman of the St. Kilda
(Melb.) Cemetery Board of Trustees he is taking
measures to obtain a license for a bakery which
will eventually yield a revenue sufficient to “keep
up the cemetery” in elegant style after the ground
is full, and the old original business has ceased.
Turner will lay the foundation-stone of the crema-
torium, and a tablet will tell the tale to future
generations.
Fair Play : “A Bulletin writer is unjust
to M'Neill the preacher. Look at his giving up
the Regent Square congregation, the richest
Presbyterian one in London, or probably in the
world. Here he had notoriety enough, and was
deluged with money, being also enabled to live in
velvety ease, purple and fine linen, keep his
carriage, have his sermons largely devoured,
widely bought in expensive book form, and yet
he abandons all to tramp and rough it. The
Glory hypothesis won’t fit in.”
A comely Salvation Army lass encountered
Card. Moran while railway-travelling recently,
and with much confidence held up her end of a
modest theological controversy which accidentally
arose. On leaving, the Cardinal shook hands
with her and thanked her for the many edifying
remarks she had dropped, and received in recom-
pense the suave assurance; “Don’t mention it.
But, if at any time you feel in doubt or troubled
about your soul, be sure you call on me or send
me word. Where there’s a soul to be saved, there
is my place.”
Dear Bulletin, —The bogus jewels and
the tin swords and the fancy breechclouts are
half the soul of Freemasonry—as the other half
is the petty self-importance derived from a puerile
affectation of mystery. The Freemasons boast
the Brotherhood as more comprehensive than any
other ; well, it has only to become universal to go
“ bung,” because then one fellow wouldn’t have
the tin-pot satisfaction of knowing something the
other doesn’t, and which probably isn’t worth
knowing, anyhow. Sardonyx.

Chief Justice Way, of
S.A., recognises that he
could have earned much
more at the Bar than he
receives {.£2000 a year) on
the Bench, but he confesses
that he made the exchange
with his eyes open, as he
was quite prepared to pay
liberally for the dignity and
the personal advertisement
of the position. “Who is
Way, Q. 0., compared to the
Chief Justice of S. A. ? ” he
asks, and endeavours, by
much puffing, to add that
“ cubit to his stature.” Re-
cently Nesbit, Q. 0., wrote
to a S. A. friend that he had
seen no Judge in England
who outshines Way in
respect to his manner or
his law; and the little
Chief, on being told this,
smiled complacently and
remarked: “I hope you
won’t think it conceit, but
I am quite conscious of
being able to hold my own
with any occupant of the
English Bench.”

The biggest oranges ever seen in Melbourne
are now on view. They are from Gin Gin, 50 miles
from Perth, Westralia, From half an acre of
ground there, £4OO has been realised in oranges
this season.
That story, apparently invented by the
Sydney Telegraph, that the Dibbs Ministry
tried to send Humphrey Oxenham to the N.S.W.
Upper House, was the first snag which Reid fell
over in his new capacity as Premier. At his
recent meeting he hooted at the departed Ministry
concerning its attempt to send a “two-to-one
bookmaker ” into the Council. Thereupon Dibbs
wrote to Reid asking the name of the alleged
bookmaker and further particulars about him, but
got no reply. Presently he wrote again, and this
time he instructed his messenger to roost like a
raven on a pallid bust outside Reid’s door till
the answer was forthcoming, and then Reid feebly
explained as usual that he had been wrongly
reported. What he said was simply that Dibbs
“ might ” nominate such a person. Dibbs ob-
viously scores over this little incident. The fact
that he “might” have done something which he
did not do is just as relevant as the fact that Reid
might have dragged Gov. Duff through the
Botanical Gardens by the hair, but didn’t.
The Chinese Mint does not assay its metal.
It just chances it.

EVANGELIST M‘NEILL.
S.A. Legislature ought to abolish capital
punishment, for though there has been no execu-
tion in the province for 20 years the murder-rate
compares most favourably with that of the rest of
Australia. The latest capital conviction is that
of William Brown, formerly a soldier in India,
who slew his mate, Geo. Marosky, a Pole. The
two were camped on a station near Waukaringa
(S.A.), where they were prospecting, and without
the slightest apparent motive, save greed, Allen
shot the Pole through the head. Within a fort-
night Allen had sold all deceased’s effects, and
was caught just before finally flitting from S.A.
Allen has seen much army service, and when in
drink is supposed to be a madman, owing to the
effects of a sabre-cut on the skull. This latter
circumstance may afford a reason for sparing his
miserable life.
Editor Bulletin, —About two months ago
a man named Brumey Clarke was killed here by
two niggers. Said nigs, then cleared into the
ranges with horses, etc. The police asked a
doctor to go out and post-mortem Clarke’s body,
but doc. wanted ,£25 for the work, and the police
offered only lOd. a mile out (£57 10s). The doctor
would not accept, so a police-officer went to the
boiling-down establishment and had a tin can
made, and, in company with two troopers, started
to the place where Clarke had been murdered and
buried. On arrival they dug the body up, cut
the head off, put it into the tin can, and returned
with it to Burketown, where they are going to
hold an inquest on the head. The niggers first
split Clarke’s forehead open with a tomahawk,
and then put a bullet into each temple; the rest
of the body was in no way bruised, and showed no
sign of violence. We aren’t yet quite civilised up
here. —H.H.B. Burketown, N.Q., July 23, 1894
Neuralgia.
Is the first, surest and most reliable sign of nerve
decay; it is a symptom provided by nature to
warn the sufferer of approaching weakness and
distress, though in moat cases the pain is purely
local; the disease is constitutional and local
remedies, such as liniments, oils and menthols, only
give temporary relief and do not do the slightest
permanent good, for they only act by deadening
the sensibility of the affected nerve. The remedy
to be of any lasting benefit must be tissue forming
and nerve strengthening and must have general
invigorating properties ; it must also contain the
material to restore the lost tissue to the impover-
ished nerves, and no remedy except Clements Tonic
possesses these wonderful powers. Clements
Tonic has been used for thousands of cases by
young and old of both sexes for neuralgia, sciatica,
and other nervous complaints, and we have never
heard of a case where it has failed to give quick
and prompt relief. Read of the case of Mrs. T.
Watts, Woonona, N.S.W., who writes on March
23rd, 1893 : —I have suffered severely from indi-
gestion for the last three or four years, I was
unable to sleep, and had a continual languid feel-
ing, loss of appetite, severe pains in the chest and
stomach, and was half of my time unable to per-
form my household duties with any degree of
pleasure. I had the advice and medicine of
several doctors, but without receiving any benefit.
Hearing Clements Tonic so highly spoken of for
the above complaint, I resolved to give it a trial.
I felt myself improving from the very first dose,
and am happy to say before I had finished the
first bottle all disagreeable symptoms had dis-
appeared, and I felt better than I had done for
years ; I have never had a return of the old com-
plaint since. I recommend it on all occasions.—
I remain, yours gratefully, Mrs. T. Watts.
Woonona, N.S.W.

There never has been but one reliable, one
standard, one certain cure for diseased kidneys
and impure blood, and that is, Warner’s Safe
Cure. This truth is acknowledged by the best
doctors, the most renowned scientific men and the
highest authorities in the world. Throughout all
Europe, in every part of America, and even in
Australia and other distant lands, it is admitted
to be the standard, the only remedy for men and
women whose blood has become deranged, for men
who are unaccountably debilitated, and for
women whose lives do not pursue their normal
course. These are admitted truths, which you
doubtless know, and we trust, in reminding you
of them, we are doing you a favour.

The B A. National Ass party is actually
canvassing S. A. with petitions against free educa-
te present N.S.W. Legislative Assembly
has six barristers less on its benches than the pre-
ceding one.
Writing to a Bulletin man, a Canadian
correspondent sums up the Ottawa Conference in
one word ‘ Wind !
Premier Reid : “ Our financial year starts
at the wrong time.” Not so. The wrong time
starts at the financial year.
Wm Strawbridge, the new S. A. Surveyor-
Oeneral, will get .£BOO a year. His predecessor,
G W Goyder, C.M.G., drew £1450.
Reid’s action in calling a meeting of his
nartv before forming a Ministry looks like a step
towards the House-elected Cabinet system.
A. K.O.M.G. -ship is not a political mas-
Where is Dibbs, where is Parkes, and
where will J. B. Patterson and MTlwraith be
shortly ?
Parkes’ friends and enemies should look
up the allusions to Federation made by him m a
speech down South when Melbourne Exhibition
was opened.
The Wentworth (N.S.W.) electorate, re-
nresented by J. P, Abbott, contains 32,000 square
miles. Scotland contains 29,820 sq. m., and
Ireland 32,530. , , ,
Of the 25 Freetraders, members of last
NSW Parliament, who picnio’d G. H. Reid a
few months ago, only 14 have found their way to
the new Assembly.
The doom of N.S.W. Premier Reid : I
am determined that every week in the public press
of this country you shall know as much as i do.
Alas, then, for N.S.W. 1
Drake, M.P., Queensland ; “ Could Dibbs
and Parkes coalesce ? The idea may seem absurd ;
but since the Griffith-MTlwraith coalition every-

The English Ministry will next session
submit a Payment of Members Bill. To Tories of
the old school this means that the end ot tbe
world will come next session.
NSW. Premier Reid celebrates his
accession to office by interchanging Billingsgate
with ex-Premier Dibbs. Apparently Dibbs mind
is Reid’s size, and Reid’s mind is his own size.
Grafton (N.S.W.) Mercury The
interest taken in the general election by women
in some instances was so keen, that they hid their
husbands’ electors’ rights when political opinions
clashed.”
According to the rules of donkey-racing
Foran was the winner at the recent re-election for
the King Division. He came in a decided last,
having scored six votes, the exact number of his
nominators.
For the last ten years Q. expenditure per
head of population has never failed to exceed
revenue. Last year shows the nearest balance for
a long time—revenue per head, .£7 14s. Bd. ; ex-
penditure, £7 15s,
The average daily traffic on Bowen (Q.)
railway is a woman and a bag of potatoes; men
and boys walk. Working expenses last year,
,£2300 ; total receipts, -£451. And interest on
cost to pay as well!
The N.S.W. Railway Commissioners, who
complain that the trains don’t pay, allow sailors
from the Imperial ships to travel in batches to the
lifle-range free of charge. N.S.W. men, being
“ colonials,” have to pay full fares.
A sign of the times is that the Agb is
printing letters signed “Democrat,” and so on,
calling upon the people of Victoria to_ arise and
implore its junior counsel, Alfred Deakm, to come
forward and save his bleeding country.
Yic. Treasurer Carter fell ill of a severe
attack of blue funk after delivering his Budget
speech, and is not expected to recover for several
weeks. He is in his doctor’s hands. that
apothecary may cure the man, but the Treasurer
is incurable.
David Storey, M.L.A., sums up N.S.W.,
Premier Reid’s policy as an amalgam of conserva-
tive, radical and liberal elements, and pro-
mises loyal support provided a due proportion
be observed in legislating along each line. That
is just the saddleback of the ridge Reid is
climbing.
The proposal to raise salaries (Civil Service,
Q.) under £l5O by £lO or £2O, and to tax salaries
over £l5O by a graduated scale, is about the first
sensible step taken by the presen t_ Ministry, Had
it been done last year, the Ministry might have
won a few more votes at Ipswich and Townsville,
both Civil Service strongholds.
Parkes says Premier Reid is a bitter oppo-
nent of Australian Federation, and is out of touch
with the people on measures of urgent social
reform —such as the regulation of the drink-traffic,
for example. “ Urgent social reform ” is good.
Parkes has had the Knapps and other old ladies
on the teetotal string for 30 solid years.
Taking life-averages and political pro-
babilities, J. P. Abbott bids fair to break the
record as N.S.W. Speaker. This Parliament
which, even though there be a change of Govern-
ment, is likely to last out its term, will give him
six years’ currency, thus beating Daniel Cooper,
and coming within a year of Wigram Allen.
Poor little Melbourne Punch should re-
print its cartoons of a few months ago, in which
Patterson used to figure as Hercules choking the
deficit, or a heroic blacksmith straightening the
finances, and so forth. Patterson has proved a
painful subject for the creatures that crawled to
him and invented his precious “ backbone.”
J. Brown-Patterson is said to have pri-
vately spoken of the probability of his final retire-
ment from political life when the present Vic.
Parliament expires. Having been Premier and
obtained a K.C.M.G.-ship, he has reached the
height of his ambition, and does not wish to chance
the ignominy of defeat at Castlemaine next
election.
Bishop Webber (Q.) actually twits J. Bull
with shooting his pauper rubbish into Australia.
Thus: —“You have sent out your unpropertied
emigrants for a quarter of a century to shores,”
etc., etc. To do Mr. Webber justice, he sym-
pathises not with the unpropeitied emigrant, but
with the unpropertied parson who comes out to
live on him.
Glassey has introduced in Q. Parliament a
One-man-one-vote Bill which hasn’t the slightest
chance of passing, as it is only the plural vote
which keeps Boodlewraith in power. Anyhow,
the measure is complicated with weak points, and
singularly omits to provide one of the most vital
points of all—the redistribution of constituencies
as single equal electorates.
Rumoured that when Wilson (Q.) resigns
his post as Minister for Education, the amiable
and polished John Thomas Anuear, of Mary-
borough, will be offered the position. Of whom it
is recorded that as Chairman of Committees he
was accustomed to stertorously ejaculate ; “ Them
as is in favour of this ’ere motion say ‘Hi.’
Them as is agin it say ‘ No.’ ”
As the Women’s Suf-
frage Bill involves an altera-
tion of the Constitution, its
second and third readings
have to pass by an abso-
lute majority of the S. A.
Council. The numbers are
so close that the division on
the measure has been held
over for weeks because of
Labour - member Guthrie’s
absence through illness.

M.’sL. A. are afflicted, came
out strongly during the de-
bate in the Vic. Legislature
on the Woman’s Suffrage
Bill. £ £ Smith lamented
about the horrible scenes
which might arise in the
refreshment-room under cer-

stated. Staughton wanted to
know if a lady mayor should
be her own ohucker-out.
Another M.L.A. told a weird
tale about a Chinese fight in
the bygone days at Clunes,
Frank Madden thought that
if a woman differed from her
husband in politics she would
probably throw the dishes at
him. M'Lellan rose to state
that the country was
“blighted.” Murray was
of opinion that the Catholic
Church was making insidi-
ous encroachments on the
freedom of the country ; and
levers got up and stated that
when he was in Europe he
had seen women carrying
coal up six flights of stairs.
Then the collective wisdom
of the country adjourned
and went home.
Brother Madden, M.P. for Wimmera,
Vic., says the farmers lose 13s. 4d. in the £ because
they sell three bags of wheat for a sov. now, in-
stead of one bag for the same money as heretofore.
The farmers do not sell at a loss now— ergo, they
used to make over 200 per cent, profit. Which is
absurd. Brother Madden said nothing of reduced
wages and cost of production.
Australia annually imports 80,0001bs.
opium, and as the duty under the various Govern-
ments ranges from 20s. to 40s. per lb., the revenue
thus derived is not to be despised. When the
liquor question is disposed of, however, the
crime of indulging in opium will be no doubt
whipped into public attention and a conscience on
the subject be rankly cultivated.
Bart. O’Loghlen told Vic. Assembly that
his “hair stood on end,’’ when he became a member
of the Patterson Ministry, and saw the state of
the Treasury. As O’Loghlen is egg-bald, members
yelled at the announcement; but the statement,
made in an unguarded moment, went to show
something of the real truth about the internal
rottenness to which booming and jobbery have
brought Victoria.
Deakin is blamed for his lack of patriotism
in neglecting to undertake the conduct of Victoria
in her hour of need. Everybody is heartily sick
of Patterson, Shiels is supposed to be impossible.
Berry is extinct, and Deakin is the only hope of
the non-Tory party; but he can t afford to come
to the rescue of his suffering country till he has
divested himself of heavy boom obligations, which
he is fighting pluckily to do.

“No Bill, ’tisn’t that. You see, whin he wor at

there’s hundreds of young fellers turns their

they can copy their lingo from, so they gi vs us
more book laming and less rough stingo. Mudgee s
dun.”
The call of yet another Judge’s son to the
N.S.W. Bar reminds one of fact that in Victoria
during the last 30 years but two Judges have had
sons at the Bar—Hartley Williams and Hickman
Molesworth.
The seven nominated by Dibbs to N.S.W.
Legislative Council and rejected by Gov. Duff
R. J. Black, Ninian Melville, Henry Clarke, M.
C. Oowlishaw, J. P. Garvan, John Meagher (of
Bathurst), and A. Ryrie.
Carter’s abandoned Budget was guilty of
double-truckling to the Melb. Gas Co. crowd. It
proposed to put sixpence a gallon duty on kero-
sene, and remit the 35 per cent, duty on Hinglish
gas-meters. The last proposal (which may yet be
carried) means robbing Victoria to feed the com-
pany’s dividend, inasmuch as there is no possi-
bility of the amount saved being devoted towards
a reduction in the price of gas.

The Victorian Parliament is far too
decorous. It does not represent the torn and dis-
tracted country. The very passions of the time
should find their vent in it. Otherwise there is
danger. The country will shape out for itself
something else if the Parliament be a smug,
ploughed and harrowed field of respectability.
Delegation is the word nowadays. A popular
Assembly must be an absolute mirror.
The difficulties attending Bible-reading in
State schools are exemplified by an item from an
article on English board schools and dogma in the
latest Nineteenth Century. One official com-
plained that he found the scholars impressed with
the belief that Joseph was the father of Christ,
and professed to be shocked by the heretical idea.
He has been asked to show how he would set the
youngsters right, and is now struggling with the
problem.
A GREAT STAGEMANAGER.
(Chief Justice Madden directing a tableaux rehearsal at
Melbourne Alexandra.)
There were 28 Roman Catholics in last
N.S.W. Assembly. In the present Assembly
there are but seven, and none in the Ministry.
Strategically, Premier Reid has been foolish in
this matter. He should have harnessed up a good
Holy Roman in the same team with a howling
Orangeman, after the fashion of his predecessors.
It must be conceded, however, that there is not
one pronounced “ sectarian ” in the Reid Ministry.
Dear Bulletin, —Glassey was elected as
leader of Q. Labour-party unanimously and not
by a majority. There was no other nomination,
and the A.L.F. did nob interfere by nominating
Mat. Reid or anyone else. Mat. is secretary and
whip of the party, and a better one would take
some hunting for. The Q. Labour-party is solid
as ever, and it will take a bigger question than
the choice of a leader to cause a split in it.—
W.H.B.
There was another temporary misunder-
standing in Vic. Assembly the other night, when
the artful Shiels described Mr. Bryan O Loghlen
as the “guerilla of the House.” Bryan had begun
to beat his stummick after the style of the hairy
man of the woods, and his friends were yelling
“ Withdraw !” before Shiels could explain that he
didn’t say “gorilla.” It was a mild repetition of
the “cowered ” “coward” incident which
brought the quivering Patterson to his feet last
session.
Thus Pall Mall Gazette “If such
reciprocity continue, we may yet see a Canadian
regiment mounting Queen’s guard at Buckingham
Palace, and an Australian officer flying his flag
as Port-Admiral at Sheerness.” And (let it be
added) that a spindle-shanked Hindoo may yet
be appointed Governor of the North Australian
colony of Piebald, and a British subject from
Hongkong be nominated generalissimo of the
Australian forces.
The Bulletin is requested to contradict
the statement that on the night of the banquet to
Premier Reid, B. R. Wise was sitting with
Parkes reciting to him Kendall’s sonnet com-
mencing :
I cannot tell what change hath come to you
To vex your splendid hair. I only know
One srrief; the passion left betwixt n. two
Like some forsaken watchfire, burneth low.
’Tis sad to turn and find it dying so,
Without a hope of resurrection.
Vic. Public Works Department has in-
serted some very arbitrary clauses in its conditions
of contract. In future, disputes of every sort ara
to be decided by the Inspector-General, whose
decision is to be absolutely final. Arbitration
has been abolished, and no responsibility is in-
curred by Government for discrepancies between
drawings and specifications. It, of course, re-
main's to be seen how some of these innovations
will stand in a court of law. So far, the only
'result has been an increase of 10 per cent, on
ordinary contract prices.
Somebody has counted up the attendances
of Gillies, the independent Maitland M.P., at
N.S.W. Assembly last session, and having divided
them into his “honorarium,” finds that he drew
£6 ss. for every night he visited the House, and
argues from this that “a monstrous price” is
paid for legislative work. As to the monstrous
price, what about counsel’s-fees and the fees
of several other professions? Is not legislation
as important as any of them ? And mayn t a
member be working harder for Ins constituency
and the country while absent from the House
than while in it?
N.S.W. teetotallers count upon five mem-
bers of the Reid Cabinet going with them against
compensation, if a vote on the subject cun e
reached in the present House. Reid himself, who is
almost a non-drinker, favours compensation,
opposes local option, is a straight-out enemy of
prohibition, and denies that Parliament has the
right to interfere with what a man eats or drinks,
so long as ho does not trespass on the/ rights of his
neighbour. More unlikely things have happened
than that Parkes will goad on the teetotallers to
disrupt the Ministry on this question and will so
make for himself his via ad astra.
A. G-. Taylor is the last man in N.S.W.
who ought to talk about sectarianism in politics.
While he represented Mudgee he always had the
support of the Orange vote guided by His
Majesty King Dan Cassin. A. G. made a big
mistake when he said G. H. Reid had _ had
“magnificent chances,” and “was born with a
silver spoon in his mouth,” while he himself had
had to fight for every privilege he had enjoyed.
No man in N.S. or Australia has had more
faithful friends or more devoted supporters than
the brilliant A. G., and if he was not born with
a silver spoon in his mouth, the spoon was put
there, and only needed care to keep it.
Treasurer Ward has rushed a Bill through
Maoriland Parliament, giving Government
authority to collect in August the Land-tax
usually payable in December, money being
urgently wanted to pay the half-year’s interest on
the Public Debt. This, taken in conjunction
with Ward’s boom budget, intensifies the horrid
suspicion that something is wrong with the
Seddon Government. Seddon’s backbone, like
the oppressive spine of J. B. Patterson, obtrudes
itself at the wrong time, and his stern refusals to
give information, just when he ought to be candid,
helps to intensify the feeling. A big majority is
fast spoiling Premier Dick Seddon.
S.M. Herald :
We have been decided and emphatic in vindicating for
Mr. Reid the position of leader of the Freetrade party,
and the responsibility of forming a Ministry to give effect
to the mandate of the country so clearly expressed at the
general election.
Which, of course, is all pure cheek and gall. The
S.M. Herald “barracked” and intrigued for
Parkes, and printed, as long as it dared, sneaking
little feelers inciting the N.S.W. Freetraders t>.
throw Reid over, When the game was up, and
Reid and the Daily Telegraph had finally pre-
vailed, the Herald has the impudence to declare
that it has been “decided and emphatic. ’ Qut
s’excuse, &c. The Herald was never decided and
emphatic in all its life.
Westralian Assembly Ministerialists nuni"
her 19 ; Opposition, 13—the •• ame proportion as in
last Parliament. But the Opposition is on’y
nominally antagonistic, a leaderless and incoherent
concourse of atoms ; and, thanks to tin po icy of
sop, the Forrest crowd is even stronger than
before. Moran, the independent Labour-member
for Yilgarn (Coolgardie), formerly a teacher in the
R.O. school at Toowoomba, Q., will have much
to do to make up for the loss of De Hamel, leader
of the old Opposition, to whom the highest com-
pliment that can be paid is to say that the
Westralian Tory ring hates him more than any
other man in the country, and that he deserves it.
Altogether there are only 6 new representatives in
the Assembly ; but the new elective Council, with
21 members, contains only 5 of the old nominees,
and the remarkable unanimity with which these
fossils went down wherever they put up is, from a
democratic point of view, the most cheerful thing
about the recent rattling of dry bones.

WOLEEsScHNAPPS
Society
Mrs. Mount Morgan D’Arcy gave the ball
of the season at the Savoy last week (writes Thk
Bulletin’s London correspondent). Such a
galaxy of “ real swells” has rarely assembled even
in the very best houses. They were not the
ordinary “guinea-pig” order of aristocracy, but
the veritable crime de la crime , including the
Westminsters, Radziwills, Stanhopes, Derbys,
Atholes, Ilchesters, and scores of others. The
D’Arcy’s social greatness is due, in a measure, to
the fact that with all his coin D’Arcy does not put
on any “side.” By the way, they say there is a
religious hitch in the reported Marlborough-
D’Arcy alliance.
* * *
Premier Kingston (S.A.), ever since he
has been in office, has been scrupulously careful t°
invite his personal enemy, President Baker, of
S.A. Legislative Council, to all official gatherings
under Government auspices, so that Mr. Baker’s
bad form in shutting him out from the list of
invited guests to the Presidential annual dinner is
the less excusable. In any case, Kingston would
not have gone, so that Baker miuht have safely
concealed the unpolitic evidences of his malice.
Owing to the slight put upon Premier
Kingston, Speaker Coles, instead of feasting at the
presidential dinner, spent the night with two of
his numerous family at Fill is’ circus. Baker is
now believed to deeply regret his latest attempt
to get even on Kingston, for his dinner was a
ghastly failure. Though there were 13 outsiders
present the total company only numbered 30,
several members of the Council being absent.
Among these Chief Secretary Gordon, Labour-
members M‘Gregor and Guthrie, and Addison
made no secret of the fact that they stayed away
because Baker attempted to use his official posi-
tion to gratify his personal spite. The claim that
Baker invited his guests in a private capacity is
very thin. For he had the Parliamentary ban-
quet-hall, and the free use of the Parliamentary
waiters and servants, together with the run of the
official apartments,
# * •
From a private letter : —An illustration of
W.A. conservatism : A friend of mine, en route
to the goldfields, called in at an Albany barber’s
for a shave. As a victim was already being
operated upon, he sat down to await his turn.
Meanwhile, however, a third customer walked in
and took a seat. No. 1 being “finished,” my
friend is making for the chair, when he is stopped
by the barber with : “ Pardon me, sir, you are
not a resident.” Then the barber turned to the
new-comer with : “Now, Mr. Jones ! ”
Maoriland War Cry publishes
a smudge representing a railway-
station with two trains just start-
ing, one for Zion and the other for
the Pit. The train for Zion is full
of Salvationists with poke bonnets
and flat caps and foolish, vacuous
faces ; the other one contains every-
body else. The entire creed of all
the churches is summed up in that

Dear Bulletin, —You mention
the sale of Sunday newspapers has
been prohibited in Canada, as it is
prohibited in Victoria and other
Australian states. You will not, I
am sure, sneer at the remark that
Canada, Victoria, &c., are un-Hing-
lish in their Sabbath legislation.
Isn’t it about time that “Greater
Britain” began to be more English
and less Scotch? Hinglish pre-
judices get rubbed off in Australia, I
fancy, whilst Scotch cant made
powerful by Scotch clannishness and
Caledonian “ blow ” societies—is cast-
ing a shadow over the land. “ Dear
old England ” has at least managed
to keep its Nonconformist canters in
check, which is more than Young
Australia promises to do. Shall I
tell you why Sunday newspapers are
published in N.S.W. ? Because
Parkes, your intermittent ruler for
go many years, wasn’t born North
of or near the Tweed. I think I have
said all this before. But, please, let
me roar it once again. Yours,
English.
The appointment of Arthur Gallon,
Governor Duff’s late Private Secretary, as N.S.W.
official historiographer, affords yet another proof
that your average Australian politician, whenever
he rubs shoulders with anybody even on the out-
skirts of “Swelldom,” becomes immediately
debauched. But for his gubernatorial connection
Gal ton would assuredly never have got the
appointment.
# * #
N.S.W. Chief Justice Barley announced,
in a recent divorce-case, that he will never again
accept the evidence of a private detective unless
it is corroborated. Quite right; but a private
detective is very often a retired official detective,
who while taking State pay, constantly accepted
private commissions, and whose evidence, before
his retirement, was taken over and over again
without corroboration. This fact, in the light of
Mr. Darley’s new dictum, is a solemn thing to
reflect upon. Also, if he is henceforth to be
believed only when supported by corroborative
evidence, the inevitable result will be that each
private “ D ” will keep his own corroborator on
the premises. Unless Mr. Darley keeps an eye
on the man who corroborates the detective things
won’t be much improved.
* * *
The Maoriland authorities have shut down
on the sale of a dreadful American paper—the one
which contains illustrations of the lovely young
woman in her night-dress pounding a burglar
with a poker, and the lovely young wife, generally
in her night-dress, being suspended by her hair
from the window by a brutal husband, and the
midnight marauder who gets in at the window
and scares a lovely young woman in her bed, and
the beautiful bride who is being gored by a cow,
and the like. Thereupon a Maoriland corres-
pondent wants to know why the same authorities
don’t shut down on the studies from the nude in
the Art Journal. Which only goes to show
that, despite free education, there are still people
in existence who don’t know the difference
between a work of art and a picture of a lady in
striped stockings and a night-dress being dragged
round by the hair on account of compromising
letters which have been discovered in her work-
box.
* * *
The Age for 23rd July presents the most
comprehensive picture of Melbourne’s present
wretchedness which has been seen up to date.
On the first page (and in the very first column in the
paper) there are about half-a-column of death
notices, crowded together in the smallest of type,
followed by over half-a-column of “In memoriam”
notices, also in the smallest of type, and aceom
panied by great masses of execrable “poetry.”
There is a “bereavement notice,” too; and the
very last item in the paper consists of three-
quarters of a column of funeral notices. But
there is not one birth or marriage announcement-
in the whole paper ! Apparently, people can’t
afford to be born in Melbourne now.

* * *
An Ipswich (Q.) verdict. The jury was
unable to agree on a case of rape, tried before
Justice Harding. A threat that they would be
locked up till the following Monday brought the
dissenting jurors to a decision, and the foreman
announced “Guilty,” with a recommendation to
mercy. “On what grounds” queried his Honor,
blandly. “Because,” promptly returned a juror,
“ at the time the offence was committed he wasn’t
there!”

That excellent measure, “ The First
Offenders’ Probation Act,” has been greatly
abused in the North. The provisions are fre-
quently extended in directions the most astound-
ing. Surely it was not intended that such
offenders as civil servants of long standing and
handsome emoluments, whose defalcations extend
over a considerable time, should be classed as first
offenders ! Note the case of a certain Brisbane
embezzler, whose sentence of two years was re-
cently suspended under the above-mentkmed Act.
Sydney Municipal Council is thinking over
a new bye-law whereby any person spitting on
the city pavements will be liable to a fine of not
more than =6lO. If this by-law can be extended
a little, so as to cover the fiend who throws orange
and banana-peel on the footpath, and the bloke
who howls in the street, and various other coves,
The Bulletin will approve enthusiastically-
provided it is only enforced. The dismal circum-
stance is that no bye-law which will help to make
the city a little cleaner and more respectable
ever is enforced. One reason for this state of
things is that there are not one-half the neces-
sary police-force, and another is that what force
there is seems to be very tired. Also, the local
“copper” is always being sent out suddenly to
the far west to interfere in shearing strikes and
the like, and then the “push ” riots on Saturday
nights in the busiest thoroughfares, and it is only
after a free fight has lasted 30 minutes, or there-
abouts, that a lonesome officer named Gallagher
looms up on the far horizon.
* # #
Apropos of the unlucky number 13. Fitz-
gerald Brothers had their circus-wagons numbered
while in Sydney last year. After starting out the
axle of No. 13 waggon broke and threw the driver
into a creek, breaking his arm. His successor was
run in for bigamy in the next town. Humphreys,
the lion-enrager, assumed temporary command of
the unlucky waggon, and was horribly bitten by
the lions the third day after. He was followed as
driver by Frank Jones, the clown, who got a tele-
gram next day apprising him of the fact that his
wife had triplets. At this fresh calamity the firm
gave in, and bad the figure 3 painted out, since
which everything has gone all right.
# # *
An old woman named Ryan fell into a hole
30ft. deep near Castlemaine, Vic., and remained
there seven days before she was discovered. When
hauled out, she was anxious for dinner, but other-
wise quite well, thanks. She is a mother-in-law,
of course.
Melbourne Metropolitan Board of Works
has refused to fix a minimum wage of 6s. 6d. for
labourers, “in the interests of the elder men who
would suffer through not being able to get em-
Ghastly bamboozle! No contractor
will employ an old man now where he can get a
young one, and the young ones will work for
“ bare grub and a doss.”

New Sydney Hospital is a veritable palace
in freestone, costing .£147,000. It only holds 250
beds—<£sBs per bed. The public money has been
squandered in stained glass and marble fittings,
and luxurious accommodation for the staff, while
the sick poor of the city, for whose benefit the
charity is ostensibly designed, and who do not
want art statuary, but skilled treatment and good
nursing—are still left unprovided for. But Mr.
DUibs, who some three year ago pointed out that
the hospital was a huge failure most extravagantly
managed, has opened the building with a golden
key, surmounted by a crown—“a beautiful speci-
men of the goldsmith’s art.” There are always
compensations.

# • *
At Newport (U.S.A.), the tramway co.
has commenced to run cheap funerals. The cortege
consists of two cars painted black ; the corpse has
the front one all to itself, and the mourners
occupy the hinder one. The fare for each mourner
is ten cents,, and the corpse is charged a dollar.
A funeral with ten dejected relatives in attendance
thus costs Bs. 4d.
» » •
While a train was recently running from
Townsville to Charters Towers the driver and fire-
man had a disagreement, which wound up with a
prolonged and desperate free fight on the engine,
'the two men made stupendous efforts to throw
each other off, and if they had succeeded, the
locomotive would have gone on by itself, and
either blown itself up or else wound up with a
picturesque smash at the end of the line. As it
happened, however, the combat ended in a draw,
and the two swollen and battered combatants
stopped to report each other at a way-side station,
where the most bloodthirsty-looking of the pair
was suspended and yanked off the engine. Queens-
land is almost the only Australian province where
this kind of humourist flourishes now.

One or two alleged actresses who came
. to Australia per mail steamer (say about 70
years ago) were very lively en voyage. They turned
the ship into a kind of all-round variety show,
with the result that at least a dozen of the
married males, who took part in the entertain-
ments, are threatened by their wives with divorce
or judicial-separation proceedings.

CLARA’S TEMPTATION.
Upon the dusty shelf above
Her golden head it used to lie
Unsought; though Ovid’s Art of Love
Had often caught the maiden’s eye
She thought of it as “ old and dry.”
But after something she had heard
Relating to that volume brown,
Her curiosity was stirred ;
So, putting on a modest frown,
She climbed a chair —and took it down.
She read it through, when none were nigh
To see her “ burning blush of shame ”
The Customs officers, say I,
For Clara’s sin must take the blame—
They gave the book it’s naughty fame.
Melb.
E. F.
DEPRESSION.
Rents down, shops to let,
Wool low, wheat glut;
Gaol holds half our set,
Grog scarce, “ drums ” shut.
Loot—none : orgies end,
Life’s tree’s “ rung ” ;
Banks, God, dividend,
All gone bung.
D.

* * *
That medical sensation in the Dutton
(Melb.) divorce-case recalls memories of a surprise
sprung upon a London jury some years ago. A
doating husband was driven to a divorce-suit by
his wife’s scandalous behaviour in visiting military
officers at their private quarters, where she com-
muned with them under the suspicious circum-
stance of a locked door. It transpired early in
the case that there had not been cohabitation
between the petitioner and respondent. The lady
didn’t become a wife in the marriage service sense
of the word, but the husband probably worshipped
her the more on that account. He never protested
against her plea of delicate health and natural
indisposition to worldly love, until she took to
visiting his male acquaintances as aforesaid. The
circumstantial evidence of “ misconduct ” was
strong enough to divorce any woman, but for the
defence there came a doctor who stated that the
giddy married lady was still—well, as all spinsters
are politely assumed to be. The Judge, however,
ruled that no medical evidence could prevail
against illicit love-making, secret visits, and
locked doors. The inviolate wife was ultimately
found guilty and sentenced to a decree nisi.

# a •
Premier Patterson says
that Australians are “ some-
what lacking in respect for the
dead,” Patterson, who will
be dead some day himself, is
rather hurt over this trait, and
thinks it ought to be remedied,
and he apparently doesn’t con-
sider that the community may
have lost the habit of respect-
ing the dead chiefly for want
of a suitable corpse to prac-
tise on. If the dead were
not respectable when they
were alive, they aren’t worth
respecting when they have
ceased to be alive; if Patterson wants them to
be respected simply because of their decease, he
is merely inculcating the great virtue of Rever-
ence for Decayed Meat. When a man has done
nothing great or good or noteworthy in his life,
it is impossible to respect him after death for any
other reasons save that he is defunct, and that he
would be a horrid-looking object to meet, and
this seems insufficient cause for reverence, Vic-
toria, for instance, doesn’t respect Patterson, and
has no reason to; and if it can’t respect him
because he is Patterson, it will be difficult to do
it later on merely because he used to be Pat-
terson, unless it respects him because he isn’t
Patterson any longer.

Dear Bulletin, —One cannot hurriedly
skim over Jones or Smith now when reading the
papers. Instead of Mr. T. Jonesor John Smythe,
one can always see something about Russell Jones,
T. Russell Jones, B. Russell Jones— in fact, every
Jones that is mentioned in the papers now is a
something Russell Jones. Even Barton has com-
menced to bo Russell Barton. Have you over met
Jones or Smith used as a Christian name ? How
would F. 0. Jones Russell look? Would a Par-
liament of Joneses b« a greater blot upon the
history of N.B.W. than a Parliament of aldermen?
Anyway, who ever heard of a Jones as doing any-
thing brainy ? Never vote for a man who admits
his name is Jones. The many varieties of Atkin-
son one reads of have also become harassing
lately. And what, in the name of plain English,
is Colonna-Close? Is it Hibernian for Jenkins?
We are simple people up here, and we like to be
posted in up-to-date nomenclature. —Yours, F.
(Wellington, N.S.W.).

A Geelong boarding-house keeper was the
quaintestinoidentintheDuttondivorce story. This
highly-respectable female objected, so she deposed,
to Dr. Dutton and his servant, Nellie, remaining
together in the dining-room through the small
hours of the blessed Sabbath morning. She told
him that the Day of Rest had commenced and he
ought to go to bed. Dutton said : “ You can go
to h—l to-night and to church to-morrow.” Sub-
sequently he loaded his pipe three times with the
avowed purpose of' smoking the landlady out, but
although the room got full of tobacco-fumes he
could not drive her forth. Then he blazed away
with bad language until she gave him best and
retreated to her virtuous couch.

THE PICNIC GIRL
Let us strike our lyre to a joyous tune,
For the pic-nic girl will be with ua soon,
With her waving fringe and her blouse well-filled
(For the pic-nic girl is of shapely build),
And a tinge of brown where the amorous sun
Has kissed her cheek so fair ;
But we’re not enraged—though he interfered
With our monopoly there.
With her vermeil lips and her teeth of pearl,
Neat, pretty and plump is the pic-nic girl.
Fast vanishes Pleasure when Summer flies ;
For we’re pessimists all under dull, grey skies.
But the world turns rosy and girls grow nice
When the Summer’s sun strikes the Winter's ice.
While the south winds bite and the south winds screech
We fervently pray with will
For the ecstacies of Mentone beach
Or £he transports of Box Hill
For there never was sweeter music played
Than the “No ! I won’t! ” of the pic-nic maid.
It’s sweet in the evening when round we whirl
With our arm encircling the pic-nic girl;
But our pulse gains pace when we see a chance
To slip unobserved from the mazy dance
And the chaperon’s Argus-eye evade
By a masterpiece of tact;
Then we open our arms to the pic-nic maid
In metaphor and fact.
For a cuddlesome creature from foe to curl
Is the fetching, enrapturing pic-nip girl.
Melbourne^
M. MU

Michael: Only crude writers need to blurt out all the
facts. You mustn’t judge a yarn like a police-court case,

A Fiji compositor had a bit of native
lingo to set up and charged the double rate allowed
for the setting-up of foreign languages. The
manager disallowed the charge and pointed out
that it was the language of the country. The
comp.’s next bill was charged at the double rate,
for, as he explained to the dazed manager, “if
Fijian is the native language English is a foreign
one.” His first charge was promptly allowed.
THE HUMAN HEART.
Heart of man, hath chambers twain.
Wherein slumber Joy and Pain,
In one waken bright-faced Joy
Slumb’ring Pain will ne’er annoy.
Oh! Joy, beware 1 Murmur low,
Lest Sorrow waken and—you go.
ARNOLD B.

UNLIMITED
Means (Ft* Webster’s Dictionary)
“Having No Bounds-”
A Venture in Co-operation.
Now Placed Before the Public Depending
Upon Their Support, which Is Only Expected
If To Their Advantage.
Membership, £1 Is. Per Annum,
MEMBERS can have any business done by
Unlimited, to best advantage and at lowest
rates, and on completion of the business will
receive half the amount earned by Unlimited.
Examples. The National Association of
Queensland paid the Unlimited Agency .£lB 15s.
for procuring a loan of ,£750 for the Association
from the Bank of North Queensland, vide Tele-
graph, 14th June. By payment of £1 Is. to
tin limited they would now receive a refund of
£9 7s. 6d., a net saving of =£B 6s. 6d.
A.B. paid the Unlimited Agency .£5 for pro-
curing a loan of .£lOO. By payment of .£1 Is. to
Unlimited .£2 10s. would have been returned, a
saving of £l9s, more than a year’s subscription.
Every member receives a Policy of Insurance
against accident in the New Zealand Accident In-
surance Company for £5O in the case of death,
and 10s. per week in the case of disablement for
ten weeks, subject to ordinary conditions of the
company.
Every member is entitled to a discount on any
purchase above Is. made from any firm federated
with Unlimited. Such discount will be paid in
cash by Unlimited on the 15th of the month fol-
lowing the month of purchase on presentation of
receipts for payment. All receipts for payment
must be presented to Unlimited by the sth of the
month after their date, and will be returned with
the payment.
Example. —You spend ss. with one of the
Federated Firms on the sth August. Most of the
firms now give you a receipt; if not offered a
receipt, ask for one. On or before sth September
present your August receipts to Unlimited. On
15th you will receive the discount in cash and your
receipts.
Note. —The discount being per cent., if you
spend £42 a year you must get value for your
subscription.
Every member will receive (for cost of postage
only when necessary) a Monthly Register of the
Federated Firms, Such Register will take the
form of a journal useful to every member.
The whole of the books, accounts and securities
will be audited by a professional accountant, and
a certified balance-sheet will be published in the
Journal every six months.
Unlimited is new, and there seems no more
likely way of progression than by means of sug-
gestions from members. Prizes will be offered
every month through the Journal for the best
suggestions in any way connected with the im-
provement of the venture.
I reserve the right to cancel any membership
upon conviction of anything derogatory to the
venture.
Note. —Loan Prize on page 14.
The First Issue of the Journal (5000 copies) free,
on application at the office.
CO-OPERATION IS IRRESISTIBLE,
J. P. de WIKTTOW,
Opposite the Post Office,
