SPORTING NOTIONS.
Tommy Corrigan.—Died, Aug. 13, 1894.
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
You talk of riders on the flat, of nerve and dash
and pace I
Not one in fifty has the nerve to ride a steeple-
chase.
It’s gay enough while horses pull and take their
fences strong,
To rush a flyer to the front and bring the field
along;
But what about the last half-mile, with horses
blown and beat —
When every jump means all you know to keep
him on his feet!
When any slip means sudden death —with wife
and child to keep,
It needs some pluck to draw the whip and flog
him at the leap.
But Corrigan would ride them out by danger un-
dismayed,
He never flinched from fence or wall, he never
was afraid.
With easy seat and nerve of steel, light hand and
cheery face,
He held the rushing horses back and made the
sluggards race,
He gave the shirkers extra heart, he steadied
down the rash,
He rode great, clumsy, boring brutes and chanced
the fatal smash,
He got the rushing Wymlet home that never
jumped at all,
But clambered over every fence and clouted every
wall;
But, ah ! you should have heard the cheers that
shook the members’ stand
Whenever Tommy Corrigan weighed-out to ride
Lone Hand!
They were, indeed, a splendid pair—the great up-
standing horse,
The garnest jockey on his back that ever faced a
course,
Though weight was big and pace was hot and
jumps were stiff and tall,
“You follow Tommy Corrigan” was passed to one
and all.
And every man in Ballarat raised all he could
command
To put on Tommy Corrigan when riding old
Lone Hand.
But now we’ll keep his memory green while horse-
men come and go,
We may not see his like again where silks and
satins glow ;
We’ll drink to him in silence, boys, he’s followed
down the track
Where many a good man went before, but never
one came back.
And let us hope in that far land, where shades of
brave men reign,
That gallant Tommy Corrigan will ride Lone
Hand again.*
B.
*NoTE.-The old tcrey died in Maoriland buta few weeks
before his erstwhile rider was killed.
Backers bad a great day of it at Rosehill on
Saturday, when no less than four favourites “got home.”
Mutiny made an easy win of the Hurdle Race, and to the
surprise of a good many, Phararnond was brought out for
the Rosehill August Handicap, and, backed down to 2 to 1,
won comfortably. The Juvenile Stakes, the first N.S.VV.
two-year-old race of the season, fell to Killiecrankie, a
colt by Lochiel from Constance, the placed youngsters
being Witchery, a filly by Splendor from The Witch ; and
Miss Nora, a young lady by Nordenfeldt from Lady Nora.
Hereward and Ballater started equal favourites for the
Welter Mile, but Mr. W. P. Crick scored a win with Sadur
who started at 10 to 1, at which figure it is said Sweet
William won £lOOO.
• • • •
The Henley Regatta is nowin full swing, writes
The Bulletin’s correspondent by last mail. I was there
on the opening day and certainly found a great falling-off
both as regards attendance and sport. However, it has
been proved that the English amateur scullers, at all
events, can hold their own with the best of the Canadians.
Nothing could have been more decisive than the way in
which Vivian Nickalls beat Wright, the Toronto man,
and as for the American and Canadian champion Ryan,
Guy Nickalls simply rowed him stone blind. Both the
trans-Atlantio men row in characteristic style, like all the
professionals we have had from that side (except, of
course, the incomparable Hanlan—shall we ever look
upon his like again ?). They go off at a tremendous pace,
doing much unnecessary arm-work, and it is only a ques-
tion of keeping them ac it until they “crack,” which
they are bound to do. As for the “ Goblets," after the
way they were beaten in the Diamonds the Canadians,
about whom such wonders were predicted, refused to
throw down the gauntlet to the brothers Nickalls.
* * * *
Most of the English professional plungers have
come to grief lately, and settling is very shy, remarks
Tub Bulletin’s London correspondent. Tom Wilson’s
losses over Ascot ran into £30,000, and another heavy
bettor, who was warned off at the lime of the Charlie
Wood business, has dropped even more than that. Backers
must have lost a lot over Ladas in the Princess of Wales’
Stakes, won by Isinglass. The latter, though he went
dotty in his preliminary, and consequently receded to
100 to 12, carried a heap of money, principally from
Ireland, where anything trained or owned by Machell is
bound to be well supported. Paddy believes in the
“ Captin darlint.”
• * » *
Three sportsmen were charged at Footscray
(Melb.) Court, the other day, with the crime of carrying
firearms on Sunday. They didn’t know’ they were living
in Scotland, and breaking the Sabbath by roaming round
the wilderness after small game, but the penalty in each
case was 40s. The chairman said there was no option for
the Bench ; the fine was iniquitous yet the Law couldn’t
help itself. The Law, however, merely smiles a superior
grin when its administrators curse it.

mark. There is no thick ear, and his aquiline nose has
so far escaped.

Ned Triokett, now a Customs officer at Moama,
N.S. W., lately addressed an evangelical meeting, and told
how the sporting people who fdted and worshipped him
in his championship days afterwards neglected him when
he was destitute in Sydney. The long and melancholy
Ned is now a brand saved from the burning.

Memmott, the Australian billiard champion,
has come back from London with an undoubtedly

of his doings. The awful licking that he got from Peel,
in particular, is explained by the fact that he was all
abroad ” for the first few months after landing in Eogland
-an excuse that would have covered the whole of
Roberts’ tour in Australia. At the present moment,
Memmott, by his own showing, is as good. as anybody
bar Roberts, because he held his own with them in short
Barnes Probably, Peel would still like to give him 7000
in 20 000, for all the money he could put up. However,

formances there make him out to be a 20 per cent batter
flayer than the Memmott we knew last year. It seems
KS he has only returned with a view to going back
•■home” for good. He swears by “home” in general,
and thinks he will be able to knock out a good living as a
professional player and tutor of gilded London johnnies.

The Derformance of Sharland, the English
champioiF 24-hours cyclist, in putting up 460 J miles in
the t.r. is not so much better than Ohr.sp s 401 at Melb
Exhibition last month, when you think over It Xhe
English tracks are as true as a die an<^
Melb man liad to ride for ten hours of the time through
blinding rain, and all the time through a strong wind.
The scheme for limiting Melb. football dubs to
draw only on their own localities for players, which is
now be"ng considered by the V.F.A., ought, if earned to
burst up the Essendon supremacy, and do much good to
the eame in the way of checking professionalism. Out of
tiie 95 available players in the Essendon club, less than
half-a-dozen reside in that district, the remainder com Ing
from all over Melb., and two even travelling from Geelong
and two from Warrnambool (1) every Saturday. The
other clubs are nearly as had. As soon as a small ciud
has a chance of getting hold of a smart local youngster,
the Fatman Football Club steps in and tempts the youth,
either with gold or fame, to leave his home club and throw
in his lot with the crowd that earns the most gate-money
and “barracks.”
| $ t
The one relaxation at the alleged football-match
Melb. v. Williamstown, the other day, was the enthusiasm
of a female barracker for the ’Town, who waited for
umpire McNab at half-time and gave him her te « com-
mandments in the face “fur hvaterlol
the ladv. who was old and wrinkled, went into hysterics
of delight and told her she would have h “ r n " a ' l !’
paypers,” but little McNab sturdily passed on without a
sign While he was returning after the int ® r ' al
delightful old thing called him a coorsid blaygaird, and a
small boy of her party foilowed up McNab and
which resulted in a smacked face for the little vermin.
The delighted onlookers waited in expectation of a big
row for a finish up, while all Ireland from Wilhmnstown
cursed, but four policemen were on guard when McNab
returned, and the row fizzled.

A few days ago bills permitting women to sit
in Maoriland Parliament and to hold public offices
were negatived by large majorities, without even
being paid the compliment of discussion. The
tendency to turn the questions into ridicule was
so strong that some newspapers think it went too
far. But the average man is so constituted that
he must either laugh or shudder at woman in
breeches: and perhaps it is better to laugh.

On a Clerical Clown.
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
Lo, the meek Nazarene made merchandise !
Talmage, his hard face cleft in horrid chasm,
Shames the sweet Christ, and with ear-splitting
spasm
Shrieks Science is a pack of lies, lies, lies !
Soft! while the witlings whelm him with their
cries,
Let us slip out, shun bigotry’s miasm,
And seek the page where grins no vain phan-
tasm,
But Truth shines clear from Darwin’s steadfast
eyes.
So, in a circus, a poor daubed buffoon,
The ape predominant, coarse jest up-piled
Till shrinking Nature sickened in a swoon.
Sudden a saucy wind the tent beguiled
To flatter heaven-free ; O blessed boon!
Then I looked up, and saw the stars, and
smiled.
Sydney.
A.G.S.
For one thing, Treasurer Carter’s 10 per
cent, drag-net would tax uncut paper and take
£3J a week from Age and Argus.
Some tall shearing scores are reported
from Peak Station (M.L.), where 24 men put
through 3925 sheep in 8 hours, just about 163
sheep per man. At Albury, last season, Mitchell
and Scott, the two crack shearers of N.S.W., met
in the same shed, and had a trial of strength. At
the end of the day (8 hours) Mitchell had done
237 and Scott 235 sheep.
The late owner of a Melbourne Cup-winner
is now dry-blowing at Ninety-Mile, Westralia,
but he is still cheerful and tells some good
stories. When his turf-luck turned he found him-
self, one day, with .£16,000 to pay and only
£lO 000 to pay it with. He ultimately had to
get the £6OOO from the Jews on executing in their
favour security over his 16 racehorses, it being
agreed that he should work them for a year on
account of the lenders, who contracted to pay all
training expenses; which deal resulted, at the
end of the year, in the £6OOO indebtedness being
wiped out and the children of Israel owing the
Gentile turfite some hundreds of pounds. I or, while
under the borrower’s control the horses prospered
not—the beggars simply wouldn’t win, and the
Chosen soon got very tired of their bargain.
They were eternally suggesting, without avail,
a compromise: “ Bay us de moniseh, they
would say, “Ant ve vill let you have de horses.
Ve don’t like dese live sdocks. Whats th ®
matter?” inquired the cheerful sport Have i
done anything wrong?” “Oh, no, but our ex-
berienoe mit life-sdocks is nod goot. Vor m-
stdance, ve vonce met a tamned scoundrel nut a
circus and ve lent him £6O for a month on de
securidy of a pyootiful tiger Veil, he didn t pay
de£lBoand ve foreclosed. Then the tamnsooundrel
brought us to de tiger and says he, ‘Now, yoost
youdakehimafay.’ Und den ve found ve had
made a taran fool of ourself. Ve had forgot to
take a mortgage on de cage, and so vecouldnt
touch de brute. The circus man vent afay and
made a fortune. Ve don’t like dose Uve-sdocks.
Everyone is surprised to see how well
ex-Premier Dibbs bears bis sudden reverse of
fortune. But yesterday he might have stood
against the world, and to-day he does stand
against it. There is not a feather ruffled in his
knightly coat; not the reflection of the shadow of
the likeness of the sign of the ghost of a droop to
be seen in his ex-Republioan crest. 1 hough a
shattered political world has fallen in fragments
around him, the ruins strike him fearless —as
Horace beautifully puts it. Would it be treason
to hint that what gives strength to his mind, elas-
ticity to his step, and colour to his complexion, is

“ Tom Collins ” dives in again :—
Dear Bulletin, — I wish our stiff-necked brother, “ Titus
Salt,” would acknowledge himself among the prophets
at once. Instead of substituting emphasis for honesty in
the endeavour to prove points not worth proving. To
dip at random into his evidence: What, after all, is
gained by taking the pious and immortal bloke of
Elizabeth Villiers as a type of continence, and Marl-
borough, whose solitary virtue was fidelity to his wife, as
a type of lasciviousness? The whole battle-argument is
not worth one misrepresentation, and, in any case, the
private character of the man who Fitzgibbons a campaign
matters nothing except he be a Gustavus Adolphus or a
Cromwell, governing with his strong personality more
or less congenial food for powder, and thus creating a
moral esprit du corps. Or private character may matter,
in the other direction, when, through the libidinous dal-
liance of its leader, a fine army becomes disorganised and
finally goes to “unutterable smash” at Flodden. Still,
man doesn’t live by war alone, and immoral people have
many other ways of becoming extinct. One more military
illustration, however, may not be out of place: On the
eve of the projected assault on New Orleans (Jan. 8,
1815) Gen. Pemberton issued the countersign, “ Booty and
Beauty.” Two sergeants’ hooks taken by American scout*
made this sample of English wit known to Gen. Jackson,
and he, in turn, made it known to his men. Apart from
the relative “ moral tone ” of the two armies, here was a
definite issue of morality suddenly superimposed on the
national quarrel. Would this—did this —enhance Ameri-
can prowess, and thus interfere with the survival of the
other party? Our argument hinges largely on the
answer. “ X.S.” admits that his Janizaries are dead ; 1
want him to admit that they are deader than the average
departed. The Huguenots, &o., are not dead, and never
will be while human nature remains susceptible to
noble example. In other words, the former are a
failure, the latter a success. “T.S.” claims that no
son of the Bay State has equalled Juarez in
greatness. True; neither has any equalled Toussaint.
But the almost actionable dishonesty of T.S. suppresses
the fact —easily verified—that Juarez, being a full-blooded
Indian was no more a descendant of the Conquistadors
than of Parkes. And if Miguel Hidalgo and Porfirio
Diaz were better known they would, perhaps, be more
appreciated. The two other Mexican advantages may be
conceded—indeed, the “happiest" people I ever knew,
and those who “ had evolved the least corrupt system of
government,” were the blackfellows on the station
where I was born. How many dwts. of proof to the
ton will that sort of evidence go ?—and it is all much
alike. To conclude. If by reason of the various misfor-
tunes besetting concupiscence, the opposite quality has
tended to survival, we shall be able to notice through
irregular cycles of progress and reaction, a distinct ex-
tension of the latter principle. If, on the other hand,
lewdness—enjoying per se the line of least resistance—
overbears its penalties and runs abreast w ith its opposite
(which is the hypothesis of T.S.) we shall find the moral
tone ” of the world a fixed quantity, or merely fluctuat-
ing above and below a given average, l ow, has there,
or has there not, been a moral advance along the line oi
civilisation, say, since the Middle Ages or since classic
historians and poets innocently unveiled gambols that
seem queer to most of us now ? Or, if advance be still
imperceptible to the jaundiced eye, we can extend the
base of our parallax back to the inception of ethnic mytho-
logy— Hellenic, by preference—and mark the angle sub-
tended from a time when sexual morality was unborn.
Evolution only can account for the existence and develop-
ment of such a principle—and how is evolution accom-
plished if not by selection and survival ?

* • • •
A proposed auction sale of valuable Crown allot-
ments at South Melbourne was knocked on the head by a
timely article in the Age. The reclaimed swamp territory
in question is within half-a-mile, or so, of Melbourne
G.P.0., a id the upset price was fixed at £3 per foot.
Minister M In tyre has forbidden any farther dissipation
of the people’s lands in this particular district “ until he
has inspected them." Heaven grant that Mac may never
inspect ’em !
• • • •
The head of a big insolvent Melbourne firm,
asked in the local Insolvency Court why he con-
tinued to trade after he knew the firm’s position to be
hopeless, said “because he thought the Bank of N.S.W.
and the consignors would accept a composition." The
longer the firm was allowed to trade at a loss the smaller
the composition would grow, of course. But here comes
in the stock Smellbourne excuse—“ they all do it."
And they all laugh a cheerful laugh when it is done.
• * • •
The new N.S.W. Ministry have already been
inundated with letters and petitions from the friends of
building-society directors and managers who are in gaol,
asking for their release.
• • • •
The powers granted by Act of Parliament to
Hillgrove and Armidale Water Power Electric Co. (a
miniature Australasian Rights Purchase Co.) expire on
10th prox., and the shareholders are in a flurry lest the
work should not be completed by that date, in which case
they lose at least a cool £20,000 and large prospective
divs.
* • • •
The Vic. banks annually handle gold to the
value of £3,600,000, and the following shows how they
share the trade: Union, £680,000; City (Ballarat),
£610,000 ; Commercial, £632,000 ; Victoria, £400,000 ;
Australasia, £336,000; National, £281,000. The others
cut up the balance.
• • * *
Dear Wild Cat, —You suppose that £55 of un-
authorised silver shillings, lately rescued in Melbourne,
were made in Germany. The assay of the coins shows
they were probably made from Mexican dollars, so the
odds are on the counterfeiter hailing from the east where
the Mexican dollars are current. The forgeries are not
good. The bust on the obverse is too like the Great
Original to be like the ideal head on the properer coin.
The lettering is clumsy and the diameter too big, so that
in a roll of good coins the counterfeit would be readily
detected by the touch.—Yours, Fldffj.
* • • •
At Melb. Tram Co.’s meeting a 5 per cent,
div. was declared, absorbing £24,000 out of the
£42,056 available—of which £22,333 was brought for-
ward from last year. £18,056 was carried forward.
The directors (what humourists!) whined bitterly
about the competition of railways and cabs, but
congratulated the staff on taking their 45 per cent,
salary reductions lying down. They were especially
slobbersome over chairman Clapp. “If Mr. Clapp asked
for £3000,” said one director, “ the co. would have to
go down on its knees to him, lor it couldn’t get on with-
out him.” A shareholder inquired as to the rates of pay
among the tram-men, and was glibly informed that
“ Gripmen get 40s. arid conductors 38». a week. But the
second-class men get hardly as much." “Hardly as
much " means anything from 15s. to 255., while, as three-
quarters of the men are second-class, the chairman might
have truthfully observed—“ They get about 15s. a week,
but the first-class men get hardly as little.” Such are
the ways of the private corporation with a monopoly
secured by Act of Parliament.

The Stock Exchange of Melb. granted 6. D.
Mendel) the use of their hall for a lecture on the Budget,
principally dealing with retrenchment. “ Bravo ” Billy
Jones made a sputtering speech condemning the com-
mittee for letting the hall for a political purpose, and
pointing out “it might offend Government." The com-
mittee agreed and G.D. had to shift his show to another
hall. Courageous men these share-jobbers 1
* • • •
The crusade in favour of electrically lighting
Adelaide has not yet resulted in the abolition of street
gas-lamps but has compelled the local co. to reduce by 17
percent, the tender for lighting, and has won a conces-
sion from 6/8 to 6/3 per 1000 for ordinary consumers, or
to 5/10 for the burners of big quantities.
• • • •
Twenty-two per cent, of Queensland revenue
goes to pay interest.
• • • •
A "Discount Coupon Trust” scheme is being
Introduced vigorously to the notice of Melbourne trades-
men. The Trust proposes to sell 50s. worth of coupons to
traders for £3; the traders issuing them to customers on
the form of, say, 2J per cent, bonuses on cash pur-
chases. A customer having acquired £l-worth of coupons
can obtain a guinea in exchange at the office of the Trust,
thus the shopkeeper is spared any troublesome financial
settlements in the matter. The scheme seems somewhat
fanciful, but it may attract women for that reason, and
the Trust, never holding much money in its possession,
would have no overpowering temptation to bolt. It is
said to work successfully in Manchester, where a lot of
cash trade has taken the place of long credit business.
• • • •
Nobody seems to know how the Commercial
editor of the Age took his paper over to bi-metallism.
The S.M. Herald took the lead this way.

• • • •
The Hobart whaleship, Waterwitch, has just
completed her 15 months’ articles, during which time
she has secured 160 tuns of oil—all in the vicinity of the
S.W. coast of Tasmania. The largest whale yielded 11,
the smallest 7 tuns. The result of the voyage is con-
sidered splendid, as even in the best days 80 or 90 tuns
for 18 months’ articles filled the hearts of whalers with
jubilation. The new Hobart whaler, Helen, is frequently
sighted “ trying out," and should drop in at the Derwent
shortly a full ship.
• • • •
A big racing-man and a pious friend of his have
quarrelled on the Stock Exchange over the North Duke
deal because, it is said, the latter got out between 30s.
and 355., and the wily owner of horses got left at 14s. to
15s. There is much lamenting in the ducal clique over
the alleged tergiversation of the collection-snatcher.
♦ ♦ ♦ ♦
Treasurer Carter’s Budget speech contained the
threat that Victoria might insist on coining her own
shillings and sixpences. Empty as is the threat,
it contains a gleam of'sense, but the easy and accessible
manner of appropriating the advantages of domestic
currency by a State bank is burked by Carter, as by every
bolsterer of the Fat-man-bank. A State bank could issue-
half-sovereign, crown and half-crown notes, that would be
at once a public convenience and a State advantage.
With a group of provinces in the throes of
depression, and their a tendant Treasurers in the diffi-
culties of depression finance, it is wonderful that the
obvious idea of o.ie big Australasian State Bank has
never entered by chance into the aggregation of their
puzzledom. There might be cavilling at half-a-dozen
State Banks in Australia, but there is a large
certainty that a State Bank, backed up by the group,
would have no enemies but Fatman. It would have an
issue of State notes of several denominations that would be
current in every province. A trip across the provincial
boundaries would not discount such State notes, as
happens to the bank notes now. Such notes would mean
an assured and convenient currency to the public, and to
the bank a loan of at least £3,000,000 without interest.
The beauty of the thing is that there is no need to wait
on Federation and the log-rollers. The Australasian State
Bank will be a federation in itself.
• • * *
The N.Z. Loan and Mercantile Agency Co.
sold, in 1893, wool to the amount of 220,614 bales, being
the top record for the year. Of this amount no less than
127,865 bales were sold in Australia. During the last ten
years the co.’s wool business has increased 78 per cent.,
and the trade in other lines— frozen meat, tallow, skins,
&o.—has grown in proportion.
• * • *
The Equity Trustees, Executors, and Agency
00., of Melb., balance-sheet for the half-year shows a
profit of £1377 Bs. lid., which, save £23 for depreciation
of furniture, is to be carried forward. The past half-year
has been more successful than the previous, although no
dividend is declared.
• • • *
London cablegram “ Mr. Herbert Arthur
Trower, of Throgmorton Avenue, City, formerly of Sydney,
was examined in Court yesterday. He stated that he
purchased the Wentworth Goldfield Estate, Lucknow,
N.S. Wales, for £60,000, and floated it in London for
£500,000. The public subscribed £32,000, The cost of
promoting the company was £20,000 in cash and 120,000
paid-up shares." Thb Bulletin hopes that there is some
mistake about these statements which, however, are not
surprising; for even more glaring transactions than that
imputed have been carried through in London. What
chance can Australian mining possibly have under such
circumstances ?

The Mt. Drysdale mine, N.S.W., has crushed
40 tons for about IOOOoz. smelted gold carrying a propor-
tion of silver. There are a lot of good-looking shows in
that district, but they are not being mined—they are
only being gouged and pig-rooted and spoilt. You can’t
advantageously tear gold up by the roots.
• • • •
Dear Bulletin,—The Mt. Allen G.M. Co.
appears to be worked in a very peculiar fashion. Seem-
ingly, no effort is made to utilize the water in the main
shaft (now standing about 30ft. deep—it is stated that the
flow of water equals 800 gal. hourly.) Why is not adequate
pumping machinery erected and the shaft deepened to
the depth first decided on ? Again, is it a fact that the
poorest stuff is being crushed whenever the battery is
going, whilst there is any amount of loz. to IJoz. stone in
sight? The vulgar have a theory concerning these
things. Disgusted.

Dear Bulletin,-The warden at Clermont (Q.)
has a truly Solomonic fashion of doing justice. At Deep
Creek one party drove into another party’s ground and
took a load of dirt. A complaint was made, and the
warden came to investigate. Presently he came to the
party who claimed for the loss of the dirt, and said: “I’ve
settled the dispute. The trespassers say they are very
sorry and won’t do it again." “ But what about our load
of dirt—will they pay for it?—will they return it?” “I
can’t re-open the matter; the dispute is settled." And
off he goes.—Yours, Miner. And now for the other side
of the story 1
♦ ♦ • t
One Paul has jumped a hat-full of the best
Wyalong claims on the ground of non-compliance with
the regulation that holders must apply for survey within
three months of registration. The jump won’t pay any
dividend. Some of the richest scone yet found on the
field has been struck in Corry’s claim, though the reef, as
usual, is small. Meanwhile, the special Wyalong Provi-
dence is working 12-hour shifts, taking it in turns with
the ordinary artisan to sort out the petitions of the crowd
who want the Post-office at one end of the field from the
prayers of the rascals who want it at the other end.
* • * *
Premier Forrest says that the railway to Oool-
gardie will probably be laid down within eight months.
• * • •
Dunn, of Hillgrove—they now call him Mister
Dunn—who found the golden Wealth of Nations reef in
Westralia, is, Thb Bulletin believes, identical with the
prospector of the famous Bear Hill mine, which was so
much to the fore in the silver boom. He seems to have
found other good mines in Westralia before making his
marvellous “punch,” the value of which latter he esti-
mates at about a million.
* • • •
A Murchison correspondent to The Bulletin,
under date 20th July ; “ About 200 left the Murchison, a
lew weeks ago, for Lake Carey, far away to the south-east.
As none have returned, up to date, the inference is that
they are either doing well or have succumbed to want of
“tucker." Provisions must be terribly scarce, from all
accounts, but I understand that “ bungarrows’ ’’ and
lizards’ eggs are plentiful. On eastern goldfields, when a
miner sinks “ pot-holes ” all over his claim, it is described
as “pig-rooting." In W.A. it is spoken of as •• bungar-
rowing.” The “ bungarrow," or “ bung-eye,” is a species
of large “goanner," which burrows like a wombat. I
have seen him 6ft. long. Their bite is harmless, but
unpleasant.
* * • *
Ooolgardie Mines, re the Londonderry jumping-
cases “The lying done was almost ghastly in its crude
enormity. If a building could blush, our Court-house
would be dyed crimson in utter contempt for the depravity
of some of the human race.”
• • • •
The agent for the Londonderry holders, against
whom an information for perjury was laid in re the
jumping-cases, testified that Mining Registrar Oakes pro-
mised him exemption on June 14, and produced a diary
in which the conversation was written down on that date.
Then the Registrar’s clerk went into the box, and swore
that he left the office June 11, and on June 13, at noon,
quitted Ooolgardie for England, via Perth. Awkward!
wasn’t it? However, the Bench decided that a mistake
had been made and dismissed the case.
♦ ♦ * «
Huxley, one of the Londonderry men, says that
the party which originally went to Westralia consisted of
twelve. At Ooolgardie they divided, the Londonderry
crowd making for Lake Carey, and the rest for the Mur-
chison. A month before the great find Huxley and his
mates, themselves stone-broke, got word from their Mur-
chison friends that they had met not a vestige of good
fortune and were shortly returning. Then the London-
derry was found. A while after the lucky diggers heard
that their friends had found a rich reef near the
Murchison. The finds were within three day* of each
other.
• * * •
“Rioh finds in Westralia” grows monotonous.
The whole province should be pegged out, taking the
solid gold for granted, and an urgent announcement sent
along when anybody strikes a barren patch.
The Londonderry has dropped out of the running
since lucky prospector Dunn found the Wealth of Nations!
Not a word this week of Big Ben and the marvellous
riches of the little old log cabin on the claim. The
owners, on dit, will stick to their ground and dolly out the
price of a A 1 crushing plant. Then the world may stand
back open-mouthed. That, anyhow, is the alleged pro-
gramme.
Dunn’s big find is “ about 15 miles N. W. of the
45-mile, ’’ which somewhat indefinite statement was yet
tufficiently precise to enable several hundred prospectors
o smell Dunn’s brother out as he sat watching the pile
by night. An effort was made to prevent them working
the ground up to 20ft. of the reef—which is customary,
but not necessarily lawful. But there are few things so
convincing as a mob of dry-blowers with a fine old prece-
dent behind them ; and Dunn and Co. judiciously caved.
Lots of rich Westralian leases have been so treated. The
writer knows of one lease off which quite 2000 oz. alluvial
was taken right under the nose of the lessee. The custom
is a good one, and prevents the country being “hogged ”
by men who happen to have enough money to shepherd
big areas.
The orthodox Westralian crowd have the laugh
of the t’other-siders this time, for the Dunn syndicate
includes the Premier Forrest’s inevitable little brother,
Ahck; also, the local Minister for Lands; and F. Monger,
M.L.A., and others.
Dunn says the reef, apparently a true fissure,
stands 3ft. above the surface, and is from sft. to 14ft.
wide. The syndicate lease is 38 acres, but Dunn pegged
out a 9a. for himself in addition, which is already sold
to an Adelaide syndicate. If the Wealth of Nation fails,
there is 2000 sq. miles gold-bearing country to fall back
upon.
Two of the unwelcome dry-blowers, who cleaiu d
out the ground near the’Wealth of Nations reef, picne l
up two specimens holding 2900 z. altogether. The .yu
dicate oan spare them.
* * * *
“Dalziell’s telegram about six men getting
X000oz ; at some place south of Ooolgardie fell ver\ flat
and didn’t move shares a penny upward,” writes Tub
Bulletin London correspondent. London is hard to
move, but the Londonderry specimens blasted out of the
solid reef will shift it. The Bulletin predicts.
* * » *
A few of the really good di vidend-lookiny reefs
of the Ooolgardie field;—M'Auliffe’s reef and Meiklejohn’s
(White Feather), Hicks’ reef (Ninety Mile), New Victoria
and the Northern lease (near Mount Burgess), Golden Bar
and Maopherson’s (Ooolgardie), the Premier at the 26-
mile.
• • ♦ *
Some of the best mines of the Ooolgardie gold-
field the public hear the least about. Until it was bought
by Monger and Co., of York, Hicks’ of the Ninety-Mile
was comparatively unheard of. And very little would
have been had the owner continued in possession. Hicks
and his two boys have dollied out IOOOoz. from a shaft
80ft. deep, and an open cut 20ft. long by the side thereof.
There is over 3ft. solid stone, and even the impoverished
heap at grass will run 4oz. or soz. Hicks made his £4OOO
in six months by means of a hand-dolly, not by news-
paper paragraphs such as the public have had concerning
a lot of other local mines.
* • • *
Block 20, until it was floated and named the
Golden Bar, was unknown outside Ooolgardie, yet the
owners for 12 months have been raising 3oz. stone from a
shaft where the reef was from Bft. to 10ft thick. In the
immediate vicinity are many well-boomed mines of little
or no practical value.
* • • •
The Westralian Act provides for “continuous
working of leases hence under Warden Finnerty’s inter-
pretation- Mr. Finnerty is nothing if not literal—a lease
is forfeitable by reason of one day’s idleness, and if ons
day, why not one hour, or one minute ? The Act is an
absurdity.
• • • *
On many of the Ooolgardie mines more gold oan
be seen in the camp in 10 minutes than couldjbe got out
of the reef by dynamite in ten weeks.
* * • •
If true that the Londonderry marvellously rich
shoot underlies rapidly west, young Carter, one of the
original owners, who sold out of the parent mine for
£3OOO, has another big prospective show in his western
lease.
• • • •
A lot too much has been made of the value of
an enormous conglomerate deposit in the vicinity of
Marble Bar, Westralia. It outlived one or two con-
demnations by reason of the persistency with which it
was boomed by local hummers. At last it has been
effectually damned by a big mining man. If this does
not kill the mine someone should be hired to kill the men
who boom it. To go from Sydney to Marble Bar to see
a “ wild cat ” is a grim joke which costs hundreds of
pounds.
• ♦ * •
When the writer left Ooolgardie the Ninety
Mile was without even postal communication. It was
over a year old, supported a great number of miners,
teamsters, and others, yet every man had to travel Into
Ooolgardie to post a letter or make application for a
lease. In the latter case he had to choose between staying
there or making another trip to ascertain if any objection
had been lodged within SO days.
• * • *
On the road to the Ninety Mile you pass the
Twenty-five Mile (the locality of the Premier); Premier
Extended, Eureka, Golden Thistle, and other working
mines; the Forty-five Mile (the locality of Cashman
Carnage, and other mines). Yet all places, including
Siberia, had no postal communication. Sometimes chance
teams are availed of. but often letters have remained
three months at the Coolgardie post-office.
• • * *
There is a P.O. at Hannan’s hut, none at the
Four Mile, the Six Mile or Seven, at the Lake, the looali-
ties of the Ivanhoe, Great Boulder, Lake View, Crcesus
Star of the East, and many other mines. Every Saturday
afternoon hundreds of men walk many miles into
Hannan’s for letters and newspapers.
• • • «
Concealment is the distinguishing characteristic
of Coolgardie gold-mining. Bayley concealed the Reward
find for five months. Not his fault it was not concealed
for a year. Londonderry ditto, and so it will continue as
long as the W.A. mining laws, which are the most defec-
tive and unworkable in the world, remain unaltered.
• * * •
The writer, who recently spent three months in
travelling over the Coolgardie goldfields, was never asked
for food or money, from the day he landed in W.A. to the
day he left. Within an hour of arriving in Melbourne hs
was asked several times. There is no starvation but many
hardships; more human and animal cruelty at Ooolgardie
than in any other part of the Southern hemisphere.
• • * *
If 6in. of rain should happen to fall at Oool-
gardie, in six days, or, better still, six hours, 10 per cent,
of wages-miners would not so remain. There would be a
general clearing-out to work alluvial. Many puddling-
machines, Chinese and other cradles, sluioe-boxes, and
every other means of water-treatment, are lying in wait.
* • • •
M'Auliffe’s, until floated, was one of the quiet
mines of White Feather. It is a big body of stone, 4ft. in
the bottom of a shaft 100 ft. deep; traceable for half-a-
mile, and containing a run of gold proved in M'Auliffe’s
lease a considerable distance; at grass, 800 tons, worth at
least 3oz.
* * • •
Under the practically unworkable mining regu-
lations of W. A., a miner’s right issued on one goldfield is
not applicable to another, unless it is endorsed by the
Warden. Ignorance of this anomaly will yet cause serious
trouble.
• * * *
The floated Coolgardie duffer is persistently
boomed, while the rich, privately-owned mine dollies out
gold in silence. Why don’t some of those boomed Cool-
gardie mines, of which the public hears periodically
“Another rich shoot of ore,” “Splendid specimens,”
&0., cart some of the rich ore to Southern Cross, where
there are several almost idle batteries? There are hun-
dreds of teams on the road, and they all go back empty
Back-loading can be got for £7 or £8 a ton. The cruel
truth is, they are not rich enough. Some day some of
the Coolgardie liars, In an unguarded moment, will speak
the truth, and the shook will kill them. Some consola-
tion in that.
* * * *
The last seven weeks’ work at No. 8 North
Golden Gate, Croydon (Q.), has given a profit of £OOO to
shareholders. During the last nine months the
mine has divided £3OBB.
• * • *
True Blue Block, Oroydon (Q.), has been crush-
ing for (he last six mouths on 2oz. stone. The dirt now
seems working up, and next returns are expected to aver-
age over Soc.
The introduction of cyanide to Oroydon (Q.) U
likely to force attention to the big, but low grade, lodes ol
that field.
* • ♦ •
Bendigo is anticipating a bad time —growing
tired of this monotony of ealls. The Vic. Govt, has been
approached. One proposal is to drive one or two cross*
cuts from one line of reef to the other to test the inter-
vening country. This is not generally approved because
of vested interests; the next and better idea is to see it
one or two of the well-known lines of reef could be ex-
tended as has already been done in several instances,
notably the Red, White and Blue. The work will pro-
bably be done by local co-operation and State aid.

lii May, 1870, Ellis Bros., of Malvern Downs
(Q;). graziers, became insolvent, J. R. Lomax, ol Warrah,
being a creditor—£lo2l 3s. 7d., for which he proved.
The other day he got the amount, with 8 per cent, added,
£1764 165., less £242 costs. Several persons sold out their
claims for next to nothing, but Mr. Lomax was “ in the
know ” and held on.
* * * •
Editor Bulletin, —He the importation of cheap
whisky. Nobody in Australia seems ever to have thought
of turning the enormous quantity of surplus and nearly
unsaleable maize in N.S.W. and Q. into malt lor brewing
and distillation. Maize-malt is better and stronger than
barley malt. The importation of malt to Australia is an
absurd waste of money.—R.
* • • «
The Chinese are slowly but surely getting a big
hold of the Sydney grocery-trade. Already they have
about half-a-dozen well-patronised shops, which are in
Oeorge-street, between the Railway Station and Newtown
Road. Merchants prefer dealing with the Mongolian, as
he never asks lor credit.

• * • •
Labouchere points out that there is room for a
new insuranoe-eo. with brains and a smart doctor to
insure second-class lives on its own terms, and to give
annuities on any class of life. But any well-managed co.
ought to be able to offer any man either insurance or an
annuity. The worse the life, the cheaper the annuity;
the better the life, the cheaper the insurance. Lots of
men who decline to pay a stiff loading would back their
lives for a cheap annuity; and the first 00. which adapts
itself to circumstances and drops cast-iron methods, has
every chance of a big profit, if its doctors know their
business.
* ♦ * •
A new parlour pastime has been introduced to
the Stock Exchange Club, Melbourne. At lunch, when-
ever Peacock, M.L.A., whose laugh is that of a ghoul
“far, far down in Sheol,” indulges in a little light cacchi-
nation all the members knock on their tables to drown
Peacock’s thunder. If he’d only add a short, dry, hack-
ing cough to his laugh the Peacock would empty a
M‘Neill audience out of the Exhibition by merely smiling
—and that’s a big contract.

baby. “ We’re Ooolgardie, you know ;no flies on Cool-
gardie." Astonishing how gold breeds snobbery.

“Don’t you prefer grinding-mills to stampers
for ore-orushing ? ” said a machinery-agent to an Ameri-
can mining man recently. “No, 1 don’t,” was the reply.
“ Once I did, but after I had ground two or three mines
out of existence by grinding-mills I went back to stampers
and have remained there ever since.”

A lesson in co-operation. The Bendigo Co-
operative Bakery, floated by the local Labour Council on
a capital of £420, has made in three years profits to the
amount of £750. A new bakery has been built at a cost
of £6OO, and it it likely a butchery will soon be started.

The steam-stamp is coming in America. On
one big-copper mine, 4000 tons a day are crushed at the
rata of 400 tons per stamp.

the Kapai-Vermont and Great Mercury mines, M.L., where
he has done much better work than the cyanide people
themselves.
Wool and Produce Reports.
M'Burnet, Wallis & Co. report.—Wool’sales were held
this week, and prices all round were fully equal to last
quotations. A lew lots of Queensland wool were offered
which may be described as new clip, and passed into
buyers’ hands at prices which should prove satisfactory
to owners. In the Far West shearing operations seem to
be progressing without interruption, and the cry raised
by hungry agitators about a pending shearers’ strike has
ended in the proverbial smoke. The lapse of a few weeks
will see the new clip on the market and already a few
buyers have returned from abroad and are in full readiness
to operate as soon as the new clip is placed before them.
We cannot help again giving our opinion that owners who
sell in Sydney during the ensuing season will find values
■lightly above rates ruling last season, and we are certain
those who sold in London last year will greatly improve
their nett proceeds by selling in our local market this
time.
Sydney, August 11,1894.
Nbw Zealand Loan and Mercantile Agency Co., Ltd.,
report: Wool.—A buoyant tone has prevailed in the local
market during the past few weeks, and advices, per
mail, from the principal industrial centres ol Europe,
have also latterly been such as to afford promise of some
further improvement in wool vsiues in the near future.
That better prices than those current during the past
year may be forthcoming for the clip which is now
arriving is the earnest wish ol everybody in the trade,
and certainly with good, well-skirted and classed
Biverina wools, fetching only 7J per lb. in London,
as was the case at the July sales, equal to, say,
per lb. in Sydney, there is much room for an
upward movement. To what extent the tendency
towards greater firmness may be checked by the news
{ust to hand from the United States remains to be seen.
Jnfortunately this news is distinctly bad from the pro-
ducers’ point of view and would seem to indicate that the
Tariff Reform Bill, which lor many months past has been
under discussion in the United Slates Legislature, is
likely to be finally defeated, and this, too, after being
passed by both the Lower and Upper Houses. The Con-
ference Committee, to which the adjustment of certain
clauses of the measure was referred, could not agree on
some points in connection with the duties on sugar, and
it now appears uncertain what course will be adopted.
In Adelaide General Post Office last week,
at a busy time of the day, there was a rattling
stand-up fight between a lawyer and aM.P, The
latter was victorious after a lively skirmish. There
were 300 spectators.
The man-flayer at Darlinghurst prison had
another victim last week—a chronic lunatic—but
the _ daily press, which used to gloat over such
exhibitions, has carefully refrained from men-
tioning the scourging.
When Premier Reid was returned again
last week the announcement of the poll was re-
ceived with cheers. Reid made a speech, and was
greeted with cheers, and then he called for cheers
for Freetrade, which were given heartily. Next
he called for cheers for the returning officer and
his assistant, and they were also given ; and the
returning officer called for cheers for the Queen.
Finally Premier Reid drove away in a cab, and the
crowd gave cheers for the) cab, and three special
cheers for the horse, and one cheer thrown in for
the cabman. Then the meeting dispersed amid
oheers. By the way, the S.M. Herald has com-
menced the war against the new Premier by in-
sisting that he must proceed to take off the semi-
protective duties at ouoe.

Thus Far.
(With apologies to Mr. Percy F. S. Spence.)
Deep gorges upsending hob vapours to skies,
Emblazed with the sun’s scorching blood,
A stream-bed hard-crusted and wide-gaping lies
Athirst for the flush of the flood.
Rough ridges, half-crimsoned with splintering clay,
Trees smitten by swift glares of light ;
No sound but the hiss of the adder by day,
No cry but the curlew’s by night.
Parched plains out beyond where the yellow blades
crack,
By the bounds of the uttermost run—
A wayfarer sinks by the side of the track,
’Neath the stroke of the swords of the sun.
JULIAN SAKE.
In every criminal case heard at Darling-
hurst all witnesses are ordered out of court till
called onto give their evidence. Also, previous
convictions against a prisoner are concealed from
the jurymen who are tryinghim till they havefound
their verdict. But if the accused has to be tried
on two or three more charges the whole panel of
juries are allowed to remain in court and listen to
the first trial and see if he is convicted !
Gall and wormwood to the British must
be the recent decision of the Transvaal Govern-
ment, compelling British residents in the Republic
to serve in the militia, for the British developed
and made the country what it is to-day. Twenty-
five years ago the greenbacks issued by the Trans-
vaal authorities, with a face-value of <£l, were
selling at 2s. cash, and the province was going
downhill fast. Then came the clamour for British
rule, which, when attained, lasted for but five
years. And now to-day, only 13 years or so after
throwing off the British yoke, the Boers are boss-
ing their erstwhile masters with a high hand, and
the British lion refuses to roar. Gall!
“ Why,” asked an intelligent S.M. Herald
man lately, “ is the Herald called a Conservative
or religious organ ? Go over our file for the past
six months and you will find leaders advocating
or approving of smoking, drinking, card-playing,
horseracing, and betting. We urge the opening
of public-houses for at least a portion of Sunday,
and favour all decent forms of Sunday amusement
and entertainment. We support a land-tax, an
income tax, and payment of members. In our
reports of public speeches we are guided altogether
by the matter —its importance and usefulness to
the general public ; and who can say we have not
treated the Labour-party fairly? We oppose any
interference of the Churches with State functions.
Why are we called a Conservative or religious
organ ? ”
Thus a correspondent to a daily paper :
“On his release the convicted and sentenced first-
offender finds his one error has made him a
pariah. No matter how good his future life, there
is no chance for him. It is difficult for a felon to
obtain employment. A felon (at 18) cannot
become a member of Parliament at 50, even though
in the intervening years he has led an honour-
able life. A felon cannot even become a solicitor,
but a solicitor may misappropriate his clients’
money, and is suspended until he pays it back,
when he is allowed again to practise, and having
made restitution, the world (as it should do)
forgets. So long as a man’s first offence shall
make him a lawful outcast for life, we must
expect to breed criminals.”

while to peruse Herr Rassmussen’s advt. on page
18 of this issue.
Gov. McGregor is again to the front with
a report on advancement in New Guinea. Firstly,
the Governor rejoices over the progress made by
Christian missionaries in Papua. Secondly, he
draws attention to the boundless fertility of the
island, its suitability as a field for the culture of
cane (which is indigenous), coffee, tea and vanilla
—its wealth of pearl-shell and its probable rich-
ness in minerals. Thirdly, the Governor draws
attention to the spread of law and order, and
invites capital to step over and invest, asserting
that in 20 or 30 years New Guinea will export
more produce than the whole of the South Sea
Islands. ’Twas ever thus—first the missionary,
then the military Governor with law and order,
then the exploiting.capitalist, and lastly a civilised
nigger crawling over the bones of his compatriots
into a civilised gave.
All the rage in Paris. Latest Parisian
preventative posted in sealed letter, 1/2 stamps.
National Drug Co., Russell-st., Melbourne.
Archibald Forbes thinks that in the next
great war the wounded will be left pretty much
to take their chance. Owing to Maxim guns,
magazine rifles, and smokeless powder, a battle
will be a sudden and frightful convulsion at long
range, and the damage done under favourable
circumstances will be tremendous. But when the
defeated army has retired it won’t be harried in
its retreat by cavalry after the old style, because no
cavalry dare come to close quarters with the new
weapons, therefore the force which is beaten will
often be only a shade less efficient than the one
which.is victorious. And if the victorious army
hampers itself with multitudes of wounded, its
enemy of yesterday will probably be able to come
back and mash it up. Therefore, Forbes considers
that the wounded soldier of the next great cam-
paign will have to shift in a great measure for
himself and take his chance. What that chance
will amount to depends on circumstances. When
Soult left his wounded behind him in some of his
hasty retreats in Spain, they were mostly crucified
feet-upwards on barn doors, and had fires lighted
under their heads, and the world hasn’t improved
so very much since then.

WORDSWORTH UP-TO-DATE.
A goburra in native trim—
The settlers’ gum-tree clock of yore—
Whooping afar his bush-born hymn.
Aloft, in mock-muezzin whim
From leafy minaret or limb,
At daydawn grey, in gloaming dim—
A laughing jackass was to him,
“ And it was nothing more.”
GRIP.
The baby province of Tasmania has also
got a deficit—not much of a deficit in itself
but a stupendous affair for the serious little
island to tackle. Premier Fysh proposes
to meet the difficulty by levying a stamp
duty on receipts, a tax of Id. each on theatre,
concert and circus tickets —pious tea-fights and
the coming Exhibition alone excepted, and a
duty of £5 per head on stallions and .£1 on bulls.
This is almost suggestive of the desperate ex-
pedients once resorted to by the Swedish Govern-
ment in a time of great emergency, when a tax
was levied on the most sacred emotions of the
community, and any one who had such emotions
without having them stamped was liable to be run
in as a smuggler.
Journalist : “ Why are vast changes wel-
tering in the Argus? Because the proprietary is
wearing and bouting ship. There are a lot of men
on hand, vigorously trained for a traditional
Argus, which is shrinking up like water on a
pavement, under a hot sun, while the new Argus
grows up within, to blossom as the aloe. The
proprietary is not behind the time. It under-
stands all the changes that are necessary, in a broad
Americanisation, which obviously creeps on, week
by week. The shorthander sinks to a discount,
and your typical Argus man has no notion beyond
the mechanical.”
When N.S.W. Railway Commissioner
Eddy decried Tasmania’s native timber as used
for railway sleepers, &c., he perhaps spoke without
sufficient data, as pieces of Tas. hardwood have
been sent to Hobart from all parts of the island to
prove that stuff which has lain in the ground for
15, 20, and 30 years has come out almost as sound
as when cut. Tasmanian hardwood taken from
the recently demolished Police Barracks that ad-
joined the old Van Demonian Government House
of Sir John Franklin’s day is now being utilised in
the erection of a dwelling-house, after serving for
60years “under Government.”
Dear Bulletin, —A few words about the
alleged Australian adjective. Why “ Austra-
lian ? ” Is it by adoption or by virtue of new
applications? I don’t know of any prior claim on
either ground. The word is a part of our
linguistic inheritance, and Max O’Rell notes it as
an integral part of the vernacular of Britain. As
an Australian I resent the implication that we
have any monopoly in the lurid expletive. Listen
to any English, Scotch or Irish crowd, or to the
average American letting himself loose on things
in general. Has not the conversation of all the
same gory garnishment ? Here in Maoriland it is
the forceful vocable of all classes. Nor does its
use denote poverty of thought or vocabulary. An
eloquent democratic knight confidentially remarks
that an opponent is “a fool,” and an ex-
parson of proven ability sends the same epithet
vibrating over the wires in Oxford’s cultured
bleat. And though the man who “ humped his
swag from Dim-d d-boola to Warrackna-
b y-beal” was probably an Australian ; he was
not more coarsely fantastic of tongue than Bret
Harte’s Californians or Kipling’s Anglo-Indians,
Finally, “ bloody ” is a better word than “ bally ”
or “blooming.”— Crude. [The Bulletin calls it
the “ Australian adjective ” simply because it is
more used, and used more exclusively by Austra-
lians, than by any other allegedly civilised nation.
Whether the Australian’s expletive is “a better
word ” than somebody else’s expletive has no
bearing on the case. The otherman’s expletive
is his own affair.—Ed. B.]

The Voice of the Soul.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
In Youth, when through our veins runs fast
The bright red stream of life,
The Soul’s Voice is a trumpet-blast
That calls us to the strife.
The Spirit spurns its prison-bars,
And feels with force endued
To scale the ramparts of the stars.
And storm Infinitude.
Youth passes ; like a dungeon grows
The Spirit’s house of clay.
The Voice that once in music rose.
In murmurs dies away.
But in the day when sickness sore
Smites on the body’s walls,
The Soul’s Voice through the breach once more
Like to a trumpet calls.
Well shall it be with him who heeds
The mystic summons, then !
His after-life with loving deeds
Shall blossom amongst men.
We shall have gifts—the gift that feels
The germ within the clod,
And hears the whirring of the wheels
That turn the mills of God !
The gift that sees with glance profound
The secret soul of things.
And in the silence hears the sound
Of vast and viewless wings I
The veil of Isis, seven-fold.
To him as gauze shall be,
Where through, clear-eyed, he shall behold
The Ancient Mystery.
He shall do battle for the True,
Defend till death the Right,
With Shoes of Swiftness Wrong pursue.
With Sword of Sharpness smite.
And, dying, he shall haply hear,
Like golden trumpets blown
For joy, far voices sweet and clear—-
Soul-voices like his own.
So welcomed may he join the Throng
Upon the Shining Shore,
As one who, after wand’ring long,
Returneth home once more !
VICTOR J. DALEY.
One of the features of the recent Chinese
plague was, according to the Hongkong Tele-
gkaph, the cowardliness and ineptitude of the
“ medical missionaries ”to China. Although the
authorities advertised for volunteers, no member
of the cloth came forward to render assistance.
Truth (London) waxes indignant at the
fact that a woman was fined <£l apiece on 02 par-
tridge eggs, found in her possession and supposed
to have been poached. Which recalls that a decent
Scotchman, who was one of Victoria’s richest
squatters, was originally sent out here for ten
years at Government expense for stealing 11
pheasant’s eggs.
The germ of “A Yellow Aster?” From
Mannington Caffyn’s novel, “Miss Milne and I” :
It has been for years a theory of mine that women feel
neither pleasure nor pain as men do, that they have
neither mentally, morally, nor physically the same
capacity of appreciation. Pass a hot plate around the
table, and the men will howl while the women smile.
Again:
There is your modern woman who does feel, and, thanks
to evolution, education, the divine order, or what you
like, is trying to feel keenly, standing on the nebulous
heights of womanhood’s coming heritage—that is, to
include, we are told, a man’s power of grasping facts with
a woman’s intuitive appreciation of them—and in|the
haze of the dawn, blessed with but little of either.
“H. J. Mac.” gives adders and other
snaix a rest and is off on a fresh tack :
Dear Bulletin, —We are retrograding. There is no fun
in us nowadays and no fight. The history of the navvies
camp has yet to be written. In the real old bygone
navvies’ camp there was a deeper and darker hell than
has been found in shearers’. Hell was written all over it
in big, black letters. And yet there was fun in those
camps—wild, unnatural hellish fun. I saw some of this
kind of fun 25 years ago. It was on the Southern line,
and there was a spree and a fight and an inquest, and
then another spree and some more fighting and more
inquests. It all began and ended over “ Wild Fergusson,
a red-headed navvy, 6ft. 4in. in his bare feet. His oaths
were rugged, snappy and terrifying to listen to. Every
navvy on the line owed him a thrashing, for he had
thrashed everyone of them. One pay-night the mob
from Johnson’s Cutting, who were Irish to a man—and
there were 34 of them —made it up to give him the
“ father of a bating,” and “ Wild Fergusson," who was
flaming drunk, staggered right into the midst of them.
For a moment all seemed lost. Hell was let loose. So
was Fergusson—or he got loose ; and seizing a
stout butt of a man named Dwyer by the
collar of his shirt and the seat of his moleskins he
began to use him as a battering-ram, and floor all within
reach, at the same time kicking out backwards with equal
results while he kept up a torrent of blasphemy that
fairly laid the dust. Tiring of this he threw his extempore
battering-ram wildly into the air and caught it by the
legs as it neared earth again, and then with renewed
vigour began swinging it round and knocking down eight
men with every revolution. Then he paused for wind
and while doing so, threw down Dwyer and picked
up Morgan, with whom he knocked down Gallagher
and stunned Ryan. Between the murder-meaning
yells, Morgan flew from his hands at a tangent,
knocking down Rogan, Gilligan, and Hayes, and
meanwhile Ferguson found a substitute in Duggan
whom he was whirling round his head with one hand
and singing “ Highland Mary” [with the other?—Ed. B.j
when daylight broke and the police came. After this
there was temporary peace, and that evening the
coroner’s jury returned a verdict that Michael Dwyer
and Terence Rooney died “through the visitation of
God.” Yes, there’s a bigger hell than Henry Lawson 8
shearing shed, and that’s the navvy's camp. Why, at
Mandurama, only a few years ago, after the pubs, closed
for the night and a beastly drunken mob was staggering
home to camp the “ ganger ” fell into Mandurama OreeK,
and out of over 30 men—Great God 1 this is true-not one
would save him. They all said, “ Let the ——• stop there
and drown, ** And he did I Men ctve in shearing-
sheds—in navvies’ camps, never. The shearing-shed is to
the navvy’s camp what Paradise is to Hell; but I like
neither, and hate both. I once saw a shearer drive his
shears up to the handle in a kicking sheep, and I once
saw a navvy who was refused more beer pick up a beauti-
ful pet butcher-bird and tear its head off with his teeth, 1
This isn’t very nice, but it’s picturesque and
truthful-looking, and the means of relieving the
Mac. from a great pressure of gall.
“ Live-Man Gardiner.”
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
“ Bosh 1” shouted a sudden voice out of
the shadows.
The two traders started. It was hours since
their compotator had fallen ingloriously to ie
first bottle of “De Kuyper,” and they were by
this time well downstairs in the second,
Fleming half-filled a tumbler and took it over
to the hammock. “You’re supposed to ’ve been
killed in the first act, old cock! But, since you y e
come to life again, just get this down your neck,
and explain! What’s bosh ? ’
The gin went down, and Daddy Murchison sat
up. “ Help me out!” he said, and the ginger-
headed giant half-lifted him to the floor. The old
man up-ended again the gin case that had gone
down with him, and sat blinking disreputably at
the lamp for a minute before he said : “ I heard
you chaps blatherskitin’ about cannibalism. No
such thing in Borneo ! —you settled that all right,
didn’t you ?”
Gardiner foresaw contradiction. “No, daddy!
—no! We want your opinion first, you know !”
“Shows your sense, tool Three years in th®
Pel ago, I heard you say you were. And Bui
locky Fleming, here—he thinks he knows a lump
because he’s been about twice as long 1 But I’ve
been 35 years amongst the islands, young man;
and I say there’s cannibalism—plenty of it among
the Dyaks of the south-east! ”
“ Bullocky ” grinned down at him through the
smoke. “ Cock- sure little animal it is !” he said.
“ But the question is—what proof have you th’
ever was any man-eatin’ hereaway ? That’s the
ta The old man got up—wild-eyed with sudden
h °“Proof, man? Why, I’ve .” He broke off,
took a gulp of gin, and sat down again, shaking.
The others—smiling no longer—said not a word.
There was some ugly reality behind this!
Daddv Murchison smote his ancient fast upon
the table. “I’ll tell ’em!” he muttered By
God! I must tell somebody before I die ! tie
forced a mirthless smile, and lit his pipe- Ufa,
you needn’t look like that, Bullocky ! No more
cranky than yourself; though I’ve a lot more
reason to be! '’ . ... ~
Puff, puff for half-a-minute—then, with the
suddenness of a man keeping himself to the
C “ There was a chap used to make the round-trip
with trade—Singapore across Landak, down to
Banjermassin, and away round again, by the east
coast, up to Shanghai, with trepang and birds -
nests and things. ’Bout 30 years ago, it was;
before the Dutchies had squared things up much ;
and, you bet, it was risky work. How Blinker
Johnson—that was the name he went by-
managed to escape the pumpaks, I dunno; unless
’twas because he was such an infernally bad lot,
himself. Runaway mate of an American barque,
he was ; put a bullet through his skipper some-
where up among the Carolines. I was knockin
about Banjermassin, dead - broke, and when
Blinker told me he wanted a hand I thought I
might as well try my luck up in Chow-land.
“Never got there! I wasn’t over-holy.then—
ain’t, now, for that matter!—but such a G-od-
forgotten set of wretches as Blinker’s crew never
came together in one bottom ! Good-sized lump
of a brig, she was —but manned three times over.
Half-a-dozen whites—the rest all sorts and
colours, and every man of ’em a bigger scoundrel
than the next 1 Before we commenced our north-
ing I was full of it. In fact, my life wasn’t worth
a button among ’em, after they guessed what 1
thought of ’em; and, when Blinker plugged a
Sunda-man for droppin’ a marlin’spike from the
main-t’gallan’-yard, I swore to myself that—sink
or swim—l wouldn’t round Cape Kamiungun with
him.
“ A couple of days after that I got a chance;
we anchored in the mouth of a middling big river.
I forget its name, but there was a Dyak place just
above that was a good mark for swallows’ nests—
the kind the Chinkies eat, you know. I got away
all right. Chanced the sharks, I did, and swam
ashore in the darkness. Blinker kicked up a
devil of a splash, but the Dyaks kept me snug,
and he had to make sail without me.
“ Well, the Dyaks round there ain’t a bad sort,
if you don’t rub ’em the wrong way. The Orang-
Kaya took a great fancy to me, and nothing d
do him, after a bit, but I must go with ’em on a
raid against a village about 20 miles up-nyer.
Same old game, it was—came down on ’em just
before day, fired the roofs, and dropped ’em as
they bolted. The poor devils didn’t have a show,
and our fellows got nearly a couple of hundred
heads, besides a big haul of women and kids.
Half-a-dozen young-men prisoners they had, too,
but 1 didn’t know much about Dyaks then, and
I saw nothing strange in that.
“When we got back there was a big spread.
All the usual stinkin’ cookery the Dyaks are so
gone upon, and a lot more that I could see
was special for the occasion. Plenty of tuak, too,
and the women, as usual, eggin’ on the men to
drink, till three parts of ’em tumbled off their
perches. The chief was a hard old case, though,
and, when another gorge came on, some time
towards morning, he tackled it like a wolf.
Peckish, mvself, I was; and sober; for I could n t
stomach thed d rot-gut of tuak then as I can
now. Reg’lar demon to stuff himself, the Orang
was ; and, d’ye know, it gave me a queer kind of
a shiver to see the look of him into a big brass pot
that four young women put down before him.
Some kind of a stew, it smelt like, and a lot of
’em gathered round while the old boy fished out
a piece and scoffed it. Then he looked at me and
said something, and all the others looked, too, and
started laughing.
“ The Orang called me over and asked if I
would like some. ‘ Yes,’ I said, for I understood
some of their lingo; and then they all guffawea
again. I couldn’t make it out; but there
be much harm in the stuff or the old man wouldn’t
have smacked his lips over it. I took the piece lie
offered me —about the size of a mutton-chop, it
was—and, then, seeing how they were all watch-
ing me, I thought I’d ask what it was, at all
events. The old villain showed all his sharkined
teeth. 'Orang!' says he. Well, he was the
Orane, himself —the Orang-Kaya ; so I supposed
it was some special dish of his own, and that ho
wanted to do me honour. But orang-kaya means
‘rich man,’ and, as I said the words over to my
self, it all came clear to me in a flash. ‘ Orang—
man!’ I pitched the horror away, and made for
the door as if Satan was after me!”

For half-a minute the old man sat silent—
recalling the scene ; then he reached out his hand
—steady now—as was the eye beneath the bushy
white brow. Gardiner pushed him the bottle.
Fleming got up and cut himself a pipeful before —
like a man who half-dreaded the answer—he asked
—“So you got away all right, Daddy?”
Daddy slowly re-lighted his own pipe, and
eclipsed himself with vapour.
“No, Fleming, lad—not that turn 1 I was col-
lared at the door and brought back to the chief.
His face was like the devil’s in a picture, with his
eyes like a cornered cobra’s, and his lips curled
clean away from his black, crocodile teeth. He
had his parang in his hand, and he lifted it to
make a slash at me. I thought I was a goner,
and no mistake, but he didn’t strike. He says
something to one of the women, and she fishes out
another chunk of dreadfulness, and holds it out to
me. I looked at the chief, and he jabbered some-
thing I didn’t quite understand. But I under-
stood that he meant business—l had seen that
parang at work before ! ’Twas ’’ —Daddy put
down his pipe and groaned—“’Twas—cannibal—
or—dead-un — that’s what it was !”
Fleming knocked over the bottle as he jumped
to his feet again.
“ Good God! old man, you don’t mean to
say .”
Gardiner sat still. “Of course he does ! It’s
clear it wasn’t dead-un, so it must have been .”
“ Cannibal J” screeched the old man —smashed
his pipe on the table, and rushed out into the
glimmering dawn.
• • •
“ Mad ?” asked Fleming.
Said Gardiner —“Queer, to begin with —and
queerer for the liquor. But that was no madman’s
yarn !’’
The big man shuddered right through his six-
feet-three. “Faugh ! to think we’ve been drinkin’
with a man-eater !”
“Rubbish! Drinking with a man who pre-
ferred his life to his prejudices, that’s all. I don’t
envy him the fix ; but if it had been mine .”
“ You’d have done the same ?”
“Bet your pretty little boots I would !”
“ Bullocky ” doubled his historic fist, “ You’re
a better-educated man than me, Mr. William
■Gardiner —by a jugful! Seen a lot more, too, in
some w'ays. But, by gum ! if I thought you meant
what you said ”
“ You’d get plugged, old man—that’s all ! Lay
a finger on me, Mr. Bullocky Fleming, and I’ll
blow a hole in you ’fore you can take it off again!”
The giant gasped. “ Thought you was a man,
Gardiner! ”
“Man? Exac’ly: Nine-stone man! Very
reason I don’t intend to be knocked about by
eighteen-stone of bull-beef and stupidity ! See ? ”
The Mandhar trader saw. He, too, had a
“gun” at his belt, but he wasn’t in it at that
game with the Californian.
“ Don’t be an ass, Fleming, as well as a bullock!
Man that comes for me gen’lly gets left! Take
another ‘ wheel-greaser ’ and we’ll have a look for
poor old Fly-by-night. If he’s not exac’ly mad,
he’s not overpoweringly sane, either, and I don’t
like the way he skedaddled. Hello—what's this?”
“This” was a scribbled scrap of paper, pre-
sented by a Dyak boy with the awe due to the
mysterious, soundless words of white men.
Dear Gardiner,—Don't bother about me. I’ve felt for
a long time as if I must let out this horror. Don’t think
I’m mad ! The thing’s true enough ; so true that, now its
out, I'm going to do what I should have done long ago.”
“ Make away with himself, eh ?” Fleming said,
when Gardiner handed him the scrawl; “ Beat
thing he can do, too !”
“ Bigger fool than I took him for ! Come on,
and we’ll have a look for him ! No use asking
young yellowskin anything; primed with his
answer I”
• • #
The sun had hardly hove his deep-red disc
clear of the cold blue jungle-line beyond the
woolly-misted river—yet all the village was astir
with men that ran to swell a throng about the
Orang’s house —though few had ventured to join
the little group upon the platform.
The crowd fell back, right and left, from the
ladder-foot, as the white men came. Gardiner
wondered at the shame-faced greeting of the few
whose eyes met his. “What is it, brothers ?” he
called out, in Dyak, but only the Orang answered,
from above.
“ Come up, white brother ; he is here!”
“Corpse, I suppose!” and Gardiner scrambled
up.
Murchison wasn’t quite a corpse. His eyes had
recognition in them, and he faintly moved a hand.
Gardiner stooped—lifted the sarong that lay over
the old man’s chest —dropped it suddenly, and
faced fiercely round upon the Orang.
“ Who?” he said.
The Orang pointed down to the dying man.
“ Hear, first, his words. The time is very short.”
The bloodless lips were moving feebly. Gardiner
lay down upon the bamboos to bring his ear to the
old man’s mouth.
“It was Eki, the smith ; but I forced him to do
it. Spat on him, I did, and then he wouldn’t; so
I struck him in the face, and then ”
“ He gave you a slash that would have killed a
buffalo ! S’pose you know it’s a finisher ? ”
“ Yes ! They’ve stopped the Weedin’ a bit, but
I know it’s a case with me—as I meant it to be !
New thing in sui ’
Death took the last syllable.
• # •
For the killing of Roderick Murchison,
Eki, the smith, was arraigned before a court that
combined less “ frill ” with more expedition than
any other upon record.
Gardiner at top of the table in his store—
Fleming on his right hand ; on his left, Musman,
the Orang-Kaya. At bottom, the accused, dis-
armed and bound ; behind him a crowd of gaping
Dyaks and a Malay or two from a prahu in the
river.
Facts clear enough. Eki was rolling a cigar;
white man walked up and “ made spittle ” in his
face. Eki dropped cigar and took hold of parang.
Then white man struck, and Eki cut him down."
Mr. Justice Fleming would acquit the prisoner.
“It was a devil of a thing to be ‘landed ’ on the
nose without warning!”
Mr. Justioe Musman concurred. It was evident
the white man wanted to be killed. “The
prisoner had merely obliged him !”
Chief-Justice Gardiner opined that this was all
d d rubbish 1 A Dyak had killed a white
man ! There was severe provocation; but this
Court wasn’t going to establish the precedent that
a Dyak might kill a white man upon any provoca-
tion ! He found prisoner guilty, sentenced him
to be shot, and then and there—before anybody
could lift a finger— did shoot him !
The petrifaction of amazement rested for a
moment on the assemblage—then the Orang
headed a stampede for the door, and in half-a-
minute only Gardiner and Fleming were left with
the corpse. “ Bullocky,” too, got up to go, but at
the door he turned.
“Gardiner,” he said—“you can shoot me, if
you like ! —but I say you’re not a man I—you’re a
devill”
Ihe little man laughed, “Don’t want to shoot
you, Bullocky !—not worth it! Get away home
to Maudhar, and peddle cocoa-nuts —it’s about all
you re fit for ! As for me, I’m no devil—l'm only
live-man Gardiner!”
ALEX. MONTGOMERY.
The Beantree and the Gum.
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
Thus spake the beantree to the stately gum ;
lo west, yon cloud forebodes the storm wilj
come—
“ Already as it looms against the day
Within its leaden folds the lightnings play.
“ My roots are thirsting in the barren soil,
And in my parched veins the warm saps boil.
“Yet, though my languid leaves are faint for
wind,
I know the gods above are good and kind.”
“The gods care nought,” the grim, great gumtree
said,
“Else had thy sisters lived, who stand there dead.
“ ’Twas not for mercy’s sake that thou wast spared,
They lingered, perishing, and no one oared.”
Through all the moaning wood the tempest swept,
And for her sisters dead the beantree wept.
And, as the thunder rumbled overhead,
The beantree, sobbing, to the gumtree said ;
“The ways they work in how can worldlings
know?
Or who can say that ’twere not better so ?”
“Thy sullen heart is written in thy face,
But in my prayers always thou hast a place.
“ They bend the haughty, but they heed the prayer
Of humble hearts, take heed, they do not spare !”
And then the storm broke o’er them as they
stood—
“I only know,” the beantree said, “ the gods are
good.”
Flung down between them fell an angry shaft—
A grim, sardonic laugh, the gumtree laughed.
For all her faith the beantree bowed her hair,
And, trembling, called upon her gods to spare.
Against the day, clear-cut, the gumtree stood
With brooding brows above the rustling wood.
And in the fragrance of the freshened morn
The stricken beantree lay, with roots uptorn.

Western Courier (Valparaiso) says that

the port of Valparaiso
His Star of Bethlehem.
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
He had had several drinks, and he left the
shanty at midnight for his hut, with a candle in
his hand. That candle was his ‘‘Star of Beth-
lehem. ”
The path curled round a steep bluff of sandstone
reef. I orty feet below it was the creek-bed—a
tailings-deposit.
The candle went out. The pick-handle revolved,
i-i 6 ( an d beef tied round his neck
like the books of a school-boy, swayed to the left'
The man followed the bag. He tilted. He
crashed through the fragile scrub : he disappeared.
• • •
The glory of a drunken man is, that when
he falls, he falls limp. He is like a baggy pants.
jc our stiff-set individual presents a resistance that
destroys him.
In the bed of that creek, when the sun rose next
morning, was a flattened bag of bread and beef.
An ox-tongue protruded from a gap in the bag.
These instances of tongue-protrusion are charac-
teristically diversified in our own species. The
man had again disappeared.
• * 9
At 4 a.m. Simpson groped his way into
the hut and struck the bed. His mate woke
grumbled, and slept afresh. Morning brought a
revelation. Simpson couldn’t move. He was
caught between shoulders. There was an ugly
scar on his cheek, and both arms looked as if
pared down with a spokeshave.
They decided to take a holiday. Tea and
sausages for breakfast—Simpson stayed in bed.
At 11 o’clock, Jones (a neighbour) put in his
head saying, “ It’s a good job you fellows
stayed at home to-day—a slip of a thousand tons
buried your claim an hour ago. Didn’t you hear
the row ?”
DE BAEKER.
Ziba: “I sketched one of our bush-
hawkers the other day. A more than tolerably
honest fellow, supporting a wife and family, as he
goes round with his horse and shop on wheels,
tie is the kind of man throttled by the Hindoos
and Chinamen, people with no families, and
backed up by capitalists, who can feed their hands
on the next best thing to grass—rice.”
Justice Chubb (Townsville) lately com-
mitted to prison one Hopkins for contempt of
court. Hopkins failed to produce certain papers
in the estate of a deceased person whose widow he
had married —hence the contempt. In gaol Hop-
kius was pronounced insaneand sent to Woogaroo;
in Woogaroo he was pronounced sane and sent
home. Naturally, Hopkins is somewhat wroth,
for he has been pronounced, on good authority, to
be unfit for liberty ; unfit for gaol—and, finally,
unfit for Woogaroo.

Bulletin Series, No. 3.
“The Last of Six”:
Tales of the Austral Tropics.
By ERNEST FA¥ENC.
With Prefatory Note hy Rolf Roldreivood.
_K,C O J a Uli U i, 1 3" L 'J
Opinions of tlie Press :
“ The vohime takes its name from one of the sketches, ‘ The Last of Six,’ though in point of merit they nil stand pretty well on a par. The
author, who is familiar with life in Northern Queensland, writes with vigour about the doings of the- whiles and the natives in these faraway
parts. ’ A strong vein of mysticism runs through his narratives, and there are ghosts in almost every chapter, and for those who are fond of
realistic work the volume will prove attractive reading.”—3nVl. Herald, July 8, 1803.
■ “No writer has given the world a more vivid description of Australia and its wilderness than Ernest Favenc, and the secret of this is that
he has, by many years’ experience of travel and bush life, acquired a knowledge of this continent which is possessed by scarcely any other writer.
He. too, possesses great descriptive powers. Whatever he puts pen to is certain to be good. In the stories under notice he has given us some of
his best'work. That they will be widely rend is certain,”— Sidney Mail, July 8, 1893.
*• They present with force and vividness, as well as with the accuracy arising from long contact and keen observation, striking pictures of
parts of Australia unfamiliar to most of its inhabitants ; they tell, sometimes with remarkable power, of incidents such as happen in wild places
when human hearts arc stirred by strong passions, and of weird imaginings that move the minds of lonely men. ‘ The Last of Six ’is a terrible
tale of cannibalism, treachery, and revenge upon the dead, which in other hands might be gross and hideous, but which Mr. Favenc makes of
thrilling interest. ‘The Haunt of the Jinkarras’ suggests Rider Haggard and his imitators to some extent, but is itself relieved from the
suspicion of imitation by faithful local colour and a grotesquerie of conception, in which Mr. Favenc here and there approaches Poe. This
characteristic is especia ly noticeable in ‘Spirit Led,' which the great American might have written. ‘ A Cup of Cold Water’ is. perhaps, the
best tale in a collection that should he widely read, and which forms a worthy addition to .purely Australian literature. Daily Telegraph,
July 15,1893.
‘“The Last of Six and other stories. By Ernest Favenc. (Sydney: The Bulletin Newspaper Co) This is a collection of short but
graphic sketches by Mr. Favenc, the well-known explorer, who has a rare collection of Australian legendary lore. All the stories here are good,
and we can heartily endorse the recommendatory notice by Rolf Boldrewood prefixed to this collection. The present issue forms the third oFThe
Buii ktin series of Australian reminiscences.”— Australian Star, July 15,1893.

“ Every man can write at least one
book,” every man with brains has at
least one good story to tell; every man
with or without brains moves in a differ-
ent circle and knows things unknown to
any other man. Write carefully and
plainly on one side of the paper only,
obliterating every unnecessary word :
then mail your work to The Bulletin,
which pays for accepted matter. 4n
certain of the so-called prize-competi-
tions the great prizes fall to the news-
paper-proprietor, who gets the guileless
public to work for him for* nothing.
Short stories, or bal%ds, especially on
bush, mining, sporting, social, or dra-
matic themes, are • preferred by The
Bulletin ; 1360 words go to a column.
If you can possibly keep your story with-
in a column all the better. Don’t write
a column on any subject if a half-column
will do ; don't write half-a-column where
a mere paragraph is enough. “ Boil it
down.”
